Istihsan Exceptions to Liability for Necessity
Istihsan rulings that no liability for spending from another's property in emergencies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general rule of liability for unauthorized spending and identify the istihsan exceptions when the spender acts for reform, distinguishing between religious and legal liability.

Core Idea
Istihsan exempts from religious liability those who spend another's property in necessity with intent to reform, though legal liability remains.

The general rule in Hanafi fiqh is that spending another's property without permission creates liability (daman). However, the school recognizes exceptions based on istihsan (juristic preference) when the spender acts out of genuine need and with a reformative intent. This lesson examines several classic cases from al-Dhakhira where istihsan removes religious liability, while legal liability remains intact. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping how Hanafi legal reasoning balances strict analogy with equitable outcomes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The general rule (qiyas) is that anyone who spends another's property without permission is liable (daman) both legally and religiously. This is the zahir al-riwayah position.
Liability is the default, but istihsan can override it in specific circumstances.
2
List the exceptions
The passage provides four examples: (1) a traveler spends on an unconscious companion, (2) a person prepares the burial of a deceased from his own wealth, (3) a slave permitted to trade spends after his master's death, (4) a resident maintains a mosque's endowments when no custodian exists. In each, istihsan removes religious liability.
All involve necessity and inability to obtain permission.
3
Locate the dalil
Imam Muhammad cited Quran 2:220: 'And Allah knows the corrupter from the reformer' (والله يعلم المفسد من المصلح) as the proof. This verse supports that Allah distinguishes intent, so if the spender intended reform, there is no sin.
The verse is qati al-thubut but zanni al-dalala, hence it can be the basis for istihsan.
4
Trace the istihsan reasoning
The strict qiyas would impose liability in all cases. Istihsan overrides this because the spender acts for reform (islah) and there is no way to obtain permission. The Quranic verse justifies treating such cases differently.
Istihsan is not arbitrary; it is grounded in textual evidence and the principle of removing hardship.
5
Distinguish religious vs legal liability
The passage concludes: 'لا ضمان عليه فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى استحساناً، أما في الحكم فهو ضامن' means no liability before Allah by istihsan, but legally (in court) he is liable. This dual ruling is central to Hanafi methodology.
The spender should not be punished in the afterlife for a good deed, but property rights are still protected in this world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال في الذخيرة: وكذلك قال مشايخنا في رجلين كانا في سفر فأغمي على أحدهما فأنفق الآخر على المغمى عليه من المغمى عليه لم يضمن استحساناً، وكذا إذا مات فجهزه صاحبه من ماله لم يضمن استحساناً، وكذا العبد المأذون في التجارة إذا مات مولاه فأنفق في الطريق لم يضمن، وكذا روي عن مشايخ بلخ إذا كان للمسجد أوقاف ولم يكن لها متول فقام واحد من أهل المحلة في جميع الأوقاف وأنفق على المسجد فيما يحتاج إليه من الحصر والحشيش لا يضمن استحساناً فيما بينه الله تعالى. وحكي عن محمد أنه مات واحد من تلامذته فباغ محمد كتبه وأنفق في تجهيزه وبين مال هناك يقضي وصي فقيل له إنه لم يوص بذلك إلى أحد فتلا محمد قوله تعالى : والله يعلم المفسد من المصلح) [البقرة: ٢٢٠] فما كان على قياس هذا الأصل لا ضمان عليه فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى استحساناً، أما في الحكم فهو ضامن.
He said in al-Dhakhira: Likewise our scholars said concerning two men who were on a journey and one fell unconscious, so the other spent on the unconscious one from the unconscious one's wealth – he is not liable by istihsan. Similarly, if he died and his companion prepared his burial from his wealth – he is not liable by istihsan. Similarly, the slave permitted to trade if his master dies and he spends on the road – he is not liable. Similarly, it is narrated from the scholars of Balkh that if a mosque has endowments (awqaf) and no custodian, then one of the local residents took charge of all the endowments and spent on the mosque for its needs like mats and grass – he is not liable by istihsan as far as what is between him and Allah. And it is narrated from Muhammad that one of his students died, so Muhammad sold his books and spent on his burial, and there was wealth [from the student] from which the executor was to pay, and it was said to him that he had not bequeathed that to anyone, so Muhammad recited the verse of Allah 'And Allah knows the corrupter from the reformer' (2:220). So whatever is on the pattern of this principle, there is no liability on him as far as what is between him and Allah by istihsan, but in legal judgment he is liable.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage provides several concrete examples from the Hanafi school where the principle of istihsan overrides the general rule of liability for spending another's property without permission. The general rule is that unauthorized spending makes one liable (daman). However, in cases of genuine necessity or where the spender acts clearly for reform (اصلاح), istihsan removes liability in the religious sense (between him and Allah), though the legal ruling may still hold him liable in court. Examples include: a traveler spending on an unconscious companion, preparing a deceased person's burial from his own wealth, a slave spending after his master's death in transit, and a community member maintaining a mosque's endowments when no trustee exists. Notably, Imam Muhammad himself sold a deceased student's books to pay for his burial, citing the Quranic verse distinguishing reformers from corrupters. This illustrates the Hanafi methodology of using istihsan to achieve equitable outcomes while maintaining the formal legal structure.

Key Terms
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): A juristic preference that overrides a strict analogy (qiyas) to achieve a more equitable result.
المصلح (al-muslih): One who seeks reform (islah), as opposed to one who causes corruption (mufsid).
الضمان (al-daman): Liability or guarantee to compensate for loss or damage to another's property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the legal ruling (al-hukm) for a person who spends another's money to bury the owner in an emergency, according to the Hanafi school?
A) He is liable both legally and religiously.
B) He is not liable legally but is liable religiously.
C) He is liable legally but not liable religiously (by istihsan).
D) He is not liable at all.

Key Takeaway
Istihsan removes religious liability for unauthorized spending in necessity when the spender intends reform, but legal liability remains to protect property rights.

The Depositary's Liability and Recourse
Clarification of the depositary's right of recourse after liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on whether a liable depositary can recover money spent without authorization, and explain the effect of subsequent ratification.

Core Idea
A liable depositary owns the money and cannot recover it; later ratification cancels liability.

The passage addresses a gap left by the author: if a depositary spends on the owner's dependents without permission and becomes liable, can he reclaim that money from them? The Hanafi scholars answer decisively: no, because liability transfers ownership of the amount paid, making his payment a donation. However, if the owner later ratifies the spending, the liability is removed entirely. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the unaddressed issue
The author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq did not mention whether the depositary can recover the money he spent after becoming liable. This is the gap the passage fills.
The text begins with 'ولم يذكر المصنف' – 'And the author did not mention.'
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The Hanafi scholars (قالوا) rule: 'لا رجوع له' – he has no recourse. This is the mufta bihi position.
The ruling is absolute: the depositary cannot recover from the recipients.
3
Explain the legal cause (illa)
Because the depositary owns the amount paid by virtue of liability (بالضمان). Paying his own property makes him a donor (متبرع). Hence no right of recourse.
The text says: 'لأن المودع ملك المدفوع بالضمان فكان متبرعاً بملك نفسه'.
4
Distinguish the case of ratification
If the owner later ratifies (أجاز) the spending, the liability is lifted entirely. Ratification acts as an exoneration (إبراء) and is treated like prior agency.
The principle: الإجازة اللاحقة كالوكالة السابقة (later ratification equals prior agency).
5
Apply to analogous cases
The same rule applies whether the depositary spends on the dependents or directly hands over the deposit to them – both create liability and preclude recourse.
The effective cause (علة) – unauthorised disposition – is present in both.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم يذكر المصنف أنه هل يرجع بما أنفقه على من أنفق عليه عند ضمانه؟ وقالوا لا رجوع له لأن المودع ملك المدفوع بالضمان فكان متبرعاً بملك نفسه، وظاهره أنه لا فرق بين أن ينفق عليهم وبين أن يدفع الوديعة إليهم في وجوب الضمان وعدم الرجوع عليهم لوجود العلة فيهما، ولم أر أنه إذا أنفق عليهم بلا أمر ثم أجاز المالك لظهور أنه لا ضمان لأن الإجازة إبراء له من الضمان ولقولهم إن الإجازة اللاحقة كالوكالة السابقة .
And the author did not mention whether he can recover what he spent from the one on whom he spent when he is liable. They said: He has no recourse because the depositary owned the amount paid due to liability, so he is a donor of his own property. Its apparent meaning is that there is no difference between spending on them and paying the deposit to them in the necessity of liability and no recourse against them due to the presence of the effective cause in both. And I have not seen that if he spent without order and then the owner ratified it, that there is no liability because ratification is a discharge from liability, and because they say that later ratification is like prior agency.
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage addresses an issue the author (مصنف) did not explicitly cover: whether the person who spent (the depositary) can reclaim the money from the recipients after he becomes liable. The Hanafi scholars (قَالُوا) rule that he has no right of recourse (لا رجوع له). The reason is that when he becomes liable (ضمان), he effectively owns the amount he paid in compensation. By paying it, he is considered a donor (متبرع) who gave his own property. The effective cause (علة) is that he acted without permission, so he must bear the loss. The text also considers the case of subsequent ratification (إجازة) by the owner. If the owner later ratifies the spending, then there is no liability at all (لا ضمان) because ratification is an exoneration (إبراء) and is treated like prior agency. This follows the Hanafi principle that later ratification is equivalent to prior authorization.

Key Terms
الرجوع (al-ruju'): Recourse; the right to seek repayment from the recipients.
الإجازة (al-ijazah): Later ratification or approval by the owner of the property that validates the otherwise unauthorized act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a depositary spends on the owner's parents without order and becomes liable, can he later recover the money from the parents?
A) Yes, because the parents should return what they received.
B) No, because he owns the money by liability and is considered a donor.
C) Yes, but only if the parents are rich.
D) No, but only if he obtained the judge's permission.

Key Takeaway
A depositary who becomes liable for unauthorized spending owns the amount and cannot recover it; ratification nullifies liability and precludes the issue.

Manumission (al-'Itq): Definition, Types, and Conditions
Definitional and classificatory opening of the Book of Manumission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define manumission linguistically and legally, state the three conditions for its validity, and classify its four types according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Manumission is the removal of ownership, not the granting of strength – its validity requires a free, legally responsible owner.

In this opening passage of the Book of Manumission, the author sets the foundation by defining what it means to free a slave. You'll learn why the linguistic root matters for the legal definition, what conditions make an act of manumission valid, and how the ruling varies from obligatory to prohibited. This passage clarifies a common misconception about the term 'strength' and establishes the core principles of this important chapter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic and legal definitions
Read the opening lines where the author defines 'itq. Note that linguistically it means removal from ownership, not strength. Legally, it is the establishment of legal strength (al-quwwa al-shar'iyya) i.e., granting full legal capacity.
The author refutes the claim that 'itq means strength, citing lexicographical usage.
2
Analyze the conditions of validity
Locate the statement 'yasihhu min hurr mukallaf li-mamlookihi' – the manumitter must be free, legally responsible (mukallaf: adult and sane), and the owner of the slave.
The commentary elaborates that a child's manumission is invalid even if sane.
3
Classify the four types of manumission
Find the paragraph listing the types: wajib (in expiations), mandub (voluntary for Allah), mubah (without intention), and mahzur (for Satan). Distinguish between obligatory by specification vs. by choice.
The hadith about freeing a limb for a limb supports the mandub category.
4
Recognize the pillar and causes
Identify the rukn as the utterance indicating manumission, and the sabab muthbit (establishing cause) such as claiming lineage, owning a relative, etc., and the sabab ba'ith (motivating cause).
The causes are listed with examples; note the special case of dar al-harb according to Abu Hanifa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْحَمْدُ لِلَّهِ رَبِّ الْعَالَمِينَ. كِتَابُ الْعِتْقِ. هُوَ إِثْبَاتُ الْقُوَّةِ الشَّرْعِيَّةِ فِي الْمَمْلُوكِ، وَيَصِحُّ مِنْ حُرٍّ مُكَلَّفٍ لِمَمْلُوكِهِ بِأَنْتَ حُرٌّ أَوْ بِمَا يُعَبَّرُ بِهِ عَنِ الْبَدَنِ، وَعَتِيقٌ وَمُعْتَقٌ وَمُحَرَّرٌ وَحَرَّرْتُكَ وَأَعْتَقْتُكَ نَوَى أَوْ لَا. ذَكَرَهُ عَقِيبَ الطَّلَاقِ لِأَنَّ كُلًّا مِنْهُمَا إِسْقَاطُ الْحَقِّ. وَقَدَّمَ الطَّلَاقَ لِمُنَاسَبَةِ النِّكَاحِ. ثُمَّ الْإِسْقَاطَاتُ أَنْوَاعٌ تَخْتَلِفُ أَسْمَاؤُهَا بِاخْتِلَافِ أَنْوَاعِهَا، فَإِسْقَاطُ الْحَقِّ عَنِ الرِّقِّ عِتْقٌ، وَإِسْقَاطُ الْحَقِّ عَنِ الْبُضْعِ طَلَاقٌ، وَإِسْقَاطُ مَا فِي الذِّمَّةِ بَرَاءَةٌ، وَإِسْقَاطُ الْحَقِّ عَنِ الْقِصَاصِ وَالْجِرَاحَاتِ عَفْوٌ. وَالْإِعْتَاقُ فِي اللُّغَةِ الْإِخْرَاجُ عَنِ الْمِلْكِ، يُقَالُ أَعْتَقَهُ فَعَتَقَ، وَالْعِتْقُ الْخُرُوجُ عَنِ الْمِلْكِ، مِنْ بَابِ فَعَلَ بِالْفَتْحِ يَفْعِلُ بِالْكَسْرِ، عَتَقَ الْعَبْدُ عِتَاقًا إِذَا خَرَجَ عَنِ الْمِلْكِ، وَعَتَقَتِ الْفَرَسُ إِذَا سَبَقَتْ وَنَجَتْ، وَعَتَقَ فَرْخُ الْقَطَاةِ إِذَا طَارَ. وَيُقَالُ عَتَقَ فُلَانٌ بَعْدَ اسْتِعْلَاجٍ إِذَا رَقَّتْ بِشَرَتُهُ بَعْدَ غِلَظٍ، وَمَصْدَرُهُ الْعِتْقُ وَالْعِتَاقُ، وَلَيْسَ مِنْهُ الْعَتَاقَةُ بِمَعْنَى الْقِدَمِ لِأَنَّ فِعْلَهُ فَعَلَ بِالْفَتْحِ يَفْعُلُ بِالضَّمِّ، وَلَيْسَ مِنْهُ الْعِتْقُ بِمَعْنَى الْجَمَالِ لِأَنَّهُ مِنْ هَذَا الْبَابِ أَيْضًا وَهُوَ مَضْمُومُ الْعَيْنِ أَيْضًا؛ كَذَا فِي ضِيَاءِ الْحُلُومِ. فَالْعِتْقُ اللُّغَوِيُّ حِينَئِذٍ هُوَ الْعِتْقُ الشَّرْعِيُّ، وَهُوَ الْخُرُوجُ عَنِ الْمَمْلُوكِيَّةِ، وَهُوَ أَوْلَى مِنْ قَوْلِهِمْ إِنَّ الْعِتْقَ فِي اللُّغَةِ الْقُوَّةُ. وَفِي الشَّرْعِ الْقُوَّةُ الشَّرْعِيَّةُ لِأَنَّ أَهْلَ اللُّغَةِ لَمْ يَقُولُوا اعْتَقَّ الْعَبْدُ إِذَا قَوِيَ وَإِنَّمَا قَالُوا أَعْتَقَ الْعَبْدُ إِذَا خَرَجَ عَنِ الْمَمْلُوكِيَّةِ. وَإِنَّمَا ذُكِرَتِ الْقُوَّةُ فِي عِتْقِ الطَّيْرِ وَنَحْوِهِ. وَرُكْنُهُ فِي الْإِعْتَاقِ اللَّفْظِيِّ الْإِنْشَائِيِّ اللَّفْظُ الدَّالُّ عَلَيْهِ. وَفِي الْبَدَائِعِ رُكْنُهُ اللَّفْظُ الَّذِي جُعِلَ دَلَالَةً عَلَى الْعِتْقِ فِي الْجُمْلَةِ أَوْ مَا يَقُومُ مَقَامَ اللَّفْظِ اهـ. وَيُعْرَفُ ذَلِكَ بِبَيَانِ سَبَبِهِ. قَالُوا سَبَبُهُ الْمُثْبِتُ لَهُ قَدْ يَكُونُ دَعْوَى النَّسَبِ، وَقَدْ يَكُونُ نَفْسُ الْمِلْكِ فِي الْقَرِيبِ، وَقَدْ يَكُونُ الْإِقْرَارُ بِحُرِّيَّةِ عَبْدِ إِنْسَانٍ حَتَّى لَوْ مَلَكَهُ عَتَقَ، وَقَدْ يَكُونُ بِالدُّخُولِ فِي دَارِ الْحَرْبِ فَإِنَّ الْحَرْبِيَّ إِذَا اشْتَرَى عَبْدًا مُسْلِمًا فَدَخَلَ بِهِ إِلَى دَارِ الْحَرْبِ وَلَمْ يَشْعُرْ عَتَقَ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ، وَكَذَا زَوَالُ يَدِهِ عَنْهُ بِأَنْ هَرَبَ عَنْ مَوْلَاهُ الْحَرْبِيِّ إِلَى دَارِ الْإِسْلَامِ، وَقَدْ يَكُونُ اللَّفْظُ الْمَذْكُورُ. وَأَمَّا سَبَبُهُ الْبَاعِثُ فَفِي الْوَاجِبِ تَفْرِيغُ ذِمَّتِهِ، وَفِي غَيْرِهِ قَصْدُ التَّقَرُّبِ إِلَى اللَّهِ تَعَالَى. وَأَنْوَاعُهُ أَرْبَعَةٌ: وَاجِبٌ وَمَنْدُوبٌ وَمُبَاحٌ وَمَحْظُورٌ. فَالْوَاجِبُ الْإِعْتَاقُ فِي كَفَّارَةِ الْقَتْلِ وَالظِّهَارِ وَالْيَمِينِ وَالْإِفْطَارِ، إِلَّا أَنَّهُ فِي بَابِ الْقَتْلِ وَالظِّهَارِ وَالْإِفْطَارِ وَاجِبٌ عَلَى التَّعْيِينِ عِنْدَ الْقُدْرَةِ عَلَيْهِ، وَفِي بَابِ الْيَمِينِ وَاجِبٌ عَلَى التَّخْيِيرِ. وَالْمَنْدُوبُ الْإِعْتَاقُ لِوَجْهِ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى مِنْ غَيْرِ إِيجَابٍ لِأَنَّ الشَّرْعَ نَدَبَ إِلَى ذَلِكَ لِلْحَدِيثِ: "أَيُّمَا مُؤْمِنٍ أَعْتَقَ مُؤْمِنًا فِي الدُّنْيَا أَعْتَقَ اللَّهُ بِكُلِّ عَضْوٍ مِنْهُ عُضْوًا مِنَ النَّارِ" (رَوَاهُ الْبُخَارِيُّ وَمُسْلِمٌ). وَلِهَذَا اسْتَحَبُّوا أَنْ يَعْتِقَ الرَّجُلُ الْعَبْدَ وَالْمَرْأَةُ الْأَمَةَ لِيَتَحَقَّقَ مُقَابَلَةُ الْأَعْضَاءِ بِالْأَعْضَاءِ، لَكِنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِعِبَادَةٍ حَتَّى يَصِحَّ مِنَ الْكَافِرِ. وَأَمَّا الْمُبَاحُ فَهُوَ الْإِعْتَاقُ مِنْ غَيْرِ نِيَّةٍ. وَأَمَّا الْمَحْظُورُ فَهُوَ الْإِعْتَاقُ لِوَجْهِ الشَّيْطَانِ. وَسَيَأْتِي بَيَانُ شَرَائِطِهِ وَحُكْمِهِ زَوَالُ الْمِلْكِ أَوْ ثُبُوتُ الْعِتْقِ عَلَى الِاخْتِلَافِ.

ثُمَّ قَالَ الْمُصَنِّفُ: (هُوَ إِثْبَاتُ الْقُوَّةِ الشَّرْعِيَّةِ لِلْمَمْلُوكِ) أَيِ الْإِعْتَاقُ شَرْعًا. وَالْقُوَّةُ الشَّرْعِيَّةُ هِيَ قُدْرَتُهُ عَلَى التَّصَرُّفَاتِ الشَّرْعِيَّةِ وَأَهْلِيَّتُهُ لِلْوِلَايَاتِ وَالشَّهَادَاتِ وَدَفْعُ تَصَرُّفِ الْغَيْرِ عَلَيْهِ. وَحَاصِلُهُ أَنَّهُ إِزَالَةُ الضَّعْفِ الْحُكْمِيِّ الَّذِي هُوَ الرِّقُّ الَّذِي هُوَ أَثَرُ الْكُفْرِ. وَفِي الْمُحِيطِ: وَيُسْتَحَبُّ لِلْعَبْدِ أَنْ يَكْتُبَ لِلْعِتْقِ كِتَابًا وَيُشْهِدَ عَلَيْهِ شُهُودًا تَوْثِيقًا وَصِيَانَةً عَنِ التَّجَاحُدِ وَالتَّنَازُعِ فِيهِ كَمَا فِي الْمُدَايَنَةِ، بِخِلَافِ سَائِرِ التِّجَارَاتِ لِأَنَّهَا مَا يَكْثُرُ وُقُوعُهَا فَالْكِتَابَةُ فِيهَا تُؤَدِّي إِلَى الْحَرَجِ وَلَا كَذَلِكَ الْعِتْقُ.

ثُمَّ قَالَ الْمُصَنِّفُ: (وَيَصِحُّ مِنْ حُرٍّ مُكَلَّفٍ لِمَمْلُوكِهِ بِأَنْتَ حُرٌّ أَوْ بِمَا يُعَبَّرُ بِهِ عَنِ الْبَدَنِ وَعَتِيقٌ وَمُعْتَقٌ وَمُحَرَّرٌ وَحَرَّرْتُكَ وَأَعْتَقْتُكَ نَوَى أَوْ لَا) بَيَانٌ لِشَرَائِطِهِ وَصَرِيحِهِ وَحُكْمِ الصَّرِيحِ. أَمَّا شَرَائِطُهُ فَذَكَرَ الْمُصَنِّفُ أَنَّهَا ثَلَاثَةٌ. الْأَوَّلُ الْحُرِّيَّةُ: لَا حَاجَةَ إِلَيْهِ مَعَ ذِكْرِ الْمِلْكِ لِأَنَّ الْحُرِّيَّةَ لِلِاحْتِرَازِ عَنْ إِعْتَاقِ غَيْرِ الْحُرِّ وَهُوَ لَيْسَ بِمَالِكٍ كَمَا سَنُبَيِّنُهُ. وَاحْتَرَزَ بِالْمُكَلَّفِ عَنْ عِتْقِ الصَّبِيِّ فَإِنَّهُ لَا يَصِحُّ وَإِنْ كَانَ عَاقِلًا كَمَا لَا يَصِحُّ طَلَاقُهُ، وَعَنْ عِتْقِ الْمَجْنُونِ فَإِنَّهُ لَا يَصِحُّ. وَأَمَّا الَّذِي يَجِنُّ وَيُفِيقُ فَهُوَ فِي حَالَةِ إِفَاقَتِهِ عَاقِلٌ وَفِي حَالَةِ جُنُونِهِ مَجْنُونٌ. وَخَرَجَ الْمَعْتُوهُ أَيْضًا وَالْمَدْهُوشُ وَالْمُبَرْسَمُ وَالْمُغْمَى عَلَيْهِ وَالنَّائِمُ. فَلَا يَصِحُّ إِعْتَاقُهُمْ كَمَا لَا يَصِحُّ طَلَاقُهُمْ. وَلَوْ قَالَ: أَعْتَقْتُ وَأَنَا صَبِيٌّ أَوْ وَأَنَا نَائِمٌ كَانَ الْقَوْلُ قَوْلَهُ، وَكَذَا لَوْ قَالَ: أَعْتَقْتُهُ وَأَنَا مَجْنُونٌ بِشَرْطِ أَنْ يُعْلَمَ جُنُونُهُ، أَوْ قَالَ وَأَنَا حَرْبِيٌّ فِي دَارِ الْحَرْبِ وَقَدْ عُلِمَ ذَلِكَ، لِأَنَّهُ لَمَّا أَضَافَهُ إِلَى زَمَانٍ لَا يَتَصَوَّرُ مِنْهُ الْإِعْتَاقُ عَلِمَ أَنَّهُ أَرَادَ صِيغَةَ الْإِعْتَاقِ لَا حَقِيقَتَهُ فَلَمْ يَصِرْ مُعْتَرِفًا بِالْإِعْتَاقِ كَمَا لَوْ قَالَ: أَعْتَقْتُهُ وَأَنَا صَبِيٌّ.

(رَوَاهُ الْبُخَارِيُّ فِي كِتَابِ الْعِتْقِ بَابُ 1، كِتَابِ الْكَفَّارَاتِ بَابُ 6، أَبُو دَاوُدَ فِي كِتَابِ الْعِتَاقِ بَابُ 13، مُسْلِمٌ فِي كِتَابِ الْعِتْقِ حَدِيثُ 23-24، التِّرْمِذِيُّ فِي كِتَابِ النُّذُورِ بَابُ 14، 20، أَحْمَدُ فِي مُسْنَدِهِ 2/447، 3/490-491، 4/107-113).
Praise be to Allah, Lord of the worlds. The Book of Manumission. It (manumission) is the establishment of legal strength for the owned slave, and it is valid from a free, legally responsible person for his own slave by saying 'you are free' or by what expresses the body, and 'free', 'freed', 'liberated', 'I have liberated you', 'I have freed you', whether he intended or not. He (the author) mentioned it after divorce because each of them is a relinquishment of a right. He placed divorce first due to its connection to marriage. Then the types of relinquishment differ in names according to their types: relinquishment of the right of slavery is manumission, relinquishment of the right of sexual intercourse is divorce, relinquishment of what is in the liability is acquittal, and relinquishment of the right to retaliation and wounds is pardon. Linguistically, 'itiq' means removal from ownership; it is said 'a'taqahu fa'ataqa' (he freed him so he became free), and 'itq' is exiting from ownership, from the verbal pattern 'fa'ala' (fath) 'yaf'ilu' (kasr), 'ataqa al-'abdu 'itaqan' when he exited ownership, and 'ataqati al-farasu' when it outpaced and escaped, and 'ataqa farkhu al-qataati' when it flew. It is also said 'ataqa fulanun ba'da isti'lajin' when his complexion became soft after roughness. Its verbal noun is 'al-'itqu' and 'al-'itaqu', and not from it is 'al-'ataqatu' meaning oldness because its verb is 'fa'ala' (fath) 'yaf'ulu' (damm), and not from it is 'al-'itqu' meaning beauty because it is also from this verbal pattern and the 'ayn is also accompanied by dammah; as in Diya' al-Huloom. So the linguistic 'itq is then the legal 'itq, which is exiting from being owned, and it is more appropriate than their saying that 'itq linguistically is strength; and in law it is legal strength, because lexicographers did not say 'a'taqqa al-'abdu' when he became strong, but they said 'a'taqa al-'abdu' when he exited from being owned. Strength was mentioned only in the context of the bird's flight, etc. Its pillar in verbal creative manumission is the word indicating it. In al-Bada'i', its pillar is the word which is made as a sign of manumission in general, or what stands in place of a word. And that is known by explaining its cause. They said: its establishing cause may be a claim of lineage, or the ownership itself in the case of a relative, or an acknowledgment of a person's slave's freedom such that if he comes to own him he becomes free, or by entering dar al-harb, for if a harbi buys a Muslim slave and enters dar al-harb with him without his knowledge, he becomes free according to Abu Hanifa, and similarly the loss of his control over him, like fleeing from his harbi master to dar al-Islam, and the previously mentioned word may also be a cause. As for its motivating cause, in the obligatory it is to discharge his liability, and in other cases it is the intention of drawing near to Allah. Its types are four: obligatory, recommended, permissible, and prohibited. The obligatory is manumission in the expiation of killing, zihar, oath, and breaking the fast, except that in cases of killing, zihar, and breaking the fast it is obligatory by specification when one has the ability, and in the case of oath it is obligatory by choice. The recommended is manumission for the sake of Allah without obligation because the Law recommended it due to the hadith: 'Any believer who frees a believer in this world, Allah will free a limb of him from the Fire for every limb.' (Reported by al-Bukhari and Muslim). For this reason they recommended that a man free a male slave and a woman free a female slave to ensure the correspondence of limbs, but it is not an act of worship such that it is valid from a disbeliever. As for the permissible, it is manumission without intention. As for the prohibited, it is manumission for the sake of Satan. The explanation of its conditions and its legal effect—whether it is the removal of ownership or the affirmation of freedom—will come later. Then the author said: 'It is the establishment of legal strength for the owned slave' i.e., legally. And legal strength is his ability to undertake legal actions, his eligibility for guardianships and testimonies, and the repelling of others' control over him. In essence, it is the removal of the notional weakness which is slavery, which is an effect of disbelief. In al-Muhit: It is recommended for the slave to write a document for manumission and have witnesses attest it as a verification and safeguard against denial and dispute, as in the case of debts, unlike other transactions because they occur frequently, so documentation would lead to difficulty, but not so with manumission. Then the author said: 'and it is valid from a free, legally responsible person for his own slave by saying 'you are free' or by what expresses the body, and 'free', 'freed', 'liberated', 'I have liberated you', 'I have freed you', whether he intended or not' – an explanation of its conditions, its explicit expressions, and the ruling of the explicit. As for its conditions, the author mentioned three. The first is freedom: it is not needed alongside mentioning ownership, because freedom is to exclude manumission by a non-free person who is not an owner, as we will explain. By 'legally responsible' he excludes the manumission of a child, for it is not valid even if he is sane, just as his divorce is not valid; and the manumission of an insane person, for it is not valid. As for one who is sane at times and insane at others, he is sane during his sanity and insane during his insanity. Also excluded is the imbecile, the bewildered, the delirious, the unconscious, and the sleeping person. Their manumission is not valid just as their divorce is not valid. If he says 'I freed while I was a child' or 'while I was sleeping', his statement is accepted; likewise if he says 'I freed him while I was insane', provided his insanity is known, or he says 'while I was a harbi in dar al-harb' and that is known, because when he attributes it to a time in which manumission is impossible from him, it is known that he intended the formula of manumission not its reality, so he does not become an acknowledgment of manumission, just as if he said 'I freed him while I was a child.' (Reported by al-Bukhari in Kitab al-'Itq, Bab 1, Kitab al-Kaffarat Bab 6; Abu Dawud in Kitab al-'Itaq, Bab 13; Muslim in Kitab al-'Itq, Hadith 23-24; al-Tirmidhi in Kitab al-Nudhur, Bab 14, 20; Ahmad in his Musnad 2/447, 3/490-491, 4/107-113).
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Manumission (Kitab al-'Itq) from the commentary al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The author begins by defining 'itq (manumission) both linguistically and legally. Linguistically, 'itq means removal from ownership, as seen in its use for a slave becoming free, a horse escaping, or a bird flying. Some claimed 'itq meant strength, but the author refutes this by citing that lexicographers do not use 'a'taqa' in the sense of 'became strong'. Legally, 'itq is defined as establishing legal strength (al-quwwa al-shar'iyya) for the slave, i.e., granting him full legal capacity for actions, testimony, and guardianship, and removing the notional weakness of slavery which is an effect of disbelief.

The author then explains the arrangement of the book: it comes after divorce because both are relinquishments of rights. He enumerates the four types of relinquishment: manumission (right over a slave), divorce (right over sexual intercourse), acquittal (right in liability), and pardon (right to retaliation/wounds).

The pillar (rukn) of manumission is the word expressing it, whether explicit (sarih) like 'you are free' or implicit. The causes (asbab) that necessitate manumission include: claiming lineage, owning a close relative, acknowledging a slave's freedom, certain situations in dar al-harb (enemy territory), or the utterance itself. The motivating cause (sabab ba'ith) is either discharging an obligation (in obligatory cases) or seeking closeness to Allah (in voluntary cases).

Manumission is categorized into four types: (1) Obligatory (wajib) in expiations for killing, zihar, oath, and breaking fast – the first three are obligatory by specification (upon ability), oath by choice. (2) Recommended (mandub) for Allah's sake without compulsion, supported by the hadith that Allah frees a limb for each limb of a freed believer. (3) Permissible (mubah) without specific intention. (4) Prohibited (mahzur) if done for Satanic purposes.

The author then explains the conditions for valid manumission stated in the matn: the manumitter must be free (not a slave), legally responsible (mukallaf: adult and sane), and must own the slave he frees. The explicit expressions (sarih) like 'you are free' do not require intention (niyya) for validity. The commentary elaborates that a child's manumission is invalid even if sane, just as his divorce is invalid, and similarly for the insane, imbecile, etc. If a person claims he was a child, asleep, or insane when he uttered the words, his claim is accepted if known, because he could not have intended actual manumission at that time.

This passage establishes the foundational definitions and rules for the entire Book of Manumission in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
العتق (al-'itq): Legally, the establishment of legal strength (al-quwwa al-shar'iyya) for a slave, i.e., removing him from the status of ownership and granting him legal capacity.
الصريح (al-sarih): Explicit expressions of manumission like 'anta hurr' (you are free) which are valid even without intention (niyya).
الركن (al-rukn): The pillar of manumission is the word (lafz) that indicates it, either explicitly or by what stands in place of a word.
السبب (al-sabab): The establishing cause (sabab muthbit) that leads to manumission, such as claiming lineage, owning a relative, etc. Also the motivating cause (sabab ba'ith) like discharging an obligation or seeking reward.
المكلف (al-mukallaf): A legally responsible person: an adult Muslim of sound mind who is subject to legal obligations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A 12-year-old sane boy declares 'you are free' to his slave. According to the text, is this manumission valid?
A) Yes, because he is sane.
B) No, because he is not mukallaf (legally responsible).
C) Yes, if he owns the slave.
D) Only if his guardian approves.

Key Takeaway
Manumission is valid only from a free, legally responsible owner, using explicit expressions (no intention needed), and is classified into obligatory, recommended, permissible, and prohibited.

Definition and Types of 'Itq (Emancipation)
Introduction to 'itq with definition, verbal forms, and four legal categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four legal categories of emancipation ('itq) in Hanafi fiqh and identify the ruling for each category with its evidence.

Core Idea
Emancipation can be obligatory, recommended, permissible, or prohibited depending on intention and context.

Emancipation of a slave is a significant act in Islamic law with varying legal consequences. In this opening passage from the chapter on 'itq, the author defines the act, lists the verbal formulas that effect it, and introduces special scenarios like a harbi owning a Muslim slave. Most importantly, he categorizes emancipation into four types based on the motive and legal obligation. Understanding these categories helps you recognize when emancipation is a duty, a virtue, a neutral act, or even a sin.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the key definition and verbal forms
Identify the words that effect emancipation (e.g., 'a'taqtuka, harrartuka) and note that intention (niyyah) is not always required for the act to be valid—the verbal expression itself suffices.
The text says 'nawaha aw la'—whether he intends or not—indicating that explicit words are effective regardless of intention.
2
Understand the special cases (harbi and fleeing slave)
Note two examples where emancipation occurs automatically: (1) a harbi who buys a Muslim slave and brings him into dar al-harb without the slave's knowledge (Abu Hanifa's view), (2) a slave fleeing from a harbi master to dar al-Islam. These show that change of control can trigger emancipation.
This illustrates the principle that ownership by a non-Muslim over a Muslim in dar al-harb is invalid.
3
Identify the motivating cause (sabab ba'ith) for each type
For wajib emancipation, the cause is clearing one's liability (e.g., expiation for a sin). For other types, it is seeking nearness to Allah. This links the internal motive to the legal ruling.
The text distinguishes the sabab ba'ith for wajib from other categories.
4
List the four types and their rulings
Memorize the four categories: wajib (obligatory), mandub (recommended), mubah (permissible), mahthur (prohibited). For wajib, note the sub‑distinction between 'ala al-ta'yin (specific, e.g., expiation for killing, zihar, iftar) and 'ala al-takhyir (choice, e.g., expiation for oath).
Wajib emancipation in expiation for oath is one of three options (feed, clothe, or free a slave).
5
Recognize the evidence and conditions for mandub
The hadith 'Any believer who emancipates a believer...' is cited to show the reward, making emancipation mandub. Note the recommendation that a man free a male slave and a woman free a female slave for perfect correspondence of limbs. Understand that such emancipation is not an act of worship that a disbeliever can perform.
The hadith is used as a proof for the recommended nature, though its dalala is zanni.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب العتق
بما يعبر به عن البدن وعتيق ومعتق ومحرر وحررتك وأعتقتك نواه أو لا وبلا ملك ولا دار الحرب فإن الحربي إذا اشترى عبداً مسلماً فدخل به إلى دار الحرب ولم يشعر عتق عند أبي حنيفة، وكذا زوال يده عنه بأن هرب عن مولاه الحربي إلى دار الإسلام، وقد يكون اللفظ المذكور. وأما سببه الباعث ففي الواجب تفريغ ذمته وفي غيره قصد التقرب إلى الله تعالى عز وجل. وأنواعه أربعة واجب ومندوب ومباح ومحظور. فالواجب الإعتاق في كفارة القتل والظهار واليمين والإفطار إلا أنه في باب القتل والظهار والإفطار واجب على التعيين عند القدرة عليه، وفي باب اليمين واجب على التخيير . والمندوب الإعتاق لوجه الله تعالى من غير إيجاب لأن الشرع ندب إلى ذلك للحديث أيما مؤمن أعتق مؤمناً في الدنيا أعتق الله بكل عضو منه عضواً منه من النار ولهذا استحبوا أن يعتق الرجل العبد والمرأة الأمة ليتحقق مقابلة الأعضاء بالأعضاء لكنه ليس بعبادة حتى يصح من الكافر وأما المباح فهو الإعتاق من غير نية . وأما المحظور فهو الإعتاق لوجه الشيطان وسيأتي تمامه وسيأتي بيان شرائطه وحكمه زوال الملك أو ثبوت العتق على الاختلاف .
The Book of Emancipation. By what expresses the body and 'atiq, mu'taq, muharrar, harrartuka, a'taqtuka, whether he intends or not, and without ownership and without the abode of war. For if a harbi buys a Muslim slave and enters the abode of war with him without his (the slave's) knowledge, he becomes free according to Abu Hanifa. Likewise, removal of his (the owner's) control over him by the slave fleeing from his harbi master to the abode of Islam. And the mentioned word may occur. As for its motivating cause, in the obligatory (category) it is clearing his liability, and in others it is seeking nearness to Allah Most High. Its types are four: obligatory, recommended, permissible, and prohibited. The obligatory is emancipation in the expiation for killing, zihar, oath, and (intentional) breaking the fast, except that in the chapter of killing, zihar, and breaking the fast it is specifically obligatory when one is able to do it, and in the chapter of oath it is obligatory by choice. The recommended is emancipation for the sake of Allah without it being obligatory, because the Lawgiver encouraged it by the hadith: 'Any believer who emancipates a believer (male) in the world, Allah will emancipate from the Fire for every member of his (the slave's) body a member of his (the emancipator's) body.' For this reason they recommended that a man emancipates a male slave and a woman emancipates a female slave so that the mutual correspondence of limbs is realized. But it is not an act of worship such that it is valid from a disbeliever. As for the permissible, it is emancipation without intention. As for the prohibited, it is emancipation for the sake of Satan. Its completion will follow, and the explanation of its conditions and its ruling—removal of ownership or establishment of freedom according to the difference of opinion—will follow.
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the chapter on emancipation (kitab al-'itq) in the commentary. It first presents key rulings: the words that effect emancipation, the case of a harbi purchasing a Muslim slave, and the fleeing slave. Then it explains the motivating cause (sabab ba'ith) for each type: for obligatory emancipation it is clearing one's liability (e.g., expiation); for others it is seeking nearness to Allah. The author then categorizes emancipation into four types: wajib (obligatory), mandub (recommended), mubah (permissible), and mahthur (prohibited). The obligatory includes emancipation for expiation of killing (qatl khata'), zihar (a form of divorce), oath (yamin), and intentional breaking of the fast (iftar). For killing, zihar, and iftar, it is specifically wajib 'ala al-ta'yin (specific obligation) if one is able; for oath it is wajib 'ala al-takhyir (obligatory by choice, i.e., one may also feed or clothe). The recommended is for the sake of Allah, supported by the hadith that for every limb of a slave emancipated, Allah will emancipate a limb of the emancipator from Fire. It is recommended that a man emancipate a male slave and a woman a female slave to perfectly match limbs. Note that emancipation from a disbeliever is not considered an act of worship, so it can be valid. The permissible is emancipation without any intention (niyyah). The prohibited is for the sake of Satan (e.g., to harm someone). The passage concludes by promising further discussion of conditions and the ruling regarding whether ownership is removed or freedom is established.

Key Terms
عتق ('itq): Legal emancipation of a slave, removing the legal disability of slavery.
مملوك (mamluk): A slave owned in full (raqaba); the person subject to ownership rights.
دار الحرب (dar al-harb): Territory not under Muslim rule where hostilities may exist.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following emancipations is considered wajib 'ala al-ta'yin (specifically obligatory) when the person is able?
A) Emancipation in expiation for an oath (yamin)
B) Emancipation in expiation for zihar
C) Emancipation without intention (mubah)
D) Emancipation for the sake of Allah (mandub)

Key Takeaway
Emancipation ('itq) is not a single-ruling act; it is categorized into wajib, mandub, mubah, and mahthur based on the motive and legal context, and the verbal formula alone is sufficient for legal effect.

Definition of 'Itq and the Recommendation of Writing a Deed
Definition and recommendation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of 'itq and explain why writing a deed of emancipation is recommended.

Core Idea
'Itq establishes legal capacity, not mere physical freedom; writing a deed is recommended to avoid disputes.

This passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the legal essence of emancipation (itq) as the establishment of legal capacity (quwwa shar'iyya) for the slave. It then discusses a practical recommendation: writing a deed of emancipation. Understanding the definition clarifies the transformative legal effect of freeing a slave, and the recommendation shows how Hanafi jurists balance practicality with documentation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of 'itq
Find the statement: 'itq is the establishment of legal capacity. Note that the commentary explains legal capacity as the ability to perform legal acts, eligibility for guardianship and testimony, and repelling others' control.
2
Understand the legal effect
Legal capacity (al-quwwa al-shar'iyya) is the core effect. The removal of legal weakness (da'f hukmi) which is slavery, a consequence of disbelief.
Slavery is seen as a punishment for kufr, thus emancipation is an act of reward.
3
Grasp the recommendation of writing
The text from al-Muhit states it is mustahabb for the slave to write a deed and have witnesses for documentation and to prevent denial and dispute.
4
Compare with other transactions
Writing is recommended for emancipation because it is rare; for frequent transactions, writing would cause hardship (haraj).
The analogy is with writing debts in Quran 2:282, but emancipation is not frequent.
5
Apply the concept
State that the definition determines the legal consequences of emancipation, and the recommendation is a practical fiqh ruling based on the principle of blocking harm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (هو إثبات القوة الشرعية للمملوك) أي الإعتاق شرعاً. والقوة الشرعية هي قدرته على التصرفات الشرعية وأهليته للولايات والشهادات ودفع تصرف الغير عليه . وحاصله أنه إزالة الضعف الحكمي الذي هو الرق الذي هو أثر الكفر. وفي المحيط : ويستحب للعبد أن يكتب للعتق كتاباً ويشهد عليه شهوداً توثيقاً وصيانة عن التجاحد والتنازع فيه كما في المداينة بخلاف سائر التجارات لأنه ما يكثر وقوعها فالكتابة فيها تؤدي إلى الحرج ولا كذلك العتق
His statement: (It is the establishment of legal capacity for the owned person) i.e., emancipation in Shari'ah. And legal capacity is his ability to perform legal transactions and his eligibility for guardianships and testimonies and repelling the control of others over him. In summary, it is the removal of the legal weakness which is slavery, which is the effect of disbelief. And in al-Muhit: It is recommended for the slave to write a document for emancipation and to have witnesses present for documentation and to protect against denial and dispute in it, just as in the case of debt, unlike other transactions because they occur frequently so writing in them leads to hardship, and not so for emancipation.
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn's definition: 'itq is the establishment of legal capacity (quwwa shar'iyya) for the slave. The commentator explains that legal capacity means the ability to perform legal acts, eligibility for guardianship and testimony, and the right to repel the control of others. In essence, it removes the legal weakness of slavery, which is a consequence of disbelief (as slavery originates from kufr in the Hanafi view). Then the commentator quotes from al-Muhit that it is mustahabb (recommended) for the slave to write a deed of emancipation (kitabah) and have witnesses, for documentation and to avoid denial or dispute—similar to the recommendation of writing debts as in the Quranic verse (2:282), but unlike other frequent transactions where writing would cause hardship. Emancipation is not frequent, so writing is recommended to secure rights.

Key Terms
القوة الشرعية (al-quwwa al-shar'iyya): Legal capacity: the ability to perform legal acts and be subject to rights and obligations, including eligibility for guardianship and testimony.
الرق (al-riqq): Slavery: a legal weakness (da'f hukmi) that is a consequence of disbelief (kufr).
الكتابة (al-kitabah): Writing a document; here referring to writing a deed of emancipation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it recommended to write a document for emancipation?
A) Because it is an obligation from the Quran.
B) For documentation and to avoid denial and dispute, similar to writing debts.
C) Because emancipation is a frequent transaction.
D) Because the slave must have a copy.

Key Takeaway
Emancipation (itq) in Hanafi fiqh is defined as the establishment of full legal capacity (quwwa shar'iyya) for the slave, and writing a deed of emancipation is recommended to document and prevent disputes.

Conditions for Valid Emancipation ('Itq) in Hanafi Fiqh
Explains conditions and refines matn's wording

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid emancipation and identify which conditions are required for the act to be effective (nafidh) versus merely valid (sahih).

Core Idea
Emancipation is valid only from a free, responsible owner, but lack of knowledge or sanity does not invalidate it.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq examines the conditions for a valid emancipation as stated in the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq. The commentator refines each condition, clarifying what is required for the emancipation to be legally sound (sahih) versus what is required for it to take effect (nafidh). You will learn to distinguish between the conditions for the emancipator (mukallaf, owner) and the slave (ownership in corpus), and understand that certain defects like intoxication or compulsion do not prevent validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three conditions stated in the matn
Look for the three conditions the author mentions: free (hurr), legally responsible (mukallaf), and owned slave (mamluk). Note that the commentator argues the first is redundant.
2
Examine the condition of being mukallaf
Understand who is excluded (child, madman, unconscious, etc.). Note that the emancipator's word is taken if he claims he was not mukallaf at the time.
3
Understand ownership condition
Know that full ownership of the corpus is required, excluding mudabbar and mukatab. Also, emancipation of another's slave is valid but suspended (mawquf).
4
Consider timing of ownership
For immediate emancipation, ownership at utterance is required; for suspended emancipation, ownership at suspension and condition fulfillment.
5
Note what is NOT required
Sanity, volition, intent, acceptance (except in 'itq 'ala mal), and freedom from option are not required for validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ويصح من حر مكلف لمملوكه بأنت حر أو بما يعبر به عن البدن وعتيق ومعتق ومحرر وحررتك وأعتقتك نواه أو لا بيان لشرائطه وصريحه وحكم الصريح . أما شرائطه فذكر المصنف أنها ثلاثة الأول منها لا حاجة إليه مع ذكر الملك لأن الحرية للاحتراز عن إعتاق غير الحر وهو ليس بمالك كما سنبينه . واحترز بالمكلف عن عتق الصبي فإنه لا يصح وإن كان عاقلاً كما لا يصح طلاقه، وعن عتق المجنون فإنه لا يصح، وأما الذي يجن ويفيق فهو في حالة إفاقته عاقل وفي حالة جنونه مجنون. وخرج المعتوه أيضاً والمدهوش والمبرسم والمغمى عليه والنائم . فلا يصح إعتاقهم كما لا يصح طلاقهم. ولو قال أعتقت وأنا صبي أو وأنا نائم كان القول قوله، وكذا لو قال أعتقته وأنا مجنون بشرط أن يعلم جنونه، أو قال وأنا حربي في دار الحرب وقد علم ذلك لأنه لما أضافه إلى زمان لا الإعتاق يتصور منه علم أنه أراد صيغة الإعتاق لا حقيقته فلم يصر معترفاً بالإعتاق كما لو قال أعتقته قبل أن أخلق أو يخلق . وخرج باشتراط أن يكون مملوكاً له إعتاق العبد المأذون له في التجارة أو المكاتب لانعدام ملك الرقبة، وكذا لو اشترى العبد المأذون له في التجارة محرماً منه أو المكاتب كذلك فإنه لا يعتق عليهما لعدم ملكهما ويرد على المصنف إعتاق عبد الغير فإنه صحيح موقوف على إجازة سيده إن لم يكن وكيله نعم هو شرط للنفاد وليس الكلام هنا إلا الصحة، ولو أبدله بقوله للمملوك لكان أولى لأن شرطه كما في المستصفى أن يكون المحل مملوكاً. والمراد بالمملوك المملوك رقبة وإن لم يكن في يده فصح إعتاق المولى المكاتب والعبد المأذون والمشتري قبل القبض والمرهون والمستأجر والعبد الموصي برقبته لإنسان وبخدمته لآخر إذا أعتقه الموصى له بالرقبة ولا يشترط أن يكون عالماً بأنه مملوكه حتى لو قال الغاصب للمالك أعتق رقبة هذا العبد فأعتقه وهو لا يعلم أنه عبده عتق ولا يرجع على الغاصب بشيء، وكذا لو قال البائع للمشتري أعتق عبدي هذا وأشار إلى المبيع فأعتقه أنه المشتري ولم يعلم عبده صح إعتاقه ويجعل قبضاً ويلزمه الثمن كما في الكشف الكبير في بحث القضاء. وأخرج باشتراط المملوكية عتق الحمل إذا ولدته لستة أشهر فأكثر لعدم التيقن بوجوده وقته بخلاف ما إذا ولدته لأقل منها فإنه يصح . ويشترط وجود الملك للمعتق وقت وجود الإعتاق لينفذ إن كان منجزاً وإن كان معلقاً بما سوى الملك وسببه فإنه يشترط وجود الملك وقت التعليق كالتعليق بدخول الدار ونحوه وكذا يشترط وقت نزول الجزاء، ولا يشترط بقاء الملك فيما بينهما. وأما إذا كان معلقاً بالملك كإن ملكتك فأنت حر فلا يشترط له ذلك شيء من ذلك ولم يشترط المصنف أن يكون صاحياً ولا طائعاً لصحة عتق السكران والمكره عندنا كطلاقهما، وكذا لم يشترط العمد لصحة عتق المخطئ، ولم يشترط قبول العبد للإعتاق لأنه ليس بشرط إلا في العتق على مال فإن قبوله شرط كما سنذكره في بابه. وكذا لم يشترط خلوه عن الخيار لعدم صحة الخيار فيه من جانب المولى فيقع العتق ويبطل الشرط، وأما من جانب العبد في العتق على مال فلا بد من خلوه عن خياره حتى لو رد العبد العتق في مدة الخيار ينفسخ العقد ولا يعتق كما في الطلاق على مال، وكذا الصلح من دم العمد بشرط الخيار، فإن كان من جانب المولى فهو باطل والصلح صحيح وإن كان للقاتل فهو صحيح، فإن فسخ العقد ففي القياس يبطل العفو وفي الاستحسان لا يبطل ويلزم القاتل الدية. ولم يشترط قبول العبد للإعتاق لأنه ليس بشرط إلا في العتق على مال فإن قبوله شرط كما سنذكره في بابه. وكذا لم يشترط خلوه عن الخيار لعدم صحة الخيار فيه من جانب المولى فيقع العتق ويبطل الشرط، وأما من جانب العبد في العتق على مال فلا بد من خلوه عن خياره حتى لو رد العبد العتق في مدة الخيار ينفسخ العقد ولا يعتق كما في الطلاق على مال، وكذا الصلح من دم العمد بشرط الخيار، فإن كان من جانب المولى فهو باطل والصلح صحيح وإن كان للقاتل فهو صحيح، فإن فسخ العقد ففي القياس يبطل العفو وفي الاستحسان لا يبطل ويلزم القاتل الدية. قوله : (بخلاف طلاقك على واجب إلى قوله وقوعه قال الرملي : فيه نظر أولاً بالمنع إذ هو
His statement: (And it is valid from a free, legally responsible person for his owned slave by 'You are free' or by what expresses the body, and 'atiq, mu'taq, muharrar, harrartuka, a'taqtuka, whether he intends or not) is an explanation of its conditions and explicit wording and ruling of the explicit. As for its conditions, the author mentioned three; the first of them is not needed since ownership is mentioned, because 'free' is to avoid emancipating a non-free person who is not an owner, as we will explain. By 'legally responsible' he excludes the emancipation of a child, for it is not valid even if he is intelligent, just as his divorce is not valid, and the emancipation of a madman, for it is not valid. As for the intermittent madman, in his lucid state he is sane, in his mad state he is mad. Also excluded are the imbecile, bewildered, delirious, unconscious, and sleeping person. Their emancipation is not valid just as their divorce is not valid. If he said 'I emancipated while I was a child' or 'while I was sleeping', his word is accepted. Similarly, if he said 'I emancipated him while I was mad' on condition that his madness is known, or 'while I was a harbi in Dar al-Harb' and that is known, because when he attributes it to a time when emancipation is not conceivable from him, it is known that he intended the wording of emancipation not its reality, so he is not considered admitting to emancipation, just as if he said 'I emancipated him before I was created or before he was created.' By the condition that the slave be owned by him, he excludes the emancipation of a slave authorized to trade (ma'dhun) or a mukatab, due to lack of ownership of the corpus. Likewise, if an authorized slave or mukatab buys a relative, it does not make them free because they do not own him. And an objection is made against the author regarding the emancipation of another's slave: it is valid but suspended upon the owner's permission if he is not his agent; yes, it is a condition for effectiveness (nifadh) but we are here discussing validity. If he replaced it with 'for his owned slave' it would be better, because its condition, as in al-Mustasfa, is that the subject be owned. By 'owned' is meant owned in corpus even if not in his possession, so the emancipation of a mukatab, an authorized slave, an item purchased before taking possession, a mortgaged slave, a leased slave, and a slave whose corpus is bequeathed to one person and services to another is valid if the legatee of the corpus emancipates him. It is not required that he know he is his slave; even if a usurper said to the owner 'emancipate this slave' and he emancipates him without knowing it is his own slave, it is valid and he cannot claim anything from the usurper. Similarly, if the seller said to the buyer 'emancipate this slave of mine' and pointed to the sold item, and the buyer emancipated it without knowing it is his own slave, his emancipation is valid, it counts as taking possession, and the price is binding on him, as in al-Kashf al-Kabir in the chapter of qada. By the condition of ownership, the emancipation of a fetus is excluded if she gives birth after six months or more, because of lack of certainty of its existence at the time, unlike if she gives birth before that, then it is valid. The existence of ownership for the emancipator at the time of emancipation is required for it to be effective if it is unconditional; if it is suspended on something other than ownership and its cause, then ownership is required at the time of suspension, like suspension on entering the house and similar, and also at the time of the occurrence of the condition (al-jaza'), but continuity of ownership in between is not required. If it is suspended on ownership itself, like 'if I own you, you are free', then none of that is required. The author did not require sanity or volition, for the validity of the emancipation of an intoxicated person and one under compulsion according to us, like their divorce. Likewise, he did not require intent for the validity of the emancipation of a mistaken person. He did not require the acceptance of the slave because it is not a condition except in emancipation for compensation (al-'itq 'ala mal), where acceptance is a condition, as will be mentioned in its chapter. Likewise, he did not require freedom from option (khiyar), because option is not valid from the master's side, so the emancipation occurs and the condition is void. As for the slave's side in emancipation for compensation, he must be free of option; if the slave rejects the emancipation during the option period, the contract is rescinded and he does not become free, as in divorce for compensation. Similarly, a settlement (sulh) from intentional homicide with a condition of option: if it is from the side of the master (the deceased's heir?), it is void and the settlement is valid; if it is from the killer, it is valid. If he rescinds the contract, by qiyas the pardon is void, but by istihsan it is not void and the killer must pay the blood money. His statement: (Unlike your divorce is upon wajib... to his saying 'its occurrence' al-Ramli said: there is consideration, firstly by prevention, because it is...
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This lengthy passage is a commentary on the matn's statement that emancipation is valid from a free, legally responsible person for his owned slave using clear expressions. The commentator examines each condition: 'free' (hurr) excludes a slave's emancipation of another (since a slave cannot own), 'legally responsible' (mukallaf) excludes children, madmen, and others like the unconscious, imbecile, etc. He discusses cases where someone claims he was a child, mad, etc., at the time of uttering the formula—then his word is taken if that condition is known. He then discusses the condition of ownership (mamlukiyya): the slave must be fully owned in corpus (raqaba). This excludes authorized slaves (ma'dhun) and mukatab slaves because they are not fully owned. An objection is addressed: emancipation of another's slave is valid but suspended on the owner's permission. The commentator suggests the matn should say 'for his owned slave' to be precise. Then he explains that ownership of the corpus suffices even if the slave is not in the owner's possession (e.g., mortgaged, leased, bequeathed). It is not required that the emancipator know the slave is his own; a usurper's instruction can lead to valid emancipation even if the owner is unaware. Then he discusses timing: for unconditional emancipation, ownership must exist at the moment of utterance; for suspended emancipation (on a condition other than ownership), ownership must exist at the time of suspension and at the time the condition is fulfilled; for suspension on ownership itself (e.g., 'if I own you, you are free'), no prior ownership is needed. The commentator notes that the matn did not require sanity or volition—thus a drunk or coerced person's emancipation is valid (like their divorce). Mistaken utterances are also valid. Acceptance of the slave is not required except in 'itq 'ala mal (emancipation for payment). Similarly, option (khiyar) is not valid from the master's side, so a condition of option is void and emancipation stands. For the slave's side in 'itq 'ala mal, the slave must be free of option. The passage ends with a discussion of a statement from al-Ramli critiquing something, but the text cuts off.

Key Terms
مكلف (mukallaf): A person who is legally responsible: sane, adult, and not in a state of unconsciousness, madness, etc.
إعتاق عبد الغير (i'taq 'abd al-ghayr): Emancipation of another's slave without permission; it is suspended (mawquf) on the owner's subsequent approval.
العتق على مال (al-'itq 'ala mal): Emancipation in exchange for payment from the slave to the master.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on a person emancipating a slave he does not know is his own, if he was instructed by a usurper?
A) The emancipation is invalid because he did not know.
B) The emancipation is valid and he cannot reclaim anything from the usurper.
C) The emancipation is valid but he can sue the usurper for the value.
D) The emancipation is suspended upon his later knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Emancipation is valid from any free legally responsible person for his owned slave, even if drunk, coerced, or unaware of ownership.

Explicit Expressions (Sarih) in Emancipation: Words That Free Without Intention
Explaining which expressions of emancipation are considered explicit and their immediate legal effect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify sarih expressions of emancipation and state the Hanafi ruling on limb-based expressions, including the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the private part.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, words conventionally used for the whole person are explicit for emancipation and do not require intention.

This lesson focuses on the category of explicit (sarih) expressions for emancipation. Understanding which words are sarih is crucial because they cause emancipation automatically, regardless of the master's intent. The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the list of such expressions and highlights a key dispute among the Hanafi imams regarding the private part.

Guided Path
1
Identify the category of sarih (explicit) expressions
Sarih expressions are those that are only used for emancipation, like 'hurr' and 'itq'. They cause emancipation without any need for intention (niyya).
The text lists 'anta hurr', 'ra'suka hurr', 'wajhuka hurr', 'badanuka hurr', and for female slaves 'farjuki hurr'.
2
Recognize the limb-based sarih expressions
A limb that conventionally represents the whole (like neck, head, face, private part) is treated as the whole. Therefore, saying 'your neck is free' is equivalent to 'you are free'.
Limbs like hand, foot, or liver are not conventionally used to refer to the whole.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on 'farjuka hurr' for a male slave
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf rule that 'farjuka hurr' emancipates a male slave; Muhammad has two narrations (one for emancipation, one against).
The mufta bihi (operative position) is that it does emancipate, as this is the view of the majority.
4
Understand partial emancipation
Unlike talaq (divorce), emancipation is divisible according to Abu Hanifa. Saying 'half of you is free' only frees that proportion.
This contrasts with the Shafi'i position that emancipation is indivisible.
5
Identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The difference between Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Muhammad affects whether the slave becomes free immediately or requires further evidence of intention.
If one follows the minority position of Muhammad, the slave might not be free unless the master's intent is proven.

لا يعتق وبقي إقراره لكونه غير مملوك أصلاً فترتب عليه ما ذكر وعندي أن هذه المسألة مغايرة لمسألة الكتاب وذلك أنه مسألة الكتاب إنما أقر بأنه لا ملك له
You are pure. If it is causative, he is not believed in court but is believed religiously. If he says 'You are free from such work' or 'free today from this work', he becomes free in court. If he calls his slave 'Salim', and another slave named Marzuq responds, and he says 'You are free' with no intention, the one who responded becomes free. If he says 'I meant Salim', both become free in court, but between him and Allah, only the one he intended becomes free. If he says 'O Salim, you are free' and it turns out to be another slave of his or someone else's, Salim becomes free because there was no address except to Salim. In al-Zahiriyya and al-Khaniyya: A slave woman standing before her master, a man asked her 'Are you a slave or free?' The master intended to say 'What is your question? Is she a slave or free?' but expedited and said 'She is free', she becomes free in court. In al-Khaniyya: If he says to his slave whose blood was lawful for him due to retaliation 'I emancipated you' and says 'I meant from killing', he becomes free in court and the blood is dropped due to his confession. The author mentioned that a limb by which one expresses the whole is like the whole, as when he says 'Your neck is free' or 'your head' or 'your face' or 'your body' or 'your private part' for a slave woman as explained in divorce, unlike a limb not used to express the whole like hand or foot. In al-Mujtaba, if he says to his slave 'your private part is free', he becomes free according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, and from Muhammad there are two narrations. Similarly if he says 'your liver is free' he becomes free. If he says 'your body is a free body', he becomes free. Similarly the private part and head. From Abu Yusuf, if he says 'Your head is a free head', he does not become free. If he says to her 'your private part is free from intercourse', she becomes free in court. In al-Khaniyya: if he says 'your private part is free' to a male slave or a female slave, he becomes free, unlike the male organ in the narration. If he says to his male slave 'you are free (feminine)' or to his female slave 'you are free (masculine)', he becomes free in both cases; thus related from Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. In al-Khulasa, contrary to if he says to a man 'O adulteress' meaning it, it is not slander. The author did not mention the common part as he did in divorce, due to the difference between emancipation and divorce: divorce is indivisible by agreement, so mentioning part is like the whole, while emancipation is divisible according to the Imam. So if he says 'half of you is free' or 'a third of you is free', only that amount becomes free according to him. What is in Ghayat al-Bayan equating divorce and emancipation concerning addition to a common part is an oversight. In al-Khaniyya: If he says 'the sixth is free' etc. The author did not mention expressions that run in the manner of explicit. In al-Bada'i': As for what is explicit, such as 'I gifted you yourself' or 'I gifted yourself from you' or 'I sold you yourself', he becomes free, whether he accepted or not, whether he intended or not, because the offer from the gift-giver and seller is removal of ownership from the gifted and sold, and acceptance is needed only for establishing ownership for the recipient. Here, 'be' means 'would be'. His saying 'if he said' etc. is its answer. In Sharh al-Maqdisi: Its face is that 'lam' negates the past, so it fails at the time of his speech, and is not for negation of the present; denying the wealth in the present does not entail denying it in the past, because it is possible that he paid it after that time. His saying: (And similarly the private part and head mentioned in al-Mujtaba with a different symbol than the previous). Without ownership, nor slavery, nor right over you, if he intends, and this is my son, or my father, or my mother, and this is my master, or O my master, or ownership does not establish for the slave in himself because he is not fit to be owned by himself, so the gift and sale remain removal of ownership from the slave to no one; this is the meaning of emancipation. Abu Hanifa said: If he says to his slave 'I gifted you yourself' and says 'I meant I gifted him his emancipation, i.e., I do not emancipate him', he does not become free in court because it is a departure from the apparent, and he is believed between him and Allah because he intended what his words could bear. In al-Khaniyya: He added: 'I gave in charity yourself to you'. Regarding these expressions, there are three sayings: some say they are attached to the explicit, some say they are metaphors and do not need intention, but the correct third saying is that they are real explicit expressions as stated by a group. His saying: (Without ownership, nor slavery, nor right over me, if he intends) is an explanation of metaphors, because negating these things could be by sale, kitaba, or emancipation, and negation of right could be by emancipation or making him pleased, so that he has no right in blame or punishment, so it becomes ambiguous, and the ambiguous is not determined except by intention. This refutes what is in Ghayat al-Bayan. His saying in al-Mukhtasar: 'over me' relates to the three. He restricted with his saying 'no right over me' because if he says 'no right over me except the right of wala'', he becomes free in court and he is not believed that he intended other than emancipation. If he says 'no right over me except the right of muwalat', it is a debt in court. If emancipation does not occur with 'I have no ownership' or 'you have left my ownership', can he claim it? In Khulasat al-Fatawa: If he says to his slave 'you are not owned', he does not become free, but he cannot claim him afterwards nor use him; if he dies, he does not inherit by wala'. If the slave then says 'I am his slave' and he confirms him, he becomes his slave. Similarly if he says 'this is not my slave', he does not become free. The apparent meaning is that he becomes apparently free, not owned, with the status of a free person until someone comes and claims him. So metaphors also include 'I have left your way', 'I have no right over you', and his saying to his slave woman 'I have released you' – she becomes free with intention. Also from metaphors, as in al-Bada'i': 'Your affair is in your hand', 'choose', depends on intention. There is disagreement about 'You are Allah'. In al-Zahiriyya, he does not become free according to Abu Hanifa even if he intends. Muhammad said: if he intended... From his saying: (He does not become free in court because it is a departure... as in the copies, and it is a distortion with an extra 'lam', or the original is 'they are not believed'... His saying: (does not need intention) the apparent is that 'la' is extra, and the correct is 'needs intention'. His saying: (And its apparent is that he becomes apparently free...) In al-Nahr, I say: The reasoning in al-Muhit that 'you are not owned' is not explicit in emancipation but carries it... If he does not intend it, he does not become free, and his confession remains that he is not owned at all, so what was mentioned is entailed. In my view, this issue is different from the issue of the book, because in the book he only confessed that he has no ownership.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, deals with the forms (siyagh) of emancipation (itq) and whether they are explicit (sarih) or metaphorical (kinaya). The author, Zayn al-Din ibn Nujaym, expands on the rulings from the matn and earlier sources. The passage type is an expansion probe, as it elaborates on previously mentioned principles and adds details on various expressions.

The text begins by distinguishing between direct and causative expressions. If a master uses a direct expression like 'you are free,' the slave becomes free in court. If he uses a causative expression (e.g., 'you are free from this work'), he is not believed in court regarding any qualification, but is believed religiously. Then it discusses cases where a master calls one slave but another responds, and the master says 'you are free' – the one who responded becomes free. If the master intended a specific slave, in court both are free due to the ambiguity, but religiously only the intended one is free.

The text then covers expressions using parts of the body. According to the Hanafi school, if one uses a term that conventionally represents the whole (like 'your neck,' 'your head,' 'your face'), it is like saying 'you are free' because these are used metonymically for the entire person. However, limbs like 'hand' or 'foot' are not such, so saying 'your hand is free' does not emancipate. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that saying 'your private part is free' emancipates, while Muhammad has two narrations. Similarly, 'your liver is free' emancipates. But if one says 'your head is a free head' according to Abu Yusuf, it does not emancipate. If a female slave is told 'your private part is free from intercourse,' she becomes free in court.

Then the text discusses partial emancipation. Unlike talaq (divorce), which is indivisible, emancipation can be partial according to Imam Abu Hanifa. So if one says 'half of you is free' or 'a third of you is free,' only that portion becomes free. This is a point of difference with the Shafi'i view, but the text does not present it as an inter-madhhab dispute.

The text then moves to expressions that are considered explicit (sarih) by some scholars. The author notes that in al-Bada'i', expressions like 'I gifted you yourself' or 'I sold you yourself' are explicit and cause emancipation without acceptance or intention, because the offer itself removes ownership. However, if the master says 'I gifted you yourself' but claims he meant 'I gifted him his emancipation' (i.e., not emancipation), Abu Hanifa rules that in court it is not accepted (he is not believed), but between him and Allah he is believed if his words could bear that meaning. The text presents three scholarly opinions on these expressions: some treat them as explicit, some as kinaya needing no intention (contradictory?), and the correct view (chosen by Ibn al-Humam) that they are truly explicit.

Finally, the passage discusses kinaya (metaphorical) expressions such as 'no ownership of me over you,' 'no slavery,' 'no right over you.' These are ambiguous because they could mean emancipation, sale, kitaba, etc. Therefore, emancipation only occurs with intention (niyya). If one says 'no right over me except the right of wala',' emancipation occurs in court and the master is not believed if he claims otherwise. If he says 'except the right of muwalat,' it is considered a debt in court (i.e., he must fulfill that right, but it's not emancipation). There is also discussion of the ruling when one says 'you are not my slave' or 'this is not my slave' – it does not cause emancipation, but the slave becomes effectively free in appearance until someone claims him. The master cannot use him or inherit from him. The text ends with a note about the expression 'you are Allah' – Abu Hanifa says it does not cause emancipation even with intention, while Muhammad holds that if he intended (possibly the divine attribute?), it does.

Key Terms
صريح (sarih): Explicit expression of emancipation that does not require intention, such as 'anta hurr' or 'ra'suka hurr'.
كناية (kinayah): Implicit expression that could mean emancipation or something else, requiring intention (niyya) to cause emancipation.
عتق (itq): Legal emancipation of a slave, removing ownership and making the slave free.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, if a master says to his male slave 'your private part is free' (farjuka hurr), what is the ruling according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf?
A) The slave does not become free.
B) The slave becomes free.
C) It depends on the master's intention.
D) Only if the master also says 'your head is free'.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, expressions like 'your neck is free' are explicit and cause emancipation without intention, but the list of such expressions is limited to those conventionally used for the whole person.

Implicit Expressions (Kinaya) and the 'Gift/Sale' Controversy
Explaining implicit expressions of emancipation and the scholarly disagreement on gift/sale formulations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify kinaya expressions for emancipation and explain the three scholarly opinions on whether 'I gifted you yourself' is explicit or requires intention.

Core Idea
Implicit expressions like 'no ownership' require intention to cause emancipation, while expressions like 'I gifted you yourself' are considered explicit by the majority of Hanafi scholars.

This lesson covers the category of kinaya (implicit) expressions for emancipation and the debated status of expressions like 'I gifted you yourself'. The text presents three views, with the relied-upon position being that such expressions are truly explicit. Understanding this is crucial for determining when emancipation occurs without explicit intention.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the kinaya expressions listed in the text
Kinaya expressions include 'la malaka li 'alayka', 'la sabila li 'alayka', 'khallaytu sabilak', 'la haqqa li 'alayka', 'amruki biyadik', 'ikhtari'.
These expressions are ambiguous and can mean emancipation, sale, kitaba, etc.
2
Understand that kinaya requires intention (niyya)
Because the words could mean multiple things, emancipation only occurs if the master intended it. If he did not intend emancipation, the slave remains a slave.
The text explains that nafi al-milk (denial of ownership) could be due to sale or kitaba, not necessarily emancipation.
3
Study the special case of 'I gifted you yourself' and similar expressions
Expressions like 'wahaba nafsu minni' (I gifted you yourself) and 'bitu nafsu minni' (I sold you yourself) are debated: some say they are kinaya, some say they are explicit without need for intention.
The correct view (chosen by Ibn al-Humam) is that they are truly explicit.
4
Learn the three scholarly opinions
First: attached to sarih (explicit). Second: kinaya but does not need intention (contradictory). Third: truly explicit (the relied-upon view).
The text says 'wa al-haqq al-qawlu al-thalithu annaha saraiha haqiqatun'.
5
Apply the rule: court vs. religious belief
If the master claims he meant something else (e.g., he meant 'gifted his emancipation' not literal gift), he is not believed in court but is believed between him and Allah.
This applies to both sarih and kinaya when there is a departure from the apparent meaning.
