Emancipation by Claiming 'This is My Father'
Ruling on the expression 'this is my father' for emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a master says 'this is my father' to his slave and explain the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the condition of age suitability.

Core Idea
Even a biologically impossible claim of fatherhood can trigger emancipation according to Abu Hanifa, due to the metaphorical force of the declaration.

This lesson examines the legal effect of a master's declaration 'this is my father' (هذا أبي) regarding his slave. The ruling parallels the earlier case of 'this is my son', with a specific intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa holds that emancipation occurs regardless of age suitability, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) require the slave to be plausibly aged as the master's father. Understanding this nuance helps clarify when metaphorical expressions of parenthood effect freedom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
The passage presents three cases based on two factors: whether the slave is suitable (yasluhu) to be the master's father (by age), and whether the master has a known father. Identify each scenario and the consensus or dispute attached.
Suitability is determined by age comparison, not appearance.
2
Determine consensus points
When the slave is suitable and the master has no known father, both lineage and emancipation are established by consensus. When the master has a known father, lineage is never established (a person cannot have two fathers), but emancipation still occurs according to the Hanafis.
The consensus on emancipation in the suitable case shows the general rule.
3
Pinpoint the disagreement
The dispute arises when the slave is not suitable (e.g., too young). According to Abu Hanifa, emancipation still occurs. According to the Sahibayn, emancipation does not occur because the statement is factually impossible.
The ikhtilaf mirrors that of the 'this is my son' case.
4
State each position and its basis
Abu Hanifa: the metaphorical meaning (intention to free) takes precedence over literal impossibility, so the slave is freed. Sahibayn: literal impossibility renders the statement void, so no emancipation. Recognise that both are acceptable within the madhhab.
Later Hanafi authorities (e.g., al-Marghinani) prefer Abu Hanifa's view.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position for fatwa is that of Abu Hanifa: emancipation occurs even if the slave is not suitable. This is the operative ruling in the school.
This is consistent with the principle that declarations of freedom are interpreted broadly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثاني وهو قوله هذا أبي فإن كان يصلح أباً له وليس للقائل أب معروف يثبت النسب والعتق بلا خلاف، وإن كان يصلح أباً له ولكن للقائل أب معروف لا يثبت النسب ويعتق عندنا، وإن كان لا يصلح أباً له لا يثبت النسب بلا شك ولكن يعتق عند أبي حنيفة وعندهما لا يعتق.
As for the second, which is his saying 'this is my father': if he is suitable to be his father and the speaker has no known father, lineage and emancipation are established without dispute. If he is suitable but the speaker has a known father, lineage is not established but he is freed according to us (Hanafis). If he is not suitable to be his father, lineage is not established without doubt, but he is freed according to Abu Hanifa, while according to the two (Sahibayn) he is not freed.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling for a master saying 'this is my father' (هذا أبي) to his slave. The principles mirror those for 'this is my son'. If the slave could biologically be the master's father (i.e., age appropriate) and the master has no known father, both lineage and emancipation are established by consensus. If the master has a known father, lineage is not established (since a person cannot have two fathers), but emancipation still occurs according to the Hanafi school. If the slave is not suitable to be the master's father (e.g., too young), then lineage is clearly not established, but Abu Hanifa holds that the slave is freed anyway, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that he is not freed. This mirrors the ikhtilaf seen in the previous lesson.

Key Terms
أب (ab): Father; in this context, the master claiming the slave as his father.
يصلح أباً له (yasluhu abban lahu): He is suitable to be his father, i.e., age and biological plausibility.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a master says 'this is my father' to a young slave who is clearly too young to be his father, and the master has a known father, what is the ruling according to Abu Hanifa?
A) The slave is freed and lineage is established.
B) The slave is not freed because lineage is impossible.
C) The slave is freed but lineage is not established.
D) The slave is not freed, and lineage is not established.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the metaphorical declaration 'this is my father' can effect emancipation even if biologically impossible, following Abu Hanifa's view.

Reversed Gender Kinship: 'My Daughter' to a Male Slave
Ruling on mismatched gender expressions of kinship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the preponderant Hanafi ruling when a master uses a mismatched gender kinship term for a slave, identify the two positions in the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf, and explain the tarjih.

Core Idea
A mismatched gender expression of kinship is metaphorical; emancipation is the preponderant view.

When a master says 'this is my daughter' to a male slave, or 'this is my son' to a female slave, the literal gender mismatch creates an ikhtilaf among Hanafi scholars. Some hold the statement void, while others interpret it metaphorically to effect emancipation. This lesson unpacks the dispute and the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal act and the ikhtilaf
The act is a master's statement of kinship to a slave, with a gender mismatch. The ikhtilaf arises: does emancipation occur or not?
2
State the two positions
Position 1: Some mashayikh say the slave is not freed because the literal gender mismatch makes the expression void. Position 2: Others say the slave is freed, interpreting 'daughter' or 'son' metaphorically.
3
Identify the preponderant view and its authority
The preponderant view (mufta bihi) is emancipation. This is adopted in al-Hidayah, Fath al-Qadir, and al-Mujtaba as the more apparent position.
These are major later Hanafi references.
4
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under the preponderant view, the slave becomes free; under the minority view, he remains a slave.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال لعبده هذه بنتي أو قال لأمته هذا ابني اختلف المشايخ فيه قال بعضهم يعتق، وقال بعضهم لا يعتق، ورجحه في الهداية وفتح القدير، وفي المجتبى وهو الأظهر.
And if he said to his male slave 'this is my daughter' or said to his female slave 'this is my son', the mashayikh differed. Some said he becomes free, some said he does not. In al-Hidayah and Fath al-Qadir it was preponderated (that he is freed), and in al-Mujtaba it is the more apparent.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage addresses cases where the gender of the kinship term does not match the slave's gender. For example, calling a male slave 'my daughter' or a female slave 'my son'. The earlier masters (mashayikh) differed: some held that emancipation occurs (interpreting the expression metaphorically), while others did not. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that emancipation does occur, as preponderated in al-Hidayah and Fath al-Qadir, and stated as the more apparent view in al-Mujtaba.

Key Terms
بنت (bint): Daughter; when used for a male slave, it is a mismatched expression.
ابن (ibn): Son; when used for a female slave, mismatched.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The slave is not freed because the gender does not match.
B) The slave is freed only if the master intended emancipation.
C) The slave is freed because the expression is metaphorical.
D) The slave is freed only if he is also of unknown lineage.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a master's mismatched gender kinship expression to a slave effects emancipation, per the preponderant view, as the statement is interpreted metaphorically.

Emancipation by Calling a Slave 'Uncle'
Ruling on emancipation via kin terms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a master addresses his slave as 'paternal uncle' or 'maternal uncle' and explain that it causes emancipation by consensus.

Core Idea
Calling a slave 'uncle' frees him instantly – no Hanafi disagreement.

This short passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq declares a clear-cut ruling: if a master says to his slave 'this is my paternal uncle' or 'this is my maternal uncle', the slave is set free. There is no ikhtilaf among the Hanafi imams. The text also promises a later discussion on the phrase 'this is my brother'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal effect
The act is the master's utterance of a kin term ('uncle') to his slave. The legal effect is immediate emancipation of the slave.
The ruling is not dependent on the master's intent – the statement itself causes freedom.
2
Recognize the consensus
The text explicitly says there is no disagreement among the Hanafi companions (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani). This makes the ruling firm and binding.
This is a rare case of complete intra-madhhab agreement on a verbal emancipation.
3
Differentiate from other kin terms
The passage notes that the ruling for 'brother' will be discussed later, implying that different kin terms may have different rulings.
Pay attention to the structure: the author separates this ruling from the upcoming one on 'brother'.

Source Text

And if he said to his slave 'this is my paternal uncle' or 'maternal uncle', he becomes free without disagreement among our companions. And the discussion on 'this is my brother' will come at the end of the chapter.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage states that if a master calls his slave 'my paternal uncle' (عمي) or 'my maternal uncle' (خالي), the slave is freed by consensus among the Hanafis. There is no ikhtilaf on this point. The text also notes that the discussion on 'my brother' will come later (at the end of the chapter). The ruling for uncle is considered clear because the relationship implies a familial connection that necessitates freedom, and there is no dispute that such an expression is effective metaphorically.

خال (khāl): Maternal uncle.

Ruling on Claiming a Slave as Son from Adultery
Ruling on claiming a slave as son from adultery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a master claims a slave as his son from adultery and explain why lineage is not established.

Core Idea
A master's acknowledgment of a slave as his son from adultery frees the slave but does not establish legal paternity.

In Hanafi fiqh, a master's acknowledgment of paternity over a slave triggers certain legal effects. The key is that the acknowledgment itself, when made unconditionally, establishes both freedom and lineage. However, when the master explicitly qualifies the claim with 'from adultery' (zinā), the ruling bifurcates: the slave is freed, but lineage is blocked because Islamic law does not attribute a child of zinā to the father. This lesson dissects this precise ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
The act is the master's statement: 'This is my son from adultery.' The primary ruling is that the slave becomes free (emancipation).
This is an immediate legal consequence, not contingent on investigation.
2
Separate the Two Effects
The statement has two potential effects: emancipation and lineage (nasab). The text declares lineage is not established.
Emancipation is wajib (obligatory) upon the master once the statement is made.
3
Understand the Rationale
The phrase 'from zinā' (adultery) is the decisive factor. In Islamic law, a child conceived in zinā is not attributed to the biological father for purposes of lineage, inheritance, or guardianship.
The acknowledgment triggers emancipation because the master voluntarily claims the slave as his son, but the qualification negates lineage.
4
Apply to a Similar Case
If the master says 'you are my son' without any qualification, both emancipation and lineage are established because there is no impediment.
The presence of 'from zinā' is the only difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال هذا ابني من الزنا يعتق ولا يثبت النسب
And if he said 'this is my son from adultery', he becomes free and lineage is not established.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This ruling covers the case where a master explicitly says 'this is my son from adultery' (هذا ابني من الزنا). The slave is freed because the master acknowledges him as his son, which is a cause for emancipation. However, lineage is not established due to the explicit mention of adultery, which negates paternity in Islamic law (the child of adultery is not attributed to the father). Thus, the slave gains freedom but does not become the master's legitimate son.

Key Terms
زنا (zinā): Adultery; here it qualifies the paternity claim, negating lineage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The slave becomes free and inherits from the master.
B) The slave becomes free but does not become a legitimate son.
C) The slave does not become free because the paternity is invalid.
D) The slave becomes free only if the master retracts the adultery claim.

Key Takeaway
A master's acknowledgment of a slave as his son, even when qualified by adultery, obligates emancipation but does not create a legal father-son relationship.

Ruling on the Necessity of the Slave's Confirmation for Lineage and Emancipation Claims
Discussion on the necessity of the slave's confirmation for these declarations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the slave's confirmation requirement and explain the intra-madhhab difference between claims of sonship and other lineage claims.

Core Idea
The slave's confirmation is not required for a master's claim of sonship, but some Hanafi scholars require it for other lineage claims.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq discusses a subtle point in the master-slave relationship: when a master declares that his slave is his son or relative, does the slave need to confirm that statement for it to take legal effect? The matn suggests that confirmation is not a condition, but later Hanafi scholars differed, especially for claims other than sonship. Understanding this ikhtilāf is key to grasping how Hanafi jurists balance the master’s authority with the slave’s rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Default Ruling
The master makes an admission (iqrār) regarding his slave, such as claiming the slave is his son. The default ruling from the matn is that the slave’s confirmation is not required for the admission to be valid.
The master's authority over his property (the slave) generally makes his iqrār binding without the slave's consent.
2
Recognize the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilāf
Some Hanafi scholars hold that confirmation is not needed for any lineage claim. Others restrict this to claims of sonship (بنوة) and require confirmation for other relationships (e.g., fatherhood, brotherhood).
The dispute hinges on whether the admission imposes freedom on the slave and affects third parties (lineage).
3
Understand the Reasoning for Each View
First view (no confirmation needed): The master’s iqrār is valid because he is the owner; the slave has no standing to object. Second view (confirmation needed for non-sonship): Attributing a non-sonship relationship imposes freedom and links the slave’s lineage to another free person, which affects the slave’s status and requires his assent.
The second view treats non-sonship claims as more serious because they imply a familial tie to someone other than the master.
4
Determine the Muftā Bihī
The relied-upon position (muftā bihī) is that confirmation is not required for sonship, but for other claims, the stronger view is that confirmation is not required either, though caution may dictate requiring it.
Some later authorities (fatawā muta’akhkhirīn) prefer the more permissive view to preserve the master’s authority.
5
Apply the Ruling to a Scenario
Practice by considering: A master says, 'This slave is my brother.' According to the first view, the slave becomes free and inherits. According to the second view, the master’s statement alone is insufficient unless the slave confirms.
The practical consequence (thamarah) is whether the slave is freed immediately or only after confirmation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأشار المصنف إلى أنه لا يشترط تصديق العبد المقر له بالنسب وفيه اختلاف؛ فقيل لا يحتاج إلى تصديقه لأن إقرار المالك على مملوكه يصح من غير تصديقه، وقيل يشترط تصديقه فيما سوى دعوى البنوة لأن فيه حمل النسب على الغير فيكون فيه إلزام العبد الحرية فيشترط تصديقه.
The author indicated that the confirmation of the slave to whom lineage is attributed is not required, but there is difference. Some said: he does not need his confirmation because the master's admission regarding his slave is valid without the slave's confirmation. Some said: his confirmation is required in matters other than the claim of sonship, because it involves attributing lineage to another, and thus it imposes freedom on the slave, so his confirmation is required.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage addresses whether the slave's confirmation (تصديق) is necessary for the master's statement to take effect. The matn (from Kanz al-Daqaiq) indicates that confirmation is not a condition. However, there is ikhtilaf: one view holds that since the master has full authority over his slave (as owner), his admission is valid without confirmation. Another view holds that for claims other than sonship (i.e., fatherhood, motherhood, etc.), confirmation is required because these claims impose freedom on the slave and involve external lineage. The default ruling is that for sonship, confirmation is not needed; for other relations, the more cautious view requires confirmation.

Key Terms
تصديق (taṣdīq): Confirmation by the slave that the master's statement is true.
إقرار (iqrār): Admission or declaration by the master.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to one view, for which type of claim is the slave's confirmation required?
A) For the claim of sonship only.
B) For all claims of lineage.
C) For claims other than sonship.
D) For claims that do not involve freedom.

Key Takeaway
The slave's confirmation is not a general condition; it is waived for sonship but debated for other lineage claims, with the relied-upon position being that it is not required.

The Ruling of 'This Is My Grandfather' for Emancipation
Intra-Hanafi dispute on 'grandfather' expression

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on saying 'this is my grandfather' to a slave and explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Calling a slave 'grandfather' follows the same ikhtilaf as 'son' – Abu Hanifa frees, Sahibayn do not if impossible.

This passage extends the previous discussion on expressions of kinship to a new term: 'grandfather'. The core principle is that describing a slave with a relationship that would cause emancipation upon ownership triggers freedom. However, there is an exception for 'brother' and 'sister', which will be addressed later. Within the Hanafi school, the same disagreement as for 'son' applies here.

Guided Path
1
Identify the expression and the default principle
The master says 'this is my grandfather' to a young slave. The general principle from earlier discussions is that if a master describes a slave with a relationship that would cause the slave to be freed upon acquisition (e.g., father, son), then the slave is freed.
This principle is established in the school but has exceptions.
2
Recognize the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
As with 'son' and 'father', there is a disagreement: Abu Hanifa holds that the slave is freed unconditionally. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that if the statement is linguistically impossible (e.g., a young master calling a young slave 'grandfather'), the slave is not freed.
The text says the most correct view is that it follows this ikhtilaf.
3
Note the exception for 'brother' and 'sister'
The passage mentions that the principle does not apply to 'this is my brother' or 'this is my sister' – those will be covered at the end of the chapter. This shows that not all kinship terms trigger emancipation.
4
Apply the mufta bihi position
According to the text, the most correct view (al-asahh) is that the ruling follows the same ikhtilaf, and the preponderant position is Abu Hanifa's – the slave is freed.
This is the position relied upon for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال لصغير هذا جدي فقيل هو على الخلاف وهو الأصح لأنه وصفه بصفة من يعتق عليه بملكه، والأصل أنه إذا وصف العبد بصفة من يعتق عليه إذا ملكه فإنه يعتق عليه إلا في قوله هذا أخي وهذه أختي
And if he said to a young (slave) 'this is my grandfather', it is said that it is subject to the same disagreement (as before), and this is the most correct view, because he described him with a description of one who is freed upon him by ownership. The principle is that if he describes a slave with a description that would cause emancipation if he owned him, then he is freed, except for his saying 'this is my brother' and 'this is my sister'.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This ruling covers the expression 'this is my grandfather' (هذا جدي) addressed to a young slave. The view is that it follows the same ikhtilaf as the previous cases (e.g., 'this is my son'): Abu Hanifa frees, the Sahibayn do not if the statement is impossible. The text asserts that this is the most correct view. The underlying principle is that if a master describes a slave with a relationship that would cause emancipation upon acquisition (i.e., if the slave were a free relative), then the statement effects emancipation. However, this principle has an exception for 'this is my brother' and 'this is my sister', which will be discussed later (the text refers to 'end of the chapter').

Key Terms
جد (jadd): Grandfather; here as a description that would imply emancipation if the slave were a relative.
خلاف (ikhtilaf): Disagreement; here referring to the intra-Hanafi difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.
الأصح (al-asahh): The most correct/sound view. Used to indicate the preponderant position.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the most correct view in the Hanafi school, if a master says 'this is my grandfather' to a young slave, what is the ruling?
A) The slave is always freed without dispute.
B) The ruling follows the same ikhtilaf as for 'son'.
C) The slave is not freed because it is impossible.
D) The slave is freed only if the master is younger than the slave.

Key Takeaway
The expression 'this is my grandfather' triggers the same intra-Hanafi dispute as 'son' and 'father', and the mufta bihi is that the slave is freed, following Abu Hanifa.

Ruling on the expression 'mawla' for emancipation
Detailed analysis of the term 'mawla' and its effect on emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the term 'mawla' is considered explicit for emancipation in the master-slave context and apply the ruling to determine when emancipation occurs.

Core Idea
When a master says 'my mawla' to a slave, it frees him – context makes it explicit.

The Hanafi school carefully analyzes linguistic expressions to determine their legal effect. In this passage, the author explains why the word 'mawla', despite its multiple meanings, becomes an explicit declaration of emancipation when addressed to a slave. Contextual clues specify the intended meaning, making the expression equivalent to 'you are free'.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the linguistic ambiguity of 'mawla'
The term 'mawla' has several possible meanings: helper, cousin, co-religionist, manumitter, and freed slave. Understanding this range is crucial.
2
Identify the contextual factors that specify the meaning
Examine the context: the master speaking to his own slave. This rules out the meanings of helper (master does not seek help from slave) and cousin (slave's lineage is known), and the religious sense is metaphorical. The possessive idafa to the slave contradicts the master being the manumitter, so the only remaining meaning is the freed slave.
These factors are derived from common practice and linguistic usage.
3
Apply the rule of contextual specification
When a word has multiple meanings but the context specifies one, it is treated as explicit (sarih) for that meaning. Here, the specified meaning is emancipation, so the utterance is like 'you are free'.
This is based on the usul principle that speech is interpreted literally (haqiqah) unless there is a qarinah (contextual clue).
4
Consider the effect on emancipation without intention
Since the expression is explicit, it effects emancipation even if the master did not intend it. The default is autonomy.
If the master claims he intended something else, he is not believed judicially because the apparent meaning contradicts his claim.
5
Evaluate the scope of the ruling
The same applies to the feminine form 'mawlati' and to both report (خبر) and address (نداء). If the master intends emancipation explicitly, it is effective both judicially and religiously.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الرابع أعني لفظ المولى فذكر المصنف أنه لا فرق بين الخبر والنداء؛ أما الأول فلأن اسم المولى وإن كان ينتظم الناصر ابن العم والمولاة في الدين الأعلى والأسفل في العتاقة إلا أنه تعين الأسفل مراداً فصار كاسم خاص. وهذا لأنه المولى لا يستنصر بمملوكه عادة والعبد نسبه معروف فانتفى الأول والثاني والثالث نوع مجاز والكلام بحقيقته والإضافة إلى العبد تنافي كونه معتقاً فتعين المولى الأسفل فالتحق بالصريح. وكذا إذا قال لأمته هذه مولاتي لما بينا ولو قال عنيت به المولى في الدين أو الكذب يصدق فيما بينه الله تعالى ولا يصدق في القضاء لمخالفته الظاهر؛ كذا في الهداية. وصرح في التحفة أمراً موجوداً في نفس الأمر وهذا عند عدم النية، أما إذا نوى بهذا الكلام العتق وهو صالح له فإنه يعتق قضاء وديانة كما لا يخفى.
As for the fourth, meaning the word 'mawla', the author mentioned that there is no difference between report and address. As for the first, because the name 'mawla' includes the helper, cousin, co-religionist, the higher and lower in emancipation, but it is specified that the lower is intended, so it becomes like a specific name. This is because the master does not normally seek help from his slave, and the slave's lineage is known, so the first (helper) and second (cousin) are negated, and the third (co-religionist) is a type of metaphor, while the speech is literal, and the addition (idafa) to the slave contradicts his being a manumitter, so it is specified that it means the lower mawla (freed slave), and thus it is joined to explicit expressions. Similarly, if he said to his female slave 'this is my mawlati' (my female freed slave). And if he said 'I meant by it the mawla in religion' or a lie, he is believed between him and God but not in judicial ruling because it contradicts the apparent meaning. This is in al-Hidayah. And it is stated in al-Tuhfah that it is a matter existing in reality itself, and this is when there is no intention; but if he intended emancipation by this speech and it is suitable (for it), then he becomes free judicially and religiously, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling concerning the expression 'mawla' (مولى) when addressed to a slave. The author (of Kanz al-Daqaiq) states that there is no difference between saying 'this is my mawla' (خبر) and calling him 'ya mawla' (نداء). The term 'mawla' has multiple meanings: helper (ناصر), cousin (ابن عم), co-religionist (مولاة في الدين), and the lower (أسفل) meaning freed slave (and also the higher meaning manumitter). However, when the master says to his slave 'this is my mawla', the context specifies the lower meaning (freed slave) because: (1) a master does not normally seek help from his slave, (2) the slave's lineage is known (negating cousin), and (3) the religious sense is metaphorical. The literal meaning combined with the possessive (idafa) to the slave contradicts the master being a manumitter, so it must mean that the slave is the freed one. Therefore, it is treated as an explicit expression of emancipation. The same applies to a female slave (مولاتي). If the master claims he intended the religious sense or a lie, he is believed religiously but not judicially due to contradiction of the apparent meaning. If he did not have a specific intention, the default is emancipation. If he intends emancipation by it, it is effective both judicially and religiously.

Key Terms
مولى (mawlā): A term with multiple meanings: helper, cousin, co-religionist, manumitter, freed slave. In this context, when addressed to a slave, it means 'freed slave' and effects emancipation.
مولاة (mawlah): Feminine form of mawla; similarly means freed female slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a master says to his slave 'this is my mawla' without intending emancipation, what is the judicial ruling?
A) The slave is not freed because intention is required.
B) The slave is freed because the expression is explicit in this context.
C) The slave is freed only if the master says 'ya mawla'.
D) The slave is not freed unless the master intends the lower meaning.

Key Takeaway
When a master says 'mawla' to his slave, it is an explicit expression of emancipation, freeing the slave even without intention.

Rulings on Statements of Lineage for Emancipation and Nasab
Explains rulings when a master claims paternal or maternal lineage for a slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the legal consequences of a master's claim of lineage regarding a slave, identifying when emancipation alone occurs versus when both emancipation and nasab are established, based on conditions such as age compatibility and existence of a known father.

Core Idea
A master's statement of lineage frees the slave but establishes nasab only under strict conditions of age plausibility and absence of a known father.

The master's acknowledgment of paternity or other relationships regarding his slave triggers legal consequences that differ depending on the type of claim and the circumstances. This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies when the slave becomes free, when nasab is established, and when the two diverge, drawing on Hanafi usul principles and noting points of difference within the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of claim
Determine whether the master claims the slave as his child (son/daughter), parent, or other relative. Each category has distinct rulings.
For child claims, the default is emancipation with potential nasab; for parent claims, nasab depends on existence of a known father.
2
Check age compatibility
For paternity claims (claiming the slave as one's son), assess if the master could plausibly be the father in terms of age (mumāthalah fī al-sinn). Physical resemblance is irrelevant.
If age difference is impossible, the slave is not freed judicially (according to the preferred view).
3
Consider the existence of a known father
For the claim 'this is my father,' if the master already has a known father, nasab is not established but emancipation still occurs according to the Hanafi school.
This applies even if the claimed father could be his father in age.
4
Distinguish between emancipation and nasab
Emancipation can occur without nasab, e.g., if the claimed child does not exist in reality (judicial but not religious freedom), or if the person cannot be the father (dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn).
Nasab requires additional conditions like existence and age plausibility.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Recognize where Abu Hanifa differs from Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, especially in the father case when the person cannot be the father. The mufta bihi is the view of the Sahibayn.
For claims like 'this is my son' from a female slave, the preferred view is no emancipation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكلام في المسألة طويل في الأصول في بحث الحقيقة هل المجاز خلف عنها في التكلم أو في الحكم. وصرح في فتح القدير بأنه يعتق، نوى أم لم ينو إذ لا تزاحم كيلا يلغي كلام العاقل. ثم إن كان هذا دخل في الوجود عتق قضاء وديانة وإلا فقضاء ولا تصير ولد له اهـ. وكذا صرح في الكشف الكبير بأنه يعتق فقضاء فيما إذا كان لا يولد مثله لمثله، والمعتبر المماثلة في السن لا المشاكلة حتى لو كان المدعي أبيض ناصعاً والمقول له أسود أو على القلب يثبت النسب. وقيد بالمملوك لأنه لو قال لزوجته وهي معروفة النسب من الغير هذه ابنتي لم تقع الفرقة اتفاقاً كما عرف في الأصول. وأما الثاني وهو قوله هذا أبي فإن كان يصلح أباً له وليس للقائل أب معروف يثبت النسب والعتق بلا خلاف، وإن كان يصلح أباً له ولكن للقائل أب معروف لا يثبت النسب ويعتق عندنا، وإن كان لا يصلح أباً له لا يثبت النسب بلا شك ولكن يعتق عند أبي حنيفة وعندهما لا يعتق. وأما الثالث فهو قوله هذه أمي والكلام فيه كالكلام في الأب ولو قال لعبده هذه بنتي أو قال لأمته هذا ابني اختلف المشايخ فيه قال بعضهم يعتق، وقال بعضهم لا يعتق، ورجحه في الهداية وفتح القدير، وفي المجتبى وهو الأظهر. ولو قال لمملوكه هذا عمي أو خالي يعتق بلا خلاف بين أصحابنا وسيأتي الكلام على هذا أخي آخر الباب. ولو قال هذا ابني من الزنا يعتق ولا يثبت النسب .
The discussion on this issue is lengthy in usul regarding the topic of haqiqah: whether majaz is a substitute for it in speech or in ruling. It is stated clearly in Fath al-Qadir that he becomes free, whether he intended or not, so as not to nullify the speech of a rational person. Then if this [child] came into existence, he becomes free judicially and religiously; otherwise, only judicially, and he does not become his child. Similarly, it is stated clearly in al-Kashf al-Kabir that he becomes free judicially if someone like him is not born to someone like him; and consideration is for similarity in age, not resemblance. So even if the claimant is pure white and the person addressed is black, or vice versa, lineage is established. It is restricted to the owned [slave] because if he said to his wife, who is known to be of another's lineage, 'This is my daughter,' separation does not occur by consensus, as known in usul. As for the second, which is his saying 'This is my father': if he can be his father and the speaker has no known father, lineage and emancipation are established without dispute. If he can be his father but the speaker has a known father, lineage is not established but emancipation occurs according to us. If he cannot be his father, lineage is not established without doubt, but emancipation occurs according to Abu Hanifa, while according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) he does not become free. As for the third, which is his saying 'This is my mother,' the discussion is the same as for the father. If he said to his male slave 'This is my daughter' or said to his female slave 'This is my son,' the scholars differed: some said he becomes free, some said he does not. The preferred view in al-Hidayah and Fath al-Qadir, and in al-Mujtaba it is the more apparent, is that he does not become free. And if he said to his owned slave 'This is my uncle' or 'my maternal uncle,' he becomes free without dispute among our companions. The discussion on 'my brother' will come at the end of the chapter. And if he said 'This is my son from adultery,' he becomes free but lineage is not established.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling on the effects of a master's claim of lineage regarding a slave. It opens by referencing a usul discussion on whether figurative language (majaz) acts as a substitute for literal speech in the context of rulings. The author then cites Fath al-Qadir stating that if the master says 'this is my son' (or similar), the slave is freed whether the master intended emancipation or not, to avoid nullifying rational speech. However, if the child actually exists and is the slave, the slave is freed both judicially and religiously; otherwise, only judicially, and the slave does not become a child for inheritance purposes. The condition for judicial emancipation is that someone like the slave could be born to someone like the master, with consideration for age similarity, not physical resemblance. So even if the master is white and the slave black, lineage and freedom are established if age difference is plausible. This ruling applies only to a slave; if the master says to his wife (who is known to be another's daughter) 'this is my daughter,' divorce does not occur by consensus. Then three subcases: (1) claiming a father, (2) claiming a mother, (3) claiming uncle or nephew. For father: if the person can be his father and the master has no known father, both freedom and nasab are established; if the master has a known father, nasab is not established but freedom still occurs (according to the Hanafi school); if the person cannot be his father, nasab is not established but Abu Hanifa still holds freedom, while the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree. For mother: same as father. For a male slave claiming a daughter or female slave claiming a son: scholars differed; the preferred view is that they do not become free. For uncle: unanimous emancipation. Finally, 'this is my son from adultery' results in freedom but no lineage.

Key Terms
المماثلة في السن (al-mumāthalah fī al-sinn): Similarity in age, meaning the master could plausibly be the father of the slave in terms of age difference.
المشاكلة (al-mushākalah): Physical resemblance or similarity in appearance, which is not considered; only age compatibility matters.
يصلح أباً له (yaṣluḥu abban lahu): Being suitable to be his father, meaning the age difference is such that the speaker could be the father of the person addressed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a master says to his slave 'This is my son from adultery,' what are the consequences according to the Hanafi school?
A) The slave is emancipated and nasab is established.
B) The slave is emancipated but nasab is not established.
C) The slave is not emancipated and nasab is established.
D) The slave is not emancipated and nasab is not established.

Key Takeaway
Emancipation from a master's claim of lineage does not always establish nasab; conditions such as age compatibility and the absence of a competing father are decisive.

Slave's Verification in Lineage Claims: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on requirement of slave's verification for lineage claims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two Hanafi positions on requiring the slave's verification for different lineage claims and apply the rule to cases like claiming brotherhood versus sonship.

Core Idea
For paternity claims, the master's word suffices; for other lineage ties, the slave's verification is required—except for siblings.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq unpacks an internal Hanafi disagreement. The master's acknowledgment over his slave is generally valid without the slave's input, but lineage claims impose freedom and affect the slave's status. Two views emerge: one holding verification never needed, another requiring it unless the claim is sonship. The author then extends the discussion to descriptions like 'this is my grandfather' and notes the exception for 'brother' and 'sister'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Read the first part of the passage. Note that there is a difference of opinion (فيه اختلاف). One view says verification is not needed; the other requires it except for claims of sonship.
The first view relies on the general principle that the master's acknowledgment over his property is valid without verification.
2
Analyze the proofs (dalil) for each view
For view 1: because the master's acknowledgment over his slave is valid without verification, akin to other declarations. For view 2: because claiming lineage other than sonship attributes lineage to another and imposes freedom, thus requiring the slave's consent.
The passage uses 'لأن إقرار المالك على مملوكه يصح من غير تصديقه' for view 1, and 'لأن فيه حمل النسب على الغير فيكون فيه إلزام العبد الحرية' for view 2.
3
Apply the difference to the 'grandfather' example
The passage states that if the master says to a minor 'This is my grandfather,' the same difference of opinion applies, and this is considered the more correct view (الأصح).
Grandfather is not sonship, so under view 2, verification would be required.
4
Note the exception for 'brother' and 'sister'
The passage says the principle of emancipation upon description applies except for 'this is my brother' and 'this is my sister,' which are reserved for later discussion.
This exception indicates that describing a slave as a brother or sister does not trigger emancipation upon ownership like other relations.
5
Determine the mufta bihi (relied-upon view)
The author indicates that the more correct view (الأصح) is that the same difference applies to the grandfather example, implying that the second view (requiring verification for non-sonship) is preferred.
Thus, for fatwa today, the requirement of verification for non-sonship claims is the operative position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأشار المصنف إلى أنه لا يشترط تصديق العبد المقر له بالنسب وفيه اختلاف؛ فقيل لا يحتاج إلى تصديقه لأن إقرار المالك على مملوكه يصح من غير تصديقه، وقيل يشترط تصديقه فيما سوى دعوى البنوة لأن فيه حمل النسب على الغير فيكون فيه إلزام العبد الحرية فيشترط تصديقه. ولو قال لصغير هذا جدي فقيل هو على الخلاف وهو الأصح لأنه وصفه بصفة من يعتق عليه بملكه، والأصل أنه إذا وصف العبد بصفة من يعتق عليه إذا ملكه فإنه يعتق عليه إلا في قوله هذا أخي وهذه أختي.
The author indicated that verification of the slave to whom lineage is claimed is not required. There is a difference of opinion: it is said that verification is not needed because the master's acknowledgment regarding his slave is valid without his verification. And it is said that verification is required in other than the claim of sonship because it involves attributing lineage to another, so it imposes freedom upon the slave, thus his verification is required. And if he said to a minor 'This is my grandfather,' it is said that the same difference applies, and this is the more correct view because he described him with a description of one who would be freed upon ownership; the principle is that if he describes a slave with a description of one who would be freed upon ownership, then he is freed, except in his saying 'This is my brother' and 'This is my sister.'
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage addresses an internal Hanafi dispute on whether the slave's confirmation is a condition for the validity of the master's acknowledgment of lineage. The author (صاحب البحر الرائق) notes that the original author (صاحب الكنز) hinted that verification is not required. However, there is disagreement: the first view holds that since the master's declaration over his property is effective without the slave's input, no verification is needed. The second view distinguishes claims of sonship from others: for sonship, the slave is not required to confirm because it directly involves the master's own lineage, but for other relations like brother, uncle, etc., the slave's lineage is being attributed to the master's family, so the slave must affirm. The passage then applies this to a statement like 'this is my grandfather' to a minor slave: some say the slave must confirm (since it is not sonship), but the more correct view is that it follows the same difference of opinion. The underlying principle is that if the master describes the slave with a relation that would cause emancipation if owned (like father, son, etc.), the slave is freed except in the cases of 'this is my brother' and 'this is my sister', which are reserved for later discussion.

Key Terms
تصديق العبد (taṣdīq al-‘abd): The slave's affirmative confirmation that the master's claim of lineage is true.
إقرار المالك على مملوكه (iqrār al-mālik ‘alā mamlūkihi): The master's declaration or acknowledgment concerning his slave, which is normally valid without the slave's acceptance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Claim of being a brother.
B) Claim of being a grandfather.
C) Claim of being a son (paternity).
D) Claim of being an uncle.

Key Takeaway
The preferred Hanafi view requires the slave's verification for lineage claims other than sonship, with the exception of brother and sister reserved for special treatment.

The Word 'Mawla' as Sarih or Kinayah in Emancipation
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the classification of 'mawla' as sarih or kinayah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute over whether 'mawla' is sarih for emancipation and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Context can make a homonym explicit; 'mawla' to a slave means manumitter without intention.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explores a nuanced debate within the Hanafi school: does the word 'mawla', when used by a master addressing his slave, automatically effect emancipation, or is intention required? The discussion hinges on whether contextual clues can transform a homonym into an explicit term (sarih). We will walk through the arguments and the preferred ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
State the contested ruling: Is the word 'mawla' explicit (sarih) for emancipation or is it kinayah requiring intention?
The debate is intra-Hanafi, not between schools.
2
Present the Hanafi position and its contextual evidence
Explain why the Hanafi school holds that 'mawla' is sarih: a master does not seek support from his slave, the slave's lineage is known, and the possessive construction points to the manumitter.
These are contextual clues (qara'in) that determine the meaning.
3
Examine the opposing argument
Describe the objection from Ghayat al-Bayan: 'mawla' is a homonym (mushtarak) and cannot be sarih because its meaning is not clear.
The opponent also argues that a master can seek support from slaves.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response from Fath al-Qadir
Show how Fath al-Qadir rebuts: a homonym can become clear through context, and the speaker's later claim of a different meaning is a retraction not accepted judicially.
The context of master-slave is decisive.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and mufta bihi
State the practical difference: if sarih, emancipation occurs without intention; if not, intention is required. The relied-upon view is that it is sarih.
This is the position of al-Walwaliji and Fath al-Qadir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الرابع أعني لفظ المولى فذكر المصنف أنه لا فرق بين الخبر والنداء؛ أما الأول فلأن اسم المولى وإن كان ينتظم الناصر ابن العم والمولاة في الدين الأعلى والأسفل في العتاقة إلا أنه تعين الأسفل مراداً فصار كاسم خاص. وهذا لأنه المولى لا يستنصر بمملوكه عادة والعبد نسبه معروف فانتفى الأول والثاني والثالث نوع مجاز والكلام بحقيقته والإضافة إلى العبد تنافي كونه معتقاً فتعين المولى الأسفل فالتحق بالصريح. وكذا إذا قال لأمته هذه مولاتي لما بينا ولو قال عنيت به المولى في الدين أو الكذب يصدق فيما بينه الله تعالى ولا يصدق في القضاء لمخالفته الظاهر؛ كذا في الهداية. وصرح في التحفة أمراً موجوداً في نفس الأمر وهذا عند عدم النية، أما إذا نوى بهذا الكلام العتق وهو صالح له فإنه يعتق قضاء وديانة كما لا يخفى. بأن لفظ المولى صريح لا يحتاج إلى النية. وذكر الولوالجي اختلاف المشايخ فمنهم من قال لا يعتق بغير النية والأصح أنه صريح من كل وجه اهـ. وتعقبهم في غاية البيان بأنا لا نسلم أن المولى صريح في إيقاع العتق وهذا لأن الصريح مكشوف المراد ولفظ المولى مشترك ومع استعماله في المعاني على سبيل البدل لا يكون مكشوف المراد فلا يكون صريحاً. وقولهم إن المولى لا يستنصر بمملوكه عادة لا نسلم ذلك بل تحصل له النصرة بمماليكه وخدمه، والذي لا يحتاج إلى النصير والظهير هو الله تعالى وحده على أنا نقول : الصريح يفوق الدلالة والمتكلم يصرح وينادي بأعلى صوته أني عنيت الناصر بلفظ المولى وله دلالة على ذلك حقيقة لأنه مشترك وهم يقولون دلالة الحال من كلامك تدل على أن المراد من المولى هو المعتق الأسفل ولا تعتبر إرادة الناصر ونحوه، وهذا في غاية المكابرة اهـ . وأجاب عنه في فتح القدير بأن قوله «استعمل في معان فلا يكون مكشوف المراد» إن أراد دائماً منعناه لجواز أن ينكشف المراد من المشترك في بعض الموارد الاستعمالية لاقترانه بما ينفي غيره اقتراناً ظاهراً كما هو فيما نحن فيه ومنعه أن المولى لا يستنصر بعبده لا يلائم ما أسند به من قول تحصل النصرة بهم لأن المراد أنه إذا حزبه أمر لا يستدعي للنصرة عبده بل بني عمه وإن كان العبيد والخدم ينصرونه. وأما قوله الصريح يفوق الدلالة فكأنه أراد الكناية فطغى قلمه فنقول هذا الصريح وهو قوله أردت الناصر بلفظ المولى إنما قاله بعد قوله بما هو ملحق بالصريح في إرادة العتق فأثبت حكمه ذلك ظاهراً، وهذا الصريح بعده رجوع عنه فلا يقبله القاضي والكلام فيه ونحن نقول فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى لو أراد الناصر لم يعتق فأين المكابرة .اهـ. وأما الثاني أعني في النداء فلأنه لما تعين الأسفل مراداً التحق بالصريح وبالنداء به يعتق بأن قال يا حر يا عتيق فكذا النداء بهذا اللفظ. وقيد بالمولى لأنه لا يعتق في السيد والمالك إلا بالنية كقوله يا سيدي أو يا سيد أو يا مالكي لأنه قد يذكر على وجه التعظيم والإكرام فلا يثبت به العتق بغير نية وفي الظهيرية وغيرها: لو قال أنت مولى فلان عتق في القضاء كقوله أنت عتيق فلان بخلاف أعتقك فلان. وعن أبي القاسم الصفار أنه سئل عن رجل جاءت جاريته بسراج فوقفت بين يديه فقال لها المولى: ما أصنع بالسراج ووجهك أضوأ من السراج يا من أنا عبدك ؟ قال : هذه كلمة لطف لا تعتق بها الجارية. وفي التنقيح لو قال لعبده أنا عبدك المختار عدم العتق اهـ.
As for the fourth, meaning the word 'mawla', the author mentioned that there is no difference between the informative and the vocative. As for the first, because the name 'mawla' although it includes the supporter, the cousin, the ally in religion, the higher and lower in manumission, yet the lower (the manumitter) is determined as the intended meaning, so it became like a proper noun. This is because the mawla (master) does not normally seek support from his slave, and the slave's lineage is known, so the first and second [meanings] are negated; the third is a type of metaphor, and speech is upon its literal meaning; and the addition to the slave contradicts his being the manumitter, so the lower mawla (manumitter) is determined, and thus it is attached to the explicit. Similarly, if he said to his female slave 'This is my mawlāt,' based on what we said. If he said 'I meant by it the mawla in religion or a lie,' he is believed between him and Allah, but not in judicial ruling due to contradiction of the apparent; thus in al-Hidayah. And it is stated explicitly in al-Tuhfah that it is an existing matter in reality, and this is in the absence of intention. But if he intended by this speech emancipation, and it is suitable for it, then he becomes free judicially and religiously, as is not hidden. That the word 'mawla' is explicit (sarih) not needing intention. Al-Walwaliji mentioned a difference among the scholars: some say he does not become free without intention, and the more correct is that it is explicit from every aspect. They are refuted in Ghayat al-Bayan by saying: we do not accept that 'mawla' is explicit in effecting emancipation. This is because the explicit is of clear meaning, but 'mawla' is homonymic (mushtarak), and when used in multiple senses on an alternative basis, it is not of clear meaning, so it is not explicit. And their statement that the mawla does not normally seek support from his slave, we do not accept that; rather, he obtains support from his slaves and servants, and the one who does not need support and helper is Allah alone. Moreover, we say: the explicit surpasses indication, and the speaker clearly says and shouts loudly that I meant the supporter by the word 'mawla', and there is an indication for that literally because it is homonymic, and they say the contextual evidence from your speech indicates that the intended meaning of 'mawla' is the lower manumitter, and the desire for the supporter and the like is not considered. This is extreme stubbornness. And it was answered in Fath al-Qadir that his statement 'it is used in multiple senses so it is not of clear meaning' – if he meant always, we deny it, because it is possible for the meaning of a homonym to be clarified in some usage contexts due to its conjunction with something that negates other meanings, as in our case. And his denial that the mawla does not seek support from his slave does not align with what he attributed to him of the statement that support is obtained through them, because the intended meaning is that when an affair befalls him, he does not summon his slave for support but rather his cousin, even if the slaves and servants support him. And as for his statement 'the explicit surpasses indication,' it is as if he intended kinayah (allusion) and his pen slipped. So we say this explicit (his saying 'I meant the supporter with the word mawla') he said after his saying that it is attached to the explicit in intending emancipation, so he established its ruling as apparent, and this explicit after it is a retraction, so the judge does not accept it. And the discussion is in it, and we say between him and Allah, if he intended the supporter, he does not become free, so where is the stubbornness? As for the second, meaning in the vocative: because when the lower is determined as intended, it is attached to the explicit, and by calling with it, he becomes free, such as saying 'O free, O freed,' so likewise the call with this word. And it is restricted to 'mawla' because one does not become free with 'sayyid' or 'malik' except with intention, like his saying 'O my master' or 'O master' or 'O my owner' because it may be mentioned out of glorification and honor, so emancipation is not established without intention. And in al-Zahira and others: if he says 'You are the mawla of so-and-so,' he becomes free judicially, like his saying 'You are the free of so-and-so,' unlike 'I freed you from so-and-so.' And from Abu al-Qasim al-Saffar, he was asked about a man whose female servant came with a lamp and stood before him, and he said to her: 'What am I to do with the lamp when your face is brighter than the lamp, O you whose slave I am?' He said: 'This is a kind word; the servant does not become free by it.' And in al-Tanqih: if he said to his slave 'I am your slave,' the chosen view is no emancipation.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage examines the fourth expression – the word 'mawla' – and whether it is explicit (sarih) for emancipation or requires intention. The author (sahib al-Bahr al-Raiq) notes that the original text (Kanz) stated there is no difference between news and calling with this word. The reasoning is that although 'mawla' has multiple meanings (supporter, cousin, ally in religion, manumitter, etc.), the context of addressing a slave determines the lower meaning (manumitter). This is because a master does not normally seek support from his slave, the slave's lineage is known (negating 'cousin'), and the slave is not an ally in religion necessarily; hence the only plausible meaning in this context is the manumitter. Therefore, it is treated like an explicit word (sarih). However, if the master claims he meant the religious meaning or was lying, he is believed religiously but not judicially. The passage then presents a dispute: al-Walwaliji reports that some scholars say emancipation does not occur without intention, but the soundest view is that it is sarih from all aspects. The author of Ghayat al-Bayan rebuts this, arguing that a homonym cannot be explicit because its meaning is not clear. They also challenge the idea that a master does not seek support from his slave, noting that he can. They argue that the speaker's declaration that he meant 'supporter' should be accepted. Fath al-Qadir responds that contextual clues can clarify a homonym, and the master's later claim of intending 'supporter' is a retraction not accepted by the judge. The passage then turns to the vocative: calling with 'ya mawla' is also effective. However, with words like 'sayyid' or 'malik', intention is required because they can be honorific. Other examples: 'anta mawla fulan' is like 'anta atiq fulan' in effect. A story from Abu al-Qasim al-Saffar: a man said to his slave girl 'O you whose slave I am' – this is not emancipation. And in al-Tanqih: 'I am your slave' does not cause emancipation.

Key Terms
المولى الأسفل (al-mawlā al-asfal): The lower mawla, i.e., the manumitter (master) who frees the slave, as opposed to the higher mawla (the freed slave).
الصريح (al-ṣarīḥ): An explicit term that does not require intention (niyyah) to effect a ruling; here, words like 'hurr', 'atiq' are sarih. 'Mawla' is considered sarih in this context.
المشترك (al-mushtarak): A homonym – a word that has multiple distinct meanings, like 'mawla'. The debate is whether a homonym can be considered sarih when context clarifies the intended meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view in the Hanafi school, if a master says to his slave 'You are my mawla' without any intention, does the slave become free?
A) No, because 'mawla' is ambiguous.
B) Yes, because context determines it means manumitter.
C) Only if the master intended freedom.
D) Only if the slave confirms.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's preferred view is that the word 'mawla', when used by a master to his slave, is sarih for emancipation due to contextual determination, so intention is not required.

Calling a Slave 'Ya Hurr' and Similar Expressions
Ruling on calling a slave with 'O free' and similar terms, with exceptions for nicknames

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that calling a slave with explicit freedom terms causes emancipation regardless of intention, and identify the exception when the term is the slave's nickname.

Core Idea
Calling a slave 'O free' frees him without intention, unless it's his nickname.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains a key rule in Hanafi emancipation law: certain words are so explicit that they effect freedom without any need for intent. However, the exception for nicknames shows that context matters. We'll dissect the ruling and its practical applications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is calling a slave with words like 'ya hurr' (O free), 'ya atiq' (O freed), 'ya mu'tiq' (O manumitter). The ruling: the slave becomes free immediately.
2
Understand why no intention is needed
These words are sarih (explicit) because the language has placed them specifically for the meaning of freedom. In Hanafi usul, sarih words operate without regard to speaker's inner intent.
Contrast with kinayah (allusive) expressions that require intention.
3
Learn the exception for nicknames
If the master has named his slave 'Hurr' (as a proper name/nickname) and then calls him 'O Hurr', his purpose is to address him by name, not to free him. Emancipation does not occur.
The criterion: is the word used as a name or as a description?
4
Apply the exception to Persian equivalents
If the slave is nicknamed 'Hurr' but called 'ya Azad' (Persian for free), he is freed because it is not his name. Similarly, if nicknamed 'Azad' but called 'ya Hurr', he is freed because it is a description.
The language shift reveals the descriptive intent.
5
Note the condition of witnesses
Some texts (al-Zahira, al-Khaniya) require witnesses at the time of the statement to confirm that it was not a nickname scenario. The passage cuts off, indicating additional conditions.
This condition ensures the exception is not abused.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثالث النداء بحر ونحوه كيا حريا عتيق يا معتق فلأنه ناداه بما هو صريح في الدلالة على العتق لكون اللفظ موضوعاً له ولا يعتبر المعنى في الموضوعات فيثبت العتق من غير نية . واستثنى في الهداية ما إذا سماه حراً ثم ناداه يا حر لأن مراده الإعلام باسم علمه وهو لقبه به، ولو ناداه بالفارسية يا أزاد وقد لقبه بالحر قالوا يعتق وكذا عكسه لأن هذا ليس بنداء باسم علمه فيعتبر إخباراً عن الوصف .اهـ. وشرط في الظهيرية والخانية الإشهاد وقت
As for the third, calling with 'hurr' and the like, such as 'O free, O freed, O manumitter' – because he called him with what is explicit in indicating emancipation, since the word is placed for it, and meaning is not considered in words placed [for a specific meaning], so emancipation is established without intention. An exception is made in al-Hidayah for the case where he named him 'Hurr' and then called him 'O Hurr' – because his purpose was to inform of his name, which is his nickname. And if he called him in Persian 'O Azad' while he had nicknamed him 'Hurr', they said he becomes free, and likewise the reverse, because this is not a call by his name so it is considered as informing about the description. And it is conditioned in al-Zahira and al-Khaniya that there be witnesses at the time...
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage covers the third type: calling a slave with words that explicitly mean freedom, such as 'ya hurr' (O free), 'ya atiq' (O freed), 'ya mu'tiq' (O manumitter). Since these words are placed in the language specifically for the meaning of freedom, they are considered sarih (explicit) and cause emancipation without any intention. However, there is an exception: if the master had named his slave 'Hurr' (as a proper name) and then called him 'O Hurr', his purpose is to address him by his name, not to free him, so the slave does not become free. The passage also discusses calling in Persian: if the slave has the nickname 'Hurr' but the master calls him 'ya Azad' (free in Persian), he is freed because it is not a use of his proper name. Conversely, if the slave is nicknamed 'Azad' and called 'ya Hurr', he is also freed, because it is describing him with freedom. The passage ends with a mention of condition in al-Zahira and al-Khaniya that witnesses are required at the time (of the statement) – likely to establish that the statement was indeed made for emancipation, but the text cuts off.

Key Terms
النداء بحر (al-nidā' bi-hurr): Calling a slave with the word 'hurr' (free) or similar explicit terms, which results in emancipation.
اللقب (al-laqab): A nickname or title, here used as a proper name (e.g., naming a slave 'Hurr'). If the master calls him by that name, it does not cause emancipation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a master names his slave 'Hurr' and then calls him 'O Hurr', does the slave become free according to the Hanafi school?
A) Yes, because the word 'Hurr' is explicit.
B) No, because the master intended to use it as a name.
C) Yes, but only if the intention was to free.
D) No, unless witnesses are present.

Key Takeaway
Calling a slave with explicit freedom terms frees him regardless of intent, unless the term is the slave's nickname.

Manumission by Kinayah: Calling a Slave 'Uncle/Aunt' and the Dispute over 'La Sultana'
Ruling with proof and intra-Hanafi dispute on kinayah manumission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings on kinayah expressions for manumission and distinguish between 'la sultana' and 'la sabila' in terms of their legal effect.

Core Idea
Whether a phrase causes manumission depends on customary usage and whether its negation necessarily implies loss of ownership.

A master may free his slave by using indirect expressions (kinayah), but only if he intends to do so. This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains that calling a slave 'my uncle' or 'my aunt' is a clear kinayah, while 'la sultana li alayk' is disputed. We will unpack the reasoning behind the different rulings and see how the author resolves the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the kinayah expressions
First, locate the statements that the passage considers as potential manumission. These include 'my uncle', 'my aunt', 'my brother/sister' (when specified with lineage), and the disputed 'la sultana li alayk' (no authority over you). Note that the speaker is the master addressing his slave.
The phrase 'la sabila li alayk' (no means over you) is mentioned as a benchmark.
2
Understand the customary usage test
The passage from al-Bada'i' explains that 'brother' can be a term of honor, making it ambiguous, whereas 'uncle' is not used for honor by custom, so it directly implies lineage and thus manumission. This shows that Hanafi fiqh examines the common linguistic practice ('urf) to determine if a statement is a kinayah.
3
Analyze the dispute over 'la sultana'
According to al-Hidayah, 'la sultana' does not cause manumission even with intention, because 'sultan' (authority) can remain even when ownership is absent (as with a mukatib). However, later scholars and the three imams (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, etc.) hold that it is a kinayah because 'sultan' means control, and its negation can be metaphorical for manumission.
The basis of the later view is linguistic: metaphors can encompass broader meanings.
4
Evaluate the author's tarjih
The author of al-Bahr al-Raiq (Ibn Nujaym) prefers the view that 'la sultana' is a kinayah. He argues that the objection that a word cannot give more than its literal meaning is invalid, since metaphorical meanings are broader. Thus, with intention, manumission occurs. This is the mufta bihi position according to the author.
The tarjih relies on linguistic reasoning and the report from al-Kafi that 'sultan' means control.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال لأمته هذه عمتي أو هذه خالتي أو قال لغلامه هذا خالي أو عمي فإنه يعتق؛ كذا في الظهيرية وفرق بينهما في البدائع بأن الأخوة تحتمل الإكرام والنسب بخلاف العم لأنه لا يستعمل للإكرام عادة وهذا كله إذا اقتصر على هذا أخي من أبي أو من أمي أو من النسب فإنه يعتق كما في فتح القدير وغيره، ولا يخفى أنه إذا اقتصر يكون من الكنايات فيعتق بالنية، وأما عدم العتق بقوله لا سلطان لي عليك ولو نوى به العتق كما في الهداية لأن السلطان عبارة عن السيد وسمي السلطان به لقيام يده وقد يبقى الملك دون اليد كما في المكاتب بخلاف قوله لا سبيل لي عليك لأن نفيه مطلقاً بانتفاء الملك لأن للمولى على المكاتب سبيلاً فلهذا يحتمل العتق .اهـ. وفي فتح القدير واعلم أن بعض المشايخ مال إلى أنه يعتق بالنية في لا سلطان لي عليك وبه قالت الأئمة الثلاثة . وقال بعض المشايخ: إنه ليس ببعيد. وعن الكرخي فني عمري ولم يتضح لي الفرق بين نفي السلطان والسبيل ومثل هذا الإمام لا يقع له مثل هذا إلا والمحل مشكل وهو به جدير ; أما أولاً فلأن اليد المفسر بها السلطان ليس المراد بها الجارحة المحسوسة بل القدرة، فإذا قيل له سلطان أي يد يعني الاستيلاء وقد صرح في الكافي بأن السلطان يراد به الاستيلاء وإذا كان كذلك كان نفيه نفي الاستيلاء حقيقة أو مجازاً فصح أن يراد منه ما يراد بنفي السبيل بل أولى بأدنى تأمل. وأما ثانياً فلأن المانع الذي عينه من أن يراد به العتق وهو لزوم أن يثبت باللفظ أكثر مما وضع له غير مانع إذ غاية الأمر أن يكون المعنى المجازي أوسع من الحقيقي فلا بدع في ذلك بل هو ثابت في المجازات العامة فإن المعنى الحقيقي فيها. يصير فرداً من المعنى المجازي كذا هذا يصير زوال اليد من إفراد المعنى المجازي أعني العتق أو زوال الملك، والذي يقتضيه النظر كون نفي السلطان من الكنايات .اهـ.
And if he said to his slave-woman: 'This is my aunt' or 'This is my maternal aunt' or he said to his male slave: 'This is my maternal uncle' or 'my paternal uncle', then he is manumitted; thus in al-Zahiriya. And there is a difference between them in al-Bada'i' because brotherhood can be used for honor and lineage, unlike uncle because it is not used for honor by custom. All this is when he restricts to this: 'my brother from my father or from my mother or from lineage', then he is manumitted as in Fath al-Qadir and others, and it is not hidden that when he restricts, it becomes a kinayah so he is manumitted with intention. And as for the non-occurrence of manumission by his statement 'I have no authority over you' even if he intended manumission by it, as in al-Hidayah, because 'sultan' is an expression for the master, and he is called 'sultan' because of his hand being established, and ownership may remain without the hand as in the case of the mukatib, unlike his statement 'I have no means over you' because its absolute negation arises from the negation of ownership, because the master has a means over the mukatib, so this bears the possibility of manumission. End. And in Fath al-Qadir: Know that some mashayikh inclined that it does effect manumission with intention in 'I have no authority over you,' and the three Imams said this. And some mashayikh said: It is not far. And from al-Karkhi: My life passed and it did not become clear to me the difference between negation of sultan and negation of sabil. Such an Imam does not experience such except when the matter is difficult and it is worthy of it. As for first, because the 'hand' by which sultan is explained is not meant by the tangible limb but rather power, so when it is said 'his sultan' meaning 'hand' i.e., control, and it was stated in al-Kafi that sultan means control, and if so, its negation is negation of control, literally or metaphorically, so it is valid to intend from it what is intended from negation of sabil, indeed more so with minimal reflection. As for second, because the impediment which he specified from intending manumission by it – that it would necessitate establishing more than what it was placed for – is not an impediment, since the utmost is that the metaphorical meaning is broader than the literal, there is no novelty in that; rather it is established in general metaphors, for the literal meaning becomes an individual of the metaphorical meaning. So here, the removal of the hand becomes an individual of the metaphorical meaning, namely manumission or removal of ownership. And what due consideration requires is that negation of sultan is a kinayah. End.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof discussing whether specific statements by a master to his slave cause manumission. The author begins by stating that if a master calls his slave-woman 'my aunt' or his male slave 'my uncle', the slave is manumitted. This ruling is from al-Zahiriya. The author then cites al-Bada'i' which differentiates: calling a slave 'brother' can be a term of honor or lineage, so it is ambiguous, whereas 'uncle' is not customarily used for honor, so it directly implies lineage, thus manumission occurs. If the master specifies 'my brother from my father' etc., it is a kinayah (indirect expression) requiring intention. The discussion then turns to the statements 'la sultana li alayk' (I have no authority over you) and 'la sabila li alayk' (I have no means over you). The ruling from al-Hidayah is that 'la sultana' does not effect manumission even with intention, because 'sultan' refers to the master's control, which can remain even if ownership is absent (as with a mukatib slave). In contrast, 'la sabila' negates ownership absolutely, so it can intend manumission. However, Fath al-Qadir reports that some scholars (including the other three imams) hold that 'la sultana' does effect manumission with intention. The author then explains the difficulty of distinguishing between the two phrases. The first argument is that 'sultan' means control, not a physical hand, so its negation is negation of control, which can be metaphorical for manumission. The second argument is that the objection that the word would have to mean more than its literal meaning is invalid because metaphorical meanings are broader. The author concludes that the correct view is that 'la sultana' is a kinayah, meaning it can effect manumission with intention. Throughout, the Hanafi position is clear: these statements are kinayat that require intention for manumission, and the text presents a dispute within the school and a preference for the view that 'la sultana' is also a kinayah.

Key Terms
كناية (kinayah): An indirect expression that requires intention (niyyah) for its legal effect; not as clear as a direct (sarih) expression.
سلطان (sultan): Authority or control, often referring to the master's power over a slave.
سبيل (sabil): Means or pathway; in this legal context, it refers to the master's legal right over the slave, including ownership.
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a contract of manumission (kitabah) to earn freedom by paying installments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author's preferred view in al-Bahr al-Raiq, if a master says to his slave 'la sultana li alayk' intending manumission, what is the ruling?
A) The slave does not become free because 'sultan' cannot mean ownership.
B) The slave becomes free because it is a kinayah requiring intention.
C) The slave becomes free only if the master also says 'la sabila li alayk'.
D) The slave becomes free automatically without intention.

Key Takeaway
The ruling on kinayah for manumission turns on customary usage and the possibility of metaphorical extension; 'la sultana' is a kinayah in the preferred Hanafi view.
