Divorce Words Cannot Effect Manumission – The Hanafi Position and Exceptions
Explaining the rule and its proof, with exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that divorce words do not effect manumission, explain the underlying reasoning of strength (quwwa), and identify the two exceptional phrases that can work with intention.

Core Idea
A word can only be used metaphorically for something weaker; manumission is stronger than divorce, so divorce words cannot free a slave.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains a key Hanafi principle: the hierarchy of legal effects. Divorce (talaq) removes a restraint from a free woman, while manumission ('itq) gives life to a slave who is likened to an inanimate object. Therefore, manumission is stronger. A word used metaphorically can only apply to something less strong, not more strong. Thus, divorce words cannot be used to free a slave, even with intention. However, there are two exceptions: the phrases 'amruk bi yadik' (your affair is in your hand) and 'ikhtari' (choose), which are kinayah for both divorce and manumission, so with intention they can effect manumission.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi rule
Identify the general ruling: divorce words (both sarih and kinayah) do not effect manumission, even if the speaker intends manumission. This is the relied-upon position of the madhhab.
The exception: 'amruk bi yadik' and 'ikhtari' are excluded.
2
Analyze the proof (ta'lil)
Understand the reasoning: manumission establishes power (quwwa) for the slave, who is like an inanimate object; divorce removes a restraint from an already capable free woman. Manumission is stronger. A word can be used metaphorically for something weaker, not stronger. Hence, words of divorce (weaker) cannot denote manumission (stronger). Additionally, the right of possession (milk yamin) is above the right of marriage (milk nikah), so its removal is stronger.
This is qiyas based on the hierarchy of legal effects.
3
Note the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf
There is a weak narration from Abu Yusuf that saying 'tallaqtuka' to a slave-woman with intention of manumission does free her. However, the madhhab rejects this because the word is not semantically suitable.
The mufta bihi is the general rule.
4
Apply the exception
The phrases 'amruk bi yadik' and 'ikhtari' are kinayah; they can mean either divorce or manumission. With clear intention of manumission, they effect manumission immediately.
This exception is from al-Bada'i' and other sources.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما عدم الوقوع بألفاظ الطلاق ولو نوى العتق فهذا مذهبنا إلا رواية عن أبي يوسف أنه يقع بقوله لأمته طلقتك ناوياً العتق كما في المجتبى. وجه المذهب أنه نوى ما لا يحتمله لفظه لأن الإعتاق لغة إثبات القوة والطلاق رفع القيد وهذا لأن العبد ألحق بالجمادات وبالإعتاق يحيى فيقدر ولا كذلك المنكوحة فإنها قادرة إلا أن قيد النكاح مانع وبالطلاق يرتفع المانع فتظهر القوة، ولا خفاء أن الأول أقوى ولأن ملك اليمين فوق ملك النكاح فكان إسقاطه أقوى، واللفظ يصلح مجازاً في غيره دون حقيقته لا عما هو هو فوقه فلهذا امتنع في المتنازع فيه وانساغ في عكسه؛ كذا ما عن في الهداية. وحاصله أنه يستعار ألفاظ العتق للطلاق دون عكسه بناء على ما في الأصول من جواز استعارة السبب للمسبب دون عكسه إلا أن يختص المسبب بالسبب فكالمعلول فيصح استعارة كل منهما للآخر. أطلقه فشمل صريح الطلاق وكناياته فلا يقع بها العتق أصلاً فلو قال لأمته فرجك علي حرام أو أنت علي حرام فإنها لا تعتق وإن نواه لأن اللفظ غير صالح له فهو كما لو قال لها قومي واقعدي ناوياً للعتق لأن اللفظ لما لم يصلح له لغا فبقي مجرد النية وهي لا يقع بها شيء وسيأتي في الإيمان أنه إن وطئها لزمه كفارة اليمين فليحفظ هذا. ويستثنى من كنايات الطلاق أمرك بيدك أو اختاري فإنه يقع العتق به بالنية لأنه لما احتمل العتق وغيره كان كناية فهو من كنايات العتق والطلاق ولا بدع فيه كما في البدائع.
And as for the non-occurrence by divorce words even if he intended manumission, this is our madhhab except a narration from Abu Yusuf that it does occur by his saying to his slave-woman 'I have divorced you' intending manumission, as in al-Mujtaba. The basis of the madhhab is that he intended something that his word does not bear, because manumission linguistically is establishing power, and divorce is removing a restraint. This is because the slave is likened to inanimate objects, and by manumission he is revived and becomes capable, not so the married woman, for she is capable except that the restraint of marriage prevents, and by divorce the prevention is removed so the power becomes apparent. There is no doubt that the former is stronger, and because the right of possession (right hand) is above the right of marriage, so its removal is stronger. And a word can be used metaphorically for something other than its literal meaning, not for what is above it, so this is why it is prevented in the disputed case and permissible in the opposite. Such is quoted in al-Hidayah. The summary is that words of manumission are borrowed for divorce, not vice versa, based on the principle in usul of the permissibility of borrowing the cause for the effect, not the opposite, unless the effect is specific to the cause, then it is like the effect, so it is permissible to borrow each for the other. He [the author of Kanz] made it general so it includes explicit divorce and its kinayat, so manumission does not occur by them at all. So if he said to his slave-woman 'Your vagina is forbidden to me' or 'You are forbidden to me', she is not manumitted even if he intended it, because the word is not suitable for it, like if he said to her 'Stand up' and 'Sit down' intending manumission, because when the word is not suitable for it it is futile and mere intention remains, and nothing occurs by it. It will come in al-Iman that if he has intercourse with her, the expiation of an oath becomes obligatory upon him, so preserve this. And there is an exception from the kinayat of divorce: 'Amruk bi yadik' or 'Ikhtari' – manumission does occur by them with intention, because when they bear the possibility of manumission and other things, they are kinayah, so they are from the kinayat of manumission and divorce, and there is no novelty in that, as in al-Bada'i'.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof explaining the Hanafi position that divorce words (both sarih and kinayah) cannot be used to effect manumission, even with intention. There is a weak narration from Abu Yusuf that 'tallaqtuka' said to a slave-woman with manumission intention does cause freedom, but the madhhab rejects this. The reason given is that manumission establishes power (giving life to a slave), while divorce removes a restraint (from marriage). The slave is like an inanimate object, manumission brings him to life; a married woman is already capable, but marriage restricts her. Thus, manumission is stronger. Additionally, the right of possession (milk yamin) is superior to the right of marriage (milk nikah), so its removal is stronger. A word can be used metaphorically for something less strong, not for something stronger. Therefore, words of manumission (stronger) can be used for divorce (weaker), but not vice versa. The author then notes an exception: the phrases 'amruk bi yadik' (your affair is in your hand) and 'ikhtari' (choose) – when said to a slave-woman with intention of manumission, they do cause manumission because these phrases can mean either manumission or divorce, making them kinayah for both. This exception is recorded in al-Bada'i'. The passage also mentions that if the master says 'your vagina is forbidden to me' or 'you are forbidden to me' intending manumission, it does not work; but if he then has intercourse, he incurs the kaffarah of an oath (as will come in the book of oaths).

Key Terms
طلاق (talaq): Divorce; the dissolution of a marriage contract, which removes the restraint of marriage.
عتق ('itq): Manumission; freeing a slave, which establishes the slave's capability (quwwa) and removes the master's ownership.
كناية (kinayah): An indirect expression that requires intention for its meaning; used both in divorce and manumission contexts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, if a master says to his slave-woman 'tallaqtuka' (I have divorced you) intending her manumission, what is the result?
A) She becomes free because the intention matters.
B) She becomes free only if she accepts.
C) She does not become free because divorce words are not suitable for manumission.
D) She becomes free only if the word 'tallaqtuka' is said in a divorce context.

Key Takeaway
Divorce words cannot free a slave because manumission is stronger than divorce, and metaphor only works downward; the exceptions are 'amruk bi yadik' and 'ikhtari', which are kinayah for both and can effect manumission with intention.

Distinguishing Expressions for Manumission: The Rulings on 'Ikhtari' and Other Kinayat
Clarifying the relied-upon Hanafi positions on four manumission kinayat and resolving a contradiction on 'ikhtari'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi rulings on four manumission kinayat and apply the principle that later opinions must yield to the foundational texts (al-Asl, al-Kafi).

Core Idea
Not all kinayat are equal: 'ikhtari' does not effect manumission even with intention, unlike 'amruk bi yadik'.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq resolves a seeming contradiction in the Hanafi school regarding whether the phrase 'ikhtari' (choose) can cause manumission. The author clarifies the relied-upon position by distinguishing between 'amruk bi yadik' (your affair is in your hand) and 'ikhtari', and explains why other expressions like 'atlaqtuka' and 'anta mithla al-hurr' require intention. Understanding these distinctions is essential for correctly applying manumission rulings in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four expressions and the central question
List the four phrases discussed: 'atlaqtuka', 'anta mithla al-hurr', 'amruk bi yadik', and 'ikhtari'. The central question is whether 'ikhtari' with intention of manumission frees the slave, given that it is a kinayah for divorce.
The passage initially suggests a contradiction because some earlier statements seem to equate 'ikhtari' with 'amruk bi yadik'.
2
Understand why 'atlaqtuka' works as a manumission kinayah
Analyze the author's reasoning: although 'atlaqtuka' is a kinayah for divorce, it is also a kinayah for manumission because it means 'I have freed you' (like 'khallaytu sabilak'). The prohibition against using divorce words for manumission applies only to words that are exclusively divorce terms, not to words shared between both contexts.
This distinguishes it from 'tallaqtuka', which is exclusively for divorce.
3
Analyze 'anta mithla al-hurr' as a kinayah requiring intention
The phrase 'you are like a free person' establishes similarity, but similarity can be specific or general. Due to this ambiguity, manumission does not occur without intention. With intention, however, it is a valid kinayah and frees the slave.
The source cites al-Tabyin and Ghayat al-Bayan to support this.
4
Contrast 'amruk bi yadik' with 'ikhtari' in manumission
The author shows the key difference: 'amruk bi yadik' gives the slave the power to free herself in the session (the choice is in her hand). In contrast, 'ikhtari' merely gives her a choice between options, and the default choice is divorce (talaq), not manumission. Therefore, even with the master's intention, 'ikhtari' does not bring about manumission.
This is the crux of the critical refinement: the two phrases are equated in divorce but differentiated in manumission.
5
Apply the hierarchy: al-Asl and al-Kafi overrule later opinions
The author resolves the contradiction by stating that the relied-upon position (nass al-madhhab) is found in the foundational texts al-Asl and al-Kafi, which explicitly say 'ikhtari' does not cause manumission even with intention. Later sources that suggest otherwise are overruled.
This demonstrates the Hanafi method of prioritizing sources: Zahir al-Riwayah texts are authoritative over later fatwa collections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيد بألفاظ الطلاق لأنه لو قال لأمته أطلقتك أو قال لعبده ذلك يقع العتق إذا نوى كما في فتح القدير لأنه كقوله خليت سبيلك بخلاف طلقتك كما قدمناه، وكذا إذا قال له اذهب حيث شئت توجه أينما شئت من بلاد الله لا يدلي عليك لا يقع وإن نوى كما في المجتبى مع أن أطلقتك كنايات الطلاق يقع به بالنية فكيف وقع به العتق؟ والجواب أنه كناية فيهما من والممنوع استعارة ما كان ألفاظ الطلاق خاصة صريحاً أو كناية. وأما عدم العتق في قوله من أنت مثل الحر فلأنه أثبت المماثلة بينهما وهي قد تكون خاصة. وقد تكون خاصة فلا يقع بلا نية للشك؛ كذا في التبيين وهو يفيد أنه من الكنايات يقع به العتق بالنية، وقد صرح به في غاية البيان معزياً إلى التحفة حيث قال : وقد قالوا إذا نوى يعتق فإنه ذكر في كنايات الطلاق إذا قال لأمرأته أنت مثل امرأة فلان وفلان قد آلى من امرأته ونوى الإيلاء يصدق ويصير مولياً. وإنما لم يقع بدون النية لأن المثل للتشبيه التشبيه بين الشيئين لا يقتضي اشتراكهما من اختاري ينوي يصير العتق لا العتق في يدها، فقد فرق بين الأمر باليد وبين قوله اختاري في باب العتق وسوى بينهما في الطلاق .اهـ. ومثله في التتارخانية. وكذا صرح في فتح القدير بأنه لو قال لها اختاري فاختارت نفسها لا يثبت العتق وإن نواه. وكذا صرح بذلك في كافي الحاكم فما في الأصل والكافي هو نص المذهب فيقدم على ما هنا فافهم.
And it is restricted to divorce words because if he said to his slave-woman 'I have freed you' (atlaqtuka) or he said that to his male slave, manumission occurs if he intended it, as in Fath al-Qadir, because it is like his statement 'I have left your way', unlike 'I have divorced you' as we presented. Similarly, if he said to him 'Go wherever you wish, head wherever you wish from Allah's lands, no direction over you,' it does not occur even if he intended it, as in al-Mujtaba, even though 'atlaqtuka' is from the kinayat of divorce and occurs by it with intention; so how does manumission occur by it? And the answer is that it is a kinayah in both, and what is prohibited is the borrowing of words specifically of divorce, whether explicit or kinayah. And as for the non-occurrence of manumission in his statement 'You are like a free person', because he established similarity between them, and similarity may be specific or general, so it does not occur without intention due to doubt; thus in al-Tabyin, and it indicates that it is from the kinayat: manumission occurs by it with intention. And it was stated explicitly in Ghayat al-Bayan attributed to al-Tuhfa where he said: They said, if he intends, he is manumitted. Indeed, it is mentioned in the kinayat of divorce: if he said to his wife 'You are like the wife of so-and-so' and he intended ila' (oath of abstinence), he is believed and becomes a mu'lin (one who swears ila'). And it only does not occur without intention because the simile is for comparison; comparison between two things does not necessitate their sharing in... 'ikhtari intends, manumission occurs, not that manumission is in her hand.' So there is a difference between 'amruk bi yadik' and 'ikhtari' in the chapter of manumission, and he equated between them in divorce. End. And similar is in al-Tatarkhaniyya. Similarly, it is stated explicitly in Fath al-Qadir that if he said to her 'Ikhari' and she chose herself, manumission is not established even if he intended it. Similarly, it is stated explicitly in Kafi al-Hakim. So what is in al-Asl and al-Kafi is the text of the madhhab, so it is given precedence over what is here. So understand.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the earlier rulings, clarifying the relied-upon position of the madhhab. The author restricts the exception to divorce words, explaining that words like 'atlaqtuka' (I freed you) do cause manumission with intention because they are kinayah for manumission (like 'khallaytu sabilak'), unlike 'tallaqtuka' which is not. However, phrases like 'idhhab haythu shi'ta' (go wherever you wish) do not cause manumission even with intention. The question is raised: if 'atlaqtuka' is a kinayah of divorce, how can it effect manumission? The answer is that 'atlaqtuka' is a kinayah in both contexts (divorce and manumission), so the prohibition only applies to words that are exclusively divorce words (like 'tallaqtuka'). Then the author discusses 'anta mithla al-hurr' (you are like a free person): it is a kinayah and requires intention because of doubt (is the similarity specific or general?). Finally, the author addresses a contradiction regarding 'ikhtari' (choose): some sources say it works like 'amruk bi yadik' for manumission, but others (like Fath al-Qadir, Kafi al-Hakim) state that 'ikhtari' does not establish manumission even with intention, unlike 'amruk bi yadik'. The author resolves this by stating that the relied-upon position (nass al-madhhab) is from al-Asl and al-Kafi, which differentiate between the two: 'amruk bi yadik' gives the slave the power to free herself, while 'ikhtari' does not. Therefore, the ruling in those foundational texts takes precedence over later opinions.

Key Terms
أطلقتك (atlaqtuka): I have freed you; a phrase that, when said to a slave with intention, causes manumission because it is a kinayah for freeing.
أنت مثل الحر (anta mithla al-hurr): You are like a free person; a kinayah for manumission requiring intention due to ambiguity.
أمرك بيدك (amruk bi yadik): Your affair is in your hand; a phrase that, when said to a slave-woman with intention, can effect either divorce or manumission. In manumission, it gives the slave the power to free herself in the session.
اختاري (ikhtari): Choose; a phrase that, when said to a slave-woman, is a kinayah for divorce but does not effect manumission even with intention according to the relied-upon position.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position (al-Asl and al-Kafi), if a master says to his slave-woman 'ikhtari' intending manumission, what is the ruling?
A) She becomes free because it is a kinayah for manumission.
B) She does not become free because the phrase is only for divorce.
C) She becomes free only if she says 'I choose freedom'.
D) She does not become free even with intention, as per al-Asl and al-Kafi.

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position distinguishes between 'amruk bi yadik' (which grants the slave power to free herself) and 'ikhtari' (which does not, even with intention), as established by the foundational texts al-Asl and al-Kafi.

Using Divorce Expressions for Emancipation: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on using divorce kinayah for emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling that talaq wordings do not effect emancipation and identify the mufta bihi position on 'ikhtari' and 'amruki biyadik'.

Core Idea
Divorce words stay for divorce; emancipation requires its own expressions—except where custom and authority differ.

This lesson examines a key intra-Hanafi dispute about using divorce-related expressions to free a slave. While general principle prohibits using talaq wordings for emancipation, two specific expressions – 'amruki biyadik' and 'ikhtari' – have sparked debate. Early authorities allowed them with intention; later authorities confined them to divorce. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to navigate textual hierarchy in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
State the general Hanafi rule
Identify the principle that words specific to talaq (sarih or kinayah) cannot be borrowed for emancipation; this is based on the usul rule of isti'ara: the cause (talaq) cannot be borrowed for the effect (itq) unless they are closely linked.
The passage explicitly states: 'فلا يقع بها العتق أصلاً' (emancipation does not occur at all).
2
Identify the disputed expressions
Locate the exception claimed for 'amruki biyadik' and 'ikhtari'. According to al-Badai'i, these are kinayah for both divorce and emancipation, so they can effect emancipation with intention.
The author cites al-Badai'i as the source of this view.
3
Contrast the two positions
Compare the view of al-Badai'i (and al-Muhit) with that of Fath al-Qadir and Kafi al-Hakim. The latter hold that these expressions are kinayah only for divorce (tafwid), so they never cause emancipation even with intention.
Fath al-Qadir is cited: 'لو قال لها اختاري فاختارت نفسها لا يثبت العتق وإن نواه'.
4
Apply the tarjih mechanism
Determine which view is mufta bihi. The author weighs the authorities: al-Asl and al-Kafi are the text of the madhhab and take precedence over al-Badai'i. This gives the later view (Fath al-Qadir) priority.
The passage ends: 'فما في الأصل والكافي هو نص المذهب فيقدم على ما هنا'.
5
Derive the practical consequence
If a master says 'ikhtari' intending emancipation, the slave is not emancipated. If he says 'atlaqtuki' (I released you) with intention, emancipation occurs because it is kinayah for itq.
The passage distinguishes 'atlaqtuki' from 'tallaqtuki'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب العتق
دون حقيقته لا عما هو هو فوقه فلهذا امتنع في المتنازع فيه وانساغ في عكسه؛ كذا ما عن في الهداية. وحاصله أنه يستعار ألفاظ العتق للطلاق دون عكسه بناء على ما في الأصول من جواز استعارة السبب للمسبب دون عكسه إلا أن يختص المسبب بالسبب فكالمعلول فيصح استعارة كل منهما للآخر. أطلقه فشمل صريح الطلاق وكناياته فلا يقع بها العتق أصلاً فلو قال لأمته فرجك علي حرام أو أنت علي حرام فإنها لا تعتق وإن نواه لأن اللفظ غير صالح له فهو كما لو قال لها قومي واقعدي ناوياً للعتق لأن اللفظ لما لم يصلح له لغا فبقي مجرد النية وهي لا يقع بها شيء وسيأتي في الإيمان أنه إن وطئها لزمه كفارة اليمين فليحفظ هذا. ويستثنى من كنايات الطلاق أمرك بيدك أو اختاري فإنه يقع العتق به بالنية لأنه لما احتمل العتق وغيره كان كناية فهو من كنايات العتق والطلاق ولا بدع فيه كما في البدائع، وقد يقال إنهما من كنايات تفويض الطلاق فلا استثناء كما لا يخفى. وفي المحيط : لو قال لأمته أمرك بيدك وأراد العتق فأعتقت نفسها في المجلس عتقت وإلا فلا لأنه ملكها إيقاع العتق والإعتاق إسقاط الملك كالطلاق فيقتصر حكمه على المجلس كما في الطلاق، ولو قال لها أعتقي نفسك فقالت اخترت كان باطلاً كما في الطلاق .اهـ. وفي البدائع: ولو قال لها أمر عتقك بيدك أو جعلت عتقك في يدك أو قال له اختر العتق أو خيرتك في عتقك أو في العتق لا يحتاج فيه للنية لأنه صريح لكن لا بد من اختيار العبد العتق ويتوقف على المجلس لأنه تمليك اهـ. وقيد بألفاظ الطلاق لأنه لو قال لأمته أطلقتك أو قال لعبده ذلك يقع العتق إذا نوى كما في فتح القدير لأنه كقوله خليت سبيلك بخلاف طلقتك كما قدمناه، وكذا إذا قال له اذهب حيث شئت توجه أينما شئت من بلاد الله لا يدلي عليك لا يقع وإن نوى كما في المجتبى مع أن أطلقتك كنايات الطلاق يقع به بالنية فكيف وقع به العتق؟ والجواب أنه كناية فيهما من والممنوع استعارة ما كان ألفاظ الطلاق خاصة صريحاً أو كناية. وأما عدم العتق في قوله من أنت مثل الحر فلأنه أثبت المماثلة بينهما وهي قد تكون خاصة. وقد تكون خاصة فلا يقع بلا نية للشك؛ كذا في التبيين وهو يفيد أنه من الكنايات يقع به العتق بالنية، وقد صرح به في غاية البيان معزياً إلى التحفة حيث قال : وقد قالوا إذا نوى يعتق فإنه ذكر في كنايات الطلاق إذا قال لأمرأته أنت مثل امرأة فلان وفلان قد آلى من امرأته ونوى الإيلاء يصدق ويصير مولياً. وإنما لم يقع بدون النية لأن المثل للتشبيه التشبيه بين الشيئين لا يقتضي اشتراكهما من
أختاري ينوي
يصير
العتق لا العتق في يدها، فقد فرق بين الأمر باليد وبين قوله اختاري في باب العتق وسوى بينهما في الطلاق .اهـ. ومثله في التتارخانية. وكذا صرح في فتح القدير بأنه لو قال لها اختاري فاختارت نفسها لا يثبت العتق وإن نواه. وكذا صرح بذلك في كافي الحاكم فما في الأصل والكافي هو نص المذهب فيقدم على ما هنا فافهم.
Book of Emancipation... Without its reality, not about what is above it, so it is forbidden in the contested matter and permissible in its opposite, as mentioned in al-Hidayah. Its summary: the words of emancipation are borrowed for divorce and not vice versa, based on the usul principle of allowing borrowing the cause for the effect but not vice versa, unless the effect is specific to the cause, then each can be borrowed for the other. He made it general, so it includes explicit divorce and its kinayah (allusive words), so by them emancipation does not occur at all. If he says to his slave-girl 'your private part is forbidden to me' or 'you are forbidden to me,' she is not emancipated even if he intended, because the word is not suitable for it, just as if he said to her 'stand up' and 'sit down' intending emancipation, because since the word is not suitable for it, it is invalid, and mere intention remains, and nothing occurs by it. It will come in the chapter of oaths that if he has intercourse with her, he owes the expiation of the oath; so remember this. Excepted from the kinayah of divorce are 'amruki biyadik' (your affair is in your hand) or 'ikhtari' (choose), for emancipation occurs by them with intention, because since it is possible for emancipation and other things, it is kinayah, so it is from the kinayah of emancipation and divorce, and there is no oddity in that as in al-Badai'i. And it may be said that they are from the kinayah of delegation of divorce, so there is no exception, as is not hidden. In al-Muhit: If he said to his slave-girl 'amruki biyadik' and intended emancipation, and she emancipated herself in the session, she is emancipated, otherwise not, because he gave her ownership of effecting emancipation, and emancipation is a removal of ownership like divorce, so its ruling is confined to the session as in divorce. If he said to her 'a'tiqi nafsaki' (emancipate yourself) and she said 'ikhtartu' (I chose), it is invalid, as in divorce. In al-Badai'i: If he said to her 'amru itqiki biyadik' (your emancipation affair is in your hand) or 'ja'altu itqaki fi yadik' (I placed your emancipation in your hand) or said to him 'ikhtar al-itq' (choose emancipation) or 'khayyartuka fi itqika' (I gave you choice in your emancipation) or 'fi al-itq' (about emancipation) it does not need intention because it is explicit, but the slave's choosing emancipation is necessary and it is conditioned on the session because it is tamlik (giving ownership). He restricted it to the words of divorce, because if he said to his slave-girl 'atlaqtuki' or said to his slave that, emancipation occurs if he intends, as in Fath al-Qadir, because it is like his saying 'khallaytu sabilaki' (I released your way), unlike 'tallaqtuki' as we have presented. Similarly, if he said to him 'go wherever you wish' 'head wherever you wish from Allah's lands, not concealed on you' it does not occur even if he intends, as in al-Mujtaba, even though 'atlaqtuka' is from the kinayah of divorce and occurs with intention, so how does emancipation occur by it? The answer: it is kinayah in both, and what is forbidden is borrowing what were specifically words of divorce, explicit or kinayah. As for the lack of emancipation in his saying 'you are like a free person', because he established similarity between them, and it may be specific or general, so it does not occur without intention due to doubt; so in al-Tabyin, and it indicates that it is from the kinayah and emancipation occurs by it with intention, and it is explicitly stated in Ghayat al-Bayan attributed to al-Tuhfah: they said if he intends, he is emancipated. For it is mentioned in the kinayah of divorce: if he said to his wife 'you are like the wife of so-and-so' and if he had sworn from his wife and intended ila, he is believed and becomes a muli (one who takes an oath of abstention). And it does not occur without intention because 'like' is for comparison; comparison between two things does not necessitate sharing of all aspects. 'A selection intends' it becomes emancipation, not emancipation in her hand. So he distinguished between 'amr biyad' and 'ikhtari' in the chapter of emancipation, and equated them in divorce. Similarly in al-Tatarakhaniyyah. Similarly, it is explicitly stated in Fath al-Qadir that if he said to her 'ikhtari' and she chose herself, emancipation is not established even if he intended. Similarly in Kafi al-Hakim, so what is in al-Asl and al-Kafi is the text of the madhhab, so it takes precedence over what is here, so understand.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq deals with the ruling on using words from the domain of divorce (talaq) to effect emancipation (itq). The author explains a principle from usul al-fiqh: the borrowing (isti'ara) of a cause (sabab) for its effect (musabbab) is permissible, but not vice versa unless they are closely linked like a cause and its effect. Hence, words specific to talaq (like 'tallaqtuki') cannot be used for emancipation; even with intention, no emancipation occurs. However, words that are kinayah (allusive) for both talaq and itq can work for either if intended. The exception discussed is the expressions 'amruki biyadik' and 'ikhtari' — some early authorities (like al-Badai'i) held that they can be used for emancipation if intended, since they are ambiguous. But the author notes that later authorities (Fath al-Qadir, Kafi al-Hakim) treat these as kinayah of tafwid (delegation) of divorce only, so they do not cause emancipation even with intention. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Fath al-Qadir and al-Kafi: these expressions do not emancipate. The passage also covers other examples: saying 'atlaqtuki' (I released you) can work for emancipation if intended, but 'tallaqtuki' (I divorced you) cannot; saying 'go wherever you wish' does not emancipate even with intention. The discussion of 'you are like a free person' is also introduced, which is kinayah and can emancipate with intention. The author emphasizes that the madhhab's text is found in al-Asl and al-Kafi, which take precedence over other views.

Key Terms
عتق (itq): Emancipation of a slave; the legal act of freeing a slave.
كنايات الطلاق (kinayat al-talaq): Allusive expressions that imply divorce but are not explicit, such as 'you are on your own' or 'choose'.
استعارة (isti'ara): Borrowing of a word from its original domain to another domain; here, borrowing cause for effect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position (mufta bihi), if a master says to his slave-girl 'ikhtari' intending emancipation, what is the ruling?
A) She is emancipated because intention makes it valid.
B) She is not emancipated because the expression is only kinayah for divorce.
C) She is emancipated only if she chooses in the session.
D) She becomes a free person automatically regardless of intention.

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position restricts 'ikhtari' and 'amruki biyadik' to divorce; emancipation requires explicit itq expressions or specific kinayah like 'atlaqtuki'.

Emancipation by the Phrase 'Ma Anta illa Hurr' and by Owning a Mahram Relative
Ruling on two specific causes of emancipation: the phrase and owning a relative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two causes of emancipation discussed, apply the linguistic principle of 'al-istithna min al-nafi ithbat' to identify valid emancipation phrases, and identify which relative qualifies as mahram for automatic emancipation.

Core Idea
The phrase 'ma anta illa hurr' frees a slave due to exception-after-negation being emphatic affirmation; owning a mahram relative frees that relative automatically by hadith.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains two specific triggers for emancipation: a powerful verbal formula and a particular ownership relationship. The first relies on the linguistic principle that an exception after a negation constitutes a strong affirmation, as seen in the Shahada. The second is based on a hadith that makes emancipation automatic upon ownership of a mahram relative. Understanding these cases clarifies the boundary between true emancipation and mere comparison or non-emancipating ownership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two causes
The passage opens by stating that emancipation occurs by the phrase 'ma anta illa hurr' and by owning a mahram relative. Note that these are two distinct triggers, each with its own proof.
2
Analyze the linguistic principle for the phrase
The principle is 'al-istithna min al-nafi ithbat' (exception from negation is affirmation). This means that 'ma anta illa hurr' is not a comparison but a direct emancipation, akin to 'la ilaha illa Allah' affirming Allah's divinity. The Hanafi school relies on this to effect emancipation even if the owner did not intend it (though intention matters in some other cases).
Refer to the commentary and the reference to al-Hidayah and Fath al-Qadir.
3
Distinguish between description and comparison
The text gives examples: saying 'your head is a free head' (with tanwin) is a description and frees the slave; saying 'your head is the head of a free person' is comparison and does not free. Compare also 'ma anta illa mithl al-hurr' vs. 'ma anta illa hurr'.
This distinction relies on the presence of the word 'mithl' or the structure of the sentence.
4
Apply the hadith for ownership of a mahram relative
The hadith 'man malaka dha rahim mahram minhu fahuwa 'atiq 'alayhi' establishes that owning a blood relative who is perpetually forbidden in marriage (mahram) automatically frees that relative. The effective cause is both kinship and ownership, with emancipation attributed to whichever occurs last.
The hadith is a sunnah ahad but its meaning is clear (qat'i al-dalala).
5
Note exceptions and conditions
Not all relatives trigger emancipation. Only mahram relatives through blood or rada' (fosterage) that create perpetual prohibition are included. Non-mahram relatives like paternal cousins, stepmothers, etc. do not. The owner may be a child or insane; the ruling still applies.
The author mentions that the text specifies mahram due to marriage as an exception by consensus, to avoid harming owners.

Source Text

الحر وعتق بما أنت إلا حر وبملك قريب محرم ولو كان المالك صبياً أو مجنوناً وبتحرير يعني جميع الوجوه فلذلك لم يعتق لا في القضاء ولا فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى. ومعنى المثل في اللغة النظير؛ كذا في الجمهرة اهـ. وفي المحيط : لو قال ما أنت إلا مثل الحر لا يعتق، ولو قال لحرة أنت حرة مثل هذه أمته فأمته حرة، ولو قال أنت حرة مثل هذه الأمة لم تعتق أمته اهـ. وفي الظهيرية : أخذ قميصاً خاطه غلامه وقال هذه خياطة حر لا يعتق العبد لأنه : يراد به التشبيه اهـ. فقد علمت أن بعض هذه المسائل يعتق فيها بالنية وبعضها لا فلا ينبغي إدخالها في سلك واحد وفي الخانية : لو قال لعبده أنت حر يعني في النفس لم يدين في القضاء، ولو قال أنت عتيق وقال عنيت به في الملك لا يدين في القضاء، ولو قال أنت عتيق في السن لا يعتق، ولو قال أنت حر النفس يعني في الأخلاق عتق في القضاء اهـ. وفي المحيط وغيره: لو قال لعبده بدنك بدن حر ورأسك رأس حر لم يعتق لأنه تشبيه وليس بتحقيق لأنه لو أراد التحقيق لقال بدنك حر، ولو نوّن فقال رأسك رأس . حر أو بدنك بدن حر أو وجهك وجه حر عتق لأن هذا وصف له بالحرية وليس بتشبيه فصار كأنه قال رأسك حر. قوله : (وعتق بما أنت إلا حر لأن الاستثناء من النفي إثبات على وجه التأكيد كما في كلمة الشهادة كذا في الهداية. وفي فتح القدير : هذا هو الحق المفهوم من تركيب الاستثناء وهو بخلاف قول المشايخ في الأصول وقد بيناه في الأصول، وأنه لا ينافي قولهم الاستثناء تكلم بالباقي بعد الثنيا، وأما كونه إثباتاً مؤكداً فلو روده بعد النفي بخلاف الإثبات المجرد اهـ. قوله : ( وبملك قريب محرم ولو كان المالك صبياً أو مجنوناً) معطوفاً على قوله أول الباب «بأنت حر أي يصح العتق بملك قريب محرم للحديث من ملك ذا رحم محرم منه فهو أو عتق عليه واللفظ بعمومه ينتظم كل قرابة مؤبدة بالمحرمية ولاداً أو غيره، ولأنه ملك رحم قريبه قرابة مؤثرة في المحرمية فيعتق عليه وهذا هو المؤثر في قرابة الولاد. وذكر فخر الإسلام البزدوي في بحث العلل أن العلة في عتق القريب بالملك شيئان : القرابة والملك. لكن العتق يضاف إلى آخرهما فإن تأخر الملك أضيف إليه العتق كما إذا ملك قريبه، وإن تأخرت القرابة وتقدم الملك أضيف العتق إلى القرابة كما إذا كان بين اثنين عبد ثم ادعى أحدهما إنه ابنه غرم لشريكه وأضيف العتق إلى القرابة .اهـ. قيد بالقريب لأنه لو ملك محرماً بلا للقطيعة بالإجماع لما أنهم كثير لا يحصون فلو عتقوا ربما حرجوا الملاك فيه لتعذر معرفتهم بالكلية، لو خصت القرابة المحرمية عن النص أيضاً لأدى إلى تعطيله وذلك لا يجوز، وكذا لو ملك ذا رحم محرم من الرضاع فلا And emancipation occurs by 'you are nothing but free' and by owning a close relative who is mahram, even if the owner is a boy or insane, and by 'tahrir' meaning all aspects, so for that he is not emancipated neither in court nor between him and Allah. The meaning of 'like' in language is the similar; so in al-Jumhurah. In al-Muhit: If he said 'you are nothing but like a free person' he is not emancipated. If he said to a free woman 'you are free like this slave-girl of his' then his slave-girl is free. If he said 'you are free like this slave-girl' his slave-girl is not emancipated. In al-Zahiriyyah: He took a shirt sewn by his slave and said 'this is the sewing of a free man' the slave is not emancipated because it is meant for comparison. So you have learned that some of these cases involve emancipation by intention and some do not, so they should not be grouped together. In al-Khaniyyah: If he said to his slave 'you are free' meaning in soul, he is not believed in court. If he said 'you are atiq' and meant thereby in ownership, he is not believed in court. If he said 'you are atiq in age' he is not emancipated. If he said 'you are free in soul' meaning in character, he is emancipated in court. In al-Muhit and elsewhere: If he said to his slave 'your body is the body of a free person' and 'your head is the head of a free person' he is not emancipated, because it is comparison and not realization, because if he intended realization he would have said 'your body is free'. If he said with nunation 'your head is a free head' or 'your body is a free body' or 'your face is a free face' he is emancipated because this is a description of him with freedom, not comparison, so it is as if he said 'your head is free'. His saying: (And emancipation occurs by 'you are nothing but free' because the exception from negation is affirmation in the manner of emphasis as in the Kalimah of Shahadah, so in al-Hidayah. In Fath al-Qadir: This is the truth understood from the structure of exception, contrary to the saying of the mashayikh in usul, and we have explained it in usul, and it does not contradict their saying 'exception is speaking of the remainder after exclusion'. As for it being an emphasized affirmation, if it comes after negation it is different from mere affirmation. His saying: (And by owning a close relative who is mahram, even if the owner is a boy or insane) is conjoined to his earlier saying 'by 'anta hurr', i.e., emancipation validly occurs by owning a close mahram relative, due to the hadith: 'Whoever owns a dhū rahim mahram relative, he is free (in the sense that the relative becomes free) upon him.' The wording by its generality includes every perpetual relationship through birth or otherwise. And because he owns the womb of his relative, a relationship effective in mahramiyyah, so the relative becomes free upon him, and this is the effective cause in the relationship of birth. Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi mentioned in the discussion of causes that the cause for emancipation of a relative by ownership is two things: relationship and ownership. But emancipation is attributed to the later of them: if ownership comes later (as when he owns his relative after the relationship existed), emancipation is attributed to ownership; if the relationship comes later and ownership preceded, emancipation is attributed to relationship, as when a slave is owned jointly, then one of the partners claims he is his son, he pays the partner and emancipation is attributed to relationship. He restricted it to 'close relative' because if he owns a mahram without such, like a stepmother or daughter-in-law, he does not become free, because there is no blood relationship necessitating connection and forbidding severance, so he does not deserve emancipation. He restricted it to 'mahram' to exclude a blood relative without mahramiyyah, like paternal cousins, maternal uncles, aunts; if he owns them, he does not become free. Those whom the text specifies as mahram due to marriage are excluded by consensus, because they are many and cannot be counted, so if they became free, the owners would be harmed due to the difficulty of knowing them entirely. If the mahram relationship were also excluded by the text, it would lead to its nullification, which is not permissible. Similarly, if he owns a dhū rahim mahram from rada' (fosterage), then the mahramiyyah must be from the direction of
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage discusses two distinct causes of emancipation: (1) the master saying 'ma anta illa hurr' (you are nothing but free) to his slave, and (2) owning a close relative who is a mahram. The first operates on a linguistic principle: an exception after negation (like 'la ilaha illa Allah') constitutes a strong affirmation, so the phrase effects emancipation immediately. The author clarifies that adding the word 'mithl' (like) changes it to a comparison, which does not effect emancipation. Various examples illustrate the difference between descriptive statements that are taken as comparisons versus affirmations: saying 'your head is a free head' (with tanwin) is a description and so emancipation occurs; saying 'your head is the head of a free person' is comparison and does not. The second cause is based on the hadith: 'Whoever owns a dhu rahim mahram relative, that relative becomes free upon him.' This applies to any blood relative who is perpetually mahram (prohibited in marriage), such as parents, children, siblings, etc. The author explains that the effective cause is both kinship and ownership, with emancipation attributed to whichever occurs later in time. Exceptions include non-mahram relatives like paternal cousins (who are not mahram), and relatives through marriage like stepmothers (not blood relation). The discussion ends with a note about rada' (foster) mahram: if the relationship is from fosterage, it must be from a direction that establishes permanent mahramiyyah (like foster mother, foster sister). The passage emphasizes that these are well-defined cases and should not be conflated with ambiguous kinayah expressions.

Key Terms
قريب محرم (qarib mahram): A close blood relative with whom marriage is permanently forbidden, such as parent, child, sibling.
الاستثناء من النفي إثبات (al-istithna min al-nafi ithbat): A linguistic principle: an exception after a negation constitutes an affirmation of the exception, as in 'la ilaha illa Allah' (there is no god but Allah) affirms Allah's divinity.
تشبيه (tashbih): Comparison or similitude, as in saying 'like a free person' instead of 'free'. This does not effect emancipation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following phrases would effect emancipation if said to a slave?
A) 'You are nothing but free' (ma anta illa hurr)
B) 'You are nothing but like a free person' (ma anta illa mithl al-hurr)
C) 'Your body is the body of a free person' (badanuka badan hurr)
D) 'You are free in soul' (anta hurr fi al-nafs)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school recognizes two specific causes of automatic emancipation: the emphatic phrase 'ma anta illa hurr' and ownership of a mahram relative, each supported by distinct evidence.

Manumission by 'You Are Nothing but Free': The Linguistic Principle of Istithna
Ruling with proof: effect of istithna after nafy on manumission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that 'ma anta illa hurr' causes manumission and explain the linguistic principle that exception after negation yields emphatic affirmation.

Core Idea
ما أنت إلا حر = direct manumission; istithna after nafy gives emphatic affirmation.

The source passage opens by affirming that manumission occurs through the phrase 'ما أنت إلا حر'. This ruling is grounded in a linguistic principle: exception (istithna) from negation (nafy) results in affirmation with emphasis, akin to the kalimah of testimony. In this lesson, we trace the dalil, note the internal Hanafi consensus, and contrast it with a differing opinion among usuli scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Phrase and Its Effect
The matn states: 'وعتق بما أنت إلا حر'. Recognise that this phrase is a direct declaration of freedom.
2
Analyse the Linguistic Structure
The phrase combines negation (ما) with exception (إلا). In Arabic grammar, this structure affirms the exception emphatically.
3
Apply the Hanafi Usul Principle
The principle: 'الاستثناء من النفي إثبات على وجه التأكيد'. This means that the exception is affirmed with emphasis.
This is similar to the shahada: لا إله إلا الله.
4
Confirm the Proof from the Text
The author cites al-Hidaya and Fath al-Qadir as relying on this principle. The phrase is thus treated as sarih (explicit) in manumission.
5
Note the Ikhtilaf
Some usuli scholars held that exception after negation merely clarifies, not emphasizes. The Hanafi response is that the structure inherently gives emphasis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحر وعتق بما أنت إلا حر وبملك قريب محرم ولو كان المالك صبياً أو مجنوناً وبتحرير يعني جميع الوجوه فلذلك لم يعتق لا في القضاء ولا فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى. ومعنى المثل في اللغة النظير؛ كذا في الجمهرة اهـ. وفي المحيط : لو قال ما أنت إلا مثل الحر لا يعتق، ولو قال لحرة أنت حرة مثل هذه أمته فأمته حرة، ولو قال أنت حرة مثل هذه الأمة لم تعتق أمته اهـ. وفي الظهيرية : أخذ قميصاً خاطه غلامه وقال هذه خياطة حر لا يعتق العبد لأنه : يراد به التشبيه اهـ. فقد علمت أن بعض هذه المسائل يعتق فيها بالنية وبعضها لا فلا ينبغي إدخالها في سلك واحد وفي الخانية : لو قال لعبده أنت حر يعني في النفس لم يدين في القضاء، ولو قال أنت عتيق وقال عنيت به في الملك لا يدين في القضاء، ولو قال أنت عتيق في السن لا يعتق، ولو قال أنت حر النفس يعني في الأخلاق عتق في القضاء اهـ. وفي المحيط وغيره: لو قال لعبده بدنك بدن حر ورأسك رأس حر لم يعتق لأنه تشبيه وليس بتحقيق لأنه لو أراد التحقيق لقال بدنك حر، ولو نوّن فقال رأسك رأس حر أو بدنك بدن حر أو وجهك وجه حر عتق لأن هذا وصف له بالحرية وليس بتشبيه فصار كأنه قال رأسك حر. لغة قوله : (وعتق بما أنت إلا حر لأن الاستثناء من النفي إثبات على وجه التأكيد كما في كلمة الشهادة كذا في الهداية. وفي فتح القدير : هذا هو الحق المفهوم من تركيب الاستثناء وهو بخلاف قول المشايخ في الأصول وقد بيناه في الأصول، وأنه لا ينافي قولهم الاستثناء تكلم بالباقي بعد الثنيا، وأما كونه إثباتاً مؤكداً فلو روده بعد النفي بخلاف الإثبات المجرد اهـ.
The free person and manumission by 'you are nothing but free', and by ownership of a mahram relative, even if the owner is a minor or insane, and by manumitting for God, for Satan, for an idol, meaning all aspects, therefore he is not freed neither in judiciary nor between him and Allah Almighty. The meaning of 'like' in language is 'similar'; so in al-Jamhara. In al-Muhit: If he said 'You are nothing but like a free person' he is not freed, and if he said to a free woman 'You are free like this his slave girl' then his slave girl is free, and if he said 'You are free like this slave girl' his slave girl is not freed. In al-Dhahiriyya: He took a shirt sewn by his slave and said 'This is the sewing of a free person' the slave is not freed because what is intended is likening. So you have learned that some of these cases freedom occurs by intention and some not, so they should not be grouped together. In al-Khaniyya: If he said to his slave 'You are free meaning in breath' he is not judged in judiciary, and if he said 'You are free' and he meant by it in ownership, he is not judged, and if he said 'You are free in age' he is not freed, and if he said 'You are free in breath meaning in character' he is freed in judiciary. In al-Muhit and others: If he said to his slave 'Your body is the body of a free person and your head is the head of a free person' he is not freed because it is likening and not realization, because if he intended realization he would have said 'Your body is free', and if he pronounced tanwin and said 'Your head is a free person's head' or 'your body is a free person's body' or 'your face is a free person's face' he is freed because this is description of him with freedom and not likening, so it becomes as if he said 'Your head is free'. Language: His saying: (And manumission by 'you are nothing but free' because exception from negation is affirmation in an emphatic manner as in the word of testimony, so in al-Hidaya. In Fath al-Qadir: This is the correct understanding from the structure of exception, and it is contrary to the saying of the scholars in usul, and we have explained it in usul, and that it does not contradict their saying that exception is speaking about the remainder after exclusion, and as for it being emphatic affirmation, it is only after negation, unlike plain affirmation.
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the effect of different expressions on manumission. It begins by citing the matn which states that manumission occurs by saying 'you are nothing but free' and by other means. The sharh then explains that in all cases of likening (tashbih), the slave is not freed. The author clarifies that the word 'like' (mithl) means 'similar' as per Arabic language. He then presents multiple rulings from various Hanafi sources: In al-Muhit, saying 'you are nothing but like a free person' does not effect manumission. However, if a man says to his free wife 'you are free like this slave girl', his slave girl becomes free because he likened his wife to the slave girl, but if he says to his slave girl 'you are free like this slave girl' (i.e., another), his slave girl does not become free. In al-Dhahiriyya, if a man takes a shirt sewn by his slave and says 'this is the sewing of a free person', the slave does not become free because it is likening. The author generalizes that some of these cases depend on intention (niyyah) and some do not, so they should not be treated uniformly. He then quotes al-Khaniyya distinguishing between saying 'you are free meaning in breath' (dying) — not judged in court, 'you are free meaning in ownership' — not judged, 'you are free in age' — no manumission, and 'you are free in breath meaning in character' — manumission occurs in court. Then from al-Muhit, the case of describing body parts: 'your body is the body of a free person' does not free the slave because it is likening, but if said with tanwin (making it indefinite description) as 'your head is a free person's head' and similar, then it becomes a description (sifah) rather than likening, and thus manumission occurs. Finally, the author discusses the linguistic principle: the phrase 'ما أنت إلا حر' (you are nothing but free) uses exception after negation, which yields emphatic affirmation, similar to the kalimah shahada. This is the relied-upon position as in al-Hidaya and Fath al-Qadir, contrary to the position of some usuli scholars. The key point is that the structure of exception (istithna) after negation turns the meaning into affirmation but with emphasis, and this is what causes manumission.

Key Terms
الاستثناء (al-istithna): A grammatical construction where an exception is made from a general negation, resulting in affirmation of the exception.
النفي (al-nafy): Linguistic negation, the opposite of affirmation.
الإثبات (al-ithbat): Affirmation or positive statement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the legal effect of a master saying to his slave 'ما أنت إلا حر'?
A) The slave does not become free unless the master intended it.
B) The slave becomes free immediately without needing intention.
C) The slave becomes free only if the phrase is written.
D) The slave becomes free but can be reclaimed if the master retracts.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school treats 'ما أنت إلا حر' as an explicit declaration of freedom due to the linguistic principle that exception after negation gives emphatic affirmation.

Expressions of Likening (Tashbih) vs. Description (Sifah) in Manumission
Ruling with proof: distinction between tashbih and sifah in manumission expressions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between phrases that are pure likening (tashbih) and those that constitute description (sifah), and apply the Hanafi ruling that only description effectuates manumission.

Core Idea
Pure likening does not free; description with tanwin does, because it ascribes freedom to the person.

The passage presents several cases where a master uses phrases likening the slave to a free person. The key distinction is whether the phrase is understood as tashbih (likening) or as sifah (description). This lesson explores the examples from al-Muhit and al-Khaniyya, showing how the presence of the word 'like' (مثل) or the use of idafa without tanwin indicates likening, while idafa with tanwin (e.g., 'ra'su hurrin') becomes a description that frees the slave.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Case and the Phrase
From the text, note the case of saying 'Your body is the body of a free person' (بدنك بدن حر). This does not free the slave.
The phrase uses idafa (construct state) without tanwin.
2
Determine the Linguistic Form
The phrase 'بدنك بدن حر' is a form of tashbih (likening) because it equates the slave's body to the body of a free person without directly attributing freedom.
The author says it is not tahqiq (realization).
3
Contrast with the Same Phrase with Tanwin
If he says 'رأسك رأس حر' (with tanwin on رأس in the second part), the meaning changes. It becomes 'Your head is a free person's head' as a description, not likening.
The tanwin indicates indefiniteness, making it a description (sifah).
4
Apply the Ruling
When it is description, the slave is freed because the phrase directly attributes freedom to the slave's body part, hence to the slave himself.
The author says it is like saying 'Rasuka hurr'.
5
Generalise the Principle
Any phrase that is pure likening (using مثل or idafa without descriptive intent) does not free; any phrase that describes the slave as having the attribute of a free person (tanwin, direct attribution) frees.
Intention may affect some cases, as seen in al-Khaniyya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحر وعتق بما أنت إلا حر وبملك قريب محرم ولو كان المالك صبياً أو مجنوناً وبتحرير يعني جميع الوجوه فلذلك لم يعتق لا في القضاء ولا فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى. ومعنى المثل في اللغة النظير؛ كذا في الجمهرة اهـ. وفي المحيط : لو قال ما أنت إلا مثل الحر لا يعتق، ولو قال لحرة أنت حرة مثل هذه أمته فأمته حرة، ولو قال أنت حرة مثل هذه الأمة لم تعتق أمته اهـ. وفي الظهيرية : أخذ قميصاً خاطه غلامه وقال هذه خياطة حر لا يعتق العبد لأنه : يراد به التشبيه اهـ. فقد علمت أن بعض هذه المسائل يعتق فيها بالنية وبعضها لا فلا ينبغي إدخالها في سلك واحد وفي الخانية : لو قال لعبده أنت حر يعني في النفس لم يدين في القضاء، ولو قال أنت عتيق وقال عنيت به في الملك لا يدين في القضاء، ولو قال أنت عتيق في السن لا يعتق، ولو قال أنت حر النفس يعني في الأخلاق عتق في القضاء اهـ. وفي المحيط وغيره: لو قال لعبده بدنك بدن حر ورأسك رأس حر لم يعتق لأنه تشبيه وليس بتحقيق لأنه لو أراد التحقيق لقال بدنك حر، ولو نوّن فقال رأسك رأس حر أو بدنك بدن حر أو وجهك وجه حر عتق لأن هذا وصف له بالحرية وليس بتشبيه فصار كأنه قال رأسك حر. لغة قوله : (وعتق بما أنت إلا حر لأن الاستثناء من النفي إثبات على وجه التأكيد كما في كلمة الشهادة كذا في الهداية. وفي فتح القدير : هذا هو الحق المفهوم من تركيب الاستثناء وهو بخلاف قول المشايخ في الأصول وقد بيناه في الأصول، وأنه لا ينافي قولهم الاستثناء تكلم بالباقي بعد الثنيا، وأما كونه إثباتاً مؤكداً فلو روده بعد النفي بخلاف الإثبات المجرد اهـ.
The free person and manumission by 'you are nothing but free', and by ownership of a mahram relative, even if the owner is a minor or insane, and by manumitting for God, for Satan, for an idol, meaning all aspects, therefore he is not freed neither in judiciary nor between him and Allah Almighty. Key Terms
التشبيه (al-tashbih): A figure of speech likening one thing to another using a particle of comparison like 'ka' or 'mithla'.
الوصف (al-wasf): Description; in this context, a grammatical construction that attributes a quality to the subject.
التنوين (al-tanwin): A nunation (double vowel sign) at the end of a noun, indicating indefiniteness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling if a master says to his slave 'بدنك بدن حر' (your body is the body of a free person)?
A) The slave becomes free.
B) The slave does not become free because it is tashbih.
C) The slave becomes free only if the master intended it.
D) The slave becomes free if he is a minor.

Key Takeaway
The key distinction for manumission is whether the expression is tashbih (likening) or sifah (description); the presence of tanwin on the second noun of an idafa is the grammatical sign of description.

Automatic Manumission of a Mahram Relative Upon Ownership
Explaining the hadith-based ruling that owning a mahram relative causes automatic manumission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that owning a mahram relative causes automatic manumission, apply the conditions of permanent mahram relationship and location (Dar al-Islam), and evaluate the intra-school dispute regarding Dar al-Harb.

Core Idea
Owning a mahram relative automatically frees them, but only if the relationship is permanent and the ownership occurs in Dar al-Islam.

This lesson explores a unique Hanafi ruling derived from the hadith: whoever owns a mahram relative becomes free. We will examine the proof, the precise definition of 'mahram relative,' the conditions for the ruling, and a significant disagreement within the Hanafi school about whether it applies in Dar al-Harb. Understanding this ruling clarifies the legal consequences of ownership and kinship in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
The act is owning a specified relative. The ruling is that this relative becomes free immediately upon ownership, without the owner's explicit intent.
This is not a discretionary act of manumission; it is automatic.
2
Locate the Dalil
The primary proof is the hadith: 'Whoever owns a mahram relative from him, then he is free.' Identify the hadith and note its status in Hanafi usul.
The hadith is considered sound (hasan) and acted upon in the school.
3
Examine the Conditions and Restrictions
The text specifies that the relative must be a mahram (unmarriageable) through a permanent relationship (blood or breastfeeding). Additionally, the ownership must take place in Dar al-Islam. List the key conditions and exceptions.
Non-mahram relatives (e.g., cousins) and temporary mahram (e.g., wife's sister) do not trigger the ruling.
4
Analyze the Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Dar al-Harb
Abu Yusuf disagrees with Abu Hanifa and Muhammad on whether ownership in Dar al-Harb causes manumission. State each position, the underlying reasoning, and the practical consequence.
The majority view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is relied upon for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وبملك قريب محرم ولو كان المالك صبياً أو مجنوناً) معطوفاً على قوله أول الباب «بأنت حر أي يصح العتق بملك قريب محرم للحديث من ملك ذا رحم محرم منه فهو أو عتق عليه واللفظ بعمومه ينتظم كل قرابة مؤبدة بالمحرمية ولاداً أو غيره، ولأنه ملك رحم قريبه قرابة مؤثرة في المحرمية فيعتق عليه وهذا هو المؤثر في قرابة الولاد. وذكر فخر الإسلام البزدوي في بحث العلل أن العلة في عتق القريب بالملك شيئان : القرابة والملك. لكن العتق يضاف إلى آخرهما فإن تأخر الملك أضيف إليه العتق كما إذا ملك قريبه، وإن تأخرت القرابة وتقدم الملك أضيف العتق إلى القرابة كما إذا كان بين اثنين عبد ثم ادعى أحدهما إنه ابنه غرم لشريكه وأضيف العتق إلى القرابة .اهـ. قيد بالقريب لأنه لو ملك محرماً بلا كزوجة أبيه أو ابنه لا يعتق لأنه ليس بينهما قرابة موجبة للصلة محرمة للقطيعة فلا يستحق العتق . وقيد بالمحرم احترازاً عن الرحم بلا محرم كبني الأعمام والأخوال والخالات إذا ملكه لم يعتق، وخص عن النص المحرم للقطيعة بالإجماع لما أنهم كثير لا يحصون فلو عتقوا ربما حرجوا الملاك فيه لتعذر معرفتهم بالكلية، لو خصت القرابة المحرمية عن النص أيضاً لأدى إلى تعطيله وذلك لا يجوز، وكذا لو ملك ذا رحم محرم من الرضاع فلا بد أن تكون المحرمية من جهة الصلة القرابة وذو الرحم المحرم شخصان يدليان إلى أصل واحد ليس بينهما واسطة كالأخوين أو أحدهما بواسطة والآخر بغير واسطة كابن الأخ مع العم في النسبة إلى الجد؛ كذا في المحيط، المالك فشمل المسلم والكافر لأنهما يستويان في الملك وفيما يلزمهم من وحرمة القطيعة. ويشترط أن يكون في دار الإسلام لأنه لا حكم لنا في دار الحرب فلو ملك قريبه في دار الحرب أو أعتق المسلم عبده في دار الحرب لا يعتق خلافاً لأبي يوسف، وعلى هذا الخلاف إذا أعتق الحربي عبده في دار الحرب وذكر الخلاف في الإيضاح . وفي الكافي للحاكم: عتق الحربي في دار الحرب قريبه باطل ولم يذكر خلافاً، أما إذا أعتقه وخلاه ففي المختلف قال يعتق عند أبي يوسف وولاؤه له وقالا : لا ولاء له لأن عتقه بالتخلية لا بالإعتاق ثم قال : المسلم إذا دخل دار الحرب فاشترى عبداً حربياً فأعتقه ثمة فالقياس أنه لا يعتق بدون التخلية، وفي الاستحسان يعتق بدونها ولا ولاء له عندهما قياساً، وله الولاء عند أبي يوسف استحساناً. وفي المحيط: وإن كان عبده مسلماً أو ذمياً عتق بالإجماع لأنه ليس بمحل للاسترقاق بالاستيلاء اهـ والصبي جعل أهلاً لهذا العتق وكذا المجنون حتى عتق القريب عليهما عند الملك لأنه تعلق به حق العبد فشابه النفقة. وفي البدائع : ولو اشترى أمة وهي حبلى من أبيه والأمة لغير أبيه جاز الشراء وعتق ما في بطنها ولا تعتق الأمة ولا يجوز بيعها قبل أن تضع وله أن يبيعها إذا وضعت وإنما عتق الحمل لأنه أخوه وقد ملكه فيعتق عليه اهـ. فأفاد أن الحمل داخل تحت قولهم وبملك قريب بناء على أنه مملوك قبل الوضع مع أنهم قالوا الحمل لا يدخل تحت اسم المملوك حتى لو قال كل مملوك لي حر لا يعتق الحمل فيحتاج إلى الجواب وأطلق المصنف في الملك فشمل ما إذا باشر سببه بنفسه أو بنائبه فدخل ما إذا اشترى العبد المأذون ذا رحم محرم من مولاه ولا دين عليه فإنه يعتق بخلاف المديون لا يعتق ما اشتراه عنده خلافاً لهما. وخرج المكاتب إذا اشترى ابن مولاه فإنه لا يعتق في قولهم جميعاً كما في الظهيرية، وشمل الكل والبعض فإذا ملك بعض قريبه عتق عليه بقدره كما سيأتي .
His saying: (And by ownership of a mahram relative, even if the owner is a minor or insane) is connected to his saying at the beginning of the chapter 'By you are free' i.e., manumission is valid by owning a mahram relative due to the hadith: 'Whoever owns a mahram relative from him, then he is free or free upon him' and the wording by its generality encompasses every permanent relationship by mahramiyya by birth or otherwise, and because he owns the womb of his relative, a relationship effective in mahramiyya, so he becomes free upon him, and this is the effective factor in the relationship of birth. Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi mentioned in the discussion of causes that the cause in freeing the relative by ownership is two things: relationship and ownership. But the freeing is attributed to the later of the two, so if ownership is later, freeing is attributed to it, as when he owns his relative; if the relationship is later and ownership precedes, freeing is attributed to the relationship, as when a slave is between two owners, then one claims he is his son, he compensates his partner and freeing is attributed to the relationship. It is restricted to 'relative' because if he owns a mahram without relationship, like the wife of his father or his son, he is not freed because there is no relationship necessitating connection and prohibiting severance, so he does not deserve freeing. And it is restricted to 'mahram' to exclude womb without mahram, like the sons of paternal uncles, maternal uncles, and maternal aunts; if he owns him, he is not freed. And the text specifically restricted to mahram for severance by consensus because they are many and countless; if they were freed, it would harm the owners due to the impossibility of knowing them completely. If the mahram relationship were also specified by the text, it would lead to its disuse, and that is not permissible. Similarly, if he owns a mahram relative through breastfeeding, the mahramiyya must be from the connection of relationship. And 'dhu rahim mahram' is two persons who trace back to one origin without an intermediary, like two brothers, or one with an intermediary and the other without, like the son of a brother with the paternal uncle in relation to the grandfather; thus in al-Muhit. The owner includes Muslim and disbeliever because they are equal in ownership and in what is incumbent upon them of the prohibition of severance. And it is conditioned that it be in Dar al-Islam because we have no jurisdiction in Dar al-Harb. So if he owns his relative in Dar al-Harb or a Muslim frees his slave in Dar al-Harb, he is not freed, contrary to Abu Yusuf. And there is similarly disagreement if a Harbi frees his slave in Dar al-Harb; the disagreement is mentioned in al-Idah. In al-Kafi lil-Hakim: The freeing of a Harbi in Dar al-Harb of his relative is void, and no disagreement is mentioned. As for if he frees him and lets him go, then in al-Mukhtalif: He is freed according to Abu Yusuf and his wala is for him; both (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say: no wala for him because his freeing is by letting go, not by manumission. Then he said: If a Muslim enters Dar al-Harb and buys a Harbi slave and frees him there, by qiyas he is not freed without letting go; by istihsan he is freed without it and there is no wala for him according to both (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) by qiyas, and he has wala according to Abu Yusuf by istihsan. In al-Muhit: If his slave is Muslim or dhimmi, he is freed by consensus because he is not a place for enslavement by conquest. And the minor and the insane are made eligible for this freeing, so the relative is freed upon them at ownership because a right of the slave attaches to them, resembling maintenance. In al-Badai': If he buys a slave girl who is pregnant from his father, and the slave girl belongs to someone else, the purchase is valid and what is in her womb is freed, but the slave girl is not freed, and it is not permissible to sell her before she gives birth; he may sell her after delivery. The pregnancy is freed because it is his brother and he has owned it, so it is freed upon him. Thus, the fetus is included under their statement 'by owning a relative' based on it being owned before birth, even though they said the fetus is not included under the name 'owned' such that if someone says 'every slave I own is free', the fetus is not freed, so a response is needed. And the author's expression 'ownership' is general, covering whether he directly causes the cause himself or through his agent, so it includes the case where an authorized slave buys a mahram relative from his master and has no debt; then he becomes free, contrary to if he has debt, he does not become free according to his (Abu Hanifa's) view, contrary to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad? Actually 'indahu khalafan lahuma means Abu Hanifa's view contradicts the two. And the mukatib is excluded: if he buys the son of his master, he does not become free in all their views, as in al-Dhahiriyya. And the ruling covers the whole and the part, so if he owns part of his relative, it is freed upon him proportionately, as will come later.
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling with proof on the automatic manumission of a mahram relative upon ownership. It begins by stating that the matn's phrase 'by owning a mahram relative' means that manumission is valid. The proof is the hadith 'Whoever owns a mahram relative, then he is free' (often narrated). The commentary expands on the definition of mahram relative: it must be a perpetual mahram relationship by birth or through other means, not temporary like marriage. Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi analyzes the 'illa (cause) as two factors: relationship (qarabah) and ownership (milk), and explains attributing manumission to the later of the two. The text then restricts the ruling: the relative must be a mahram with a blood relationship that prohibits marriage; owning a non-mahram like the wife of one's father does not cause manumission. Also, it excludes distant relatives like cousins, who are not mahram. The condition of being in Dar al-Islam is discussed: if the ownership occurs in Dar al-Harb, the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) hold that manumission does not occur, unlike Abu Yusuf. The text also covers the case of a Harbi freeing his relative in Dar al-Harb, with differing views. Further, it states that a minor or insane owner can cause this manumission, similar to maintenance obligations. A special case from al-Badai' is presented: buying a pregnant slave who is carrying one's brother from one's father triggers manumission of the fetus. The passage then addresses the inclusion of the fetus under the general rule despite technicalities. Finally, it excludes the mukatib (a slave who has contracted to buy his freedom) from causing manumission if he buys his master's son, and clarifies that owning a partial share of a relative frees proportionately.

Key Terms
ذو رحم محرم (dhu rahim mahram): A person related to the owner by a blood relationship that is permanent and renders marriage between them unlawful (e.g., parent, child, sibling).
المحرمية (al-mahramiyyah): The state of being a mahram, i.e., a person whom one cannot marry due to a permanent cause.
القرابة (al-qarabah): Kinship or blood relationship.
عتق القريب (itq al-qareeb): The automatic manumission of a relative upon ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if a Muslim owns his mahram relative in Dar al-Harb?
A) The relative is freed immediately.
B) The relative is not freed because Islamic rulings do not apply in Dar al-Harb (majority view).
C) The relative is freed but the owner must pay compensation.
D) The ownership is invalid.

Key Takeaway
Owning a mahram relative automatically frees them, but only if the relationship is permanent and occurs in Dar al-Islam; in Dar al-Harb, the majority (Abu Hanifa & Muhammad) rule no manumission.

Ruling on Manumission: Intention Does Not Affect Validity
Explains that manumission is valid regardless of intention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of manumission performed with a sinful or non-devotional intention and explain the proof that the rukn is the act of freeing itself.

Core Idea
Manumission is valid regardless of intention – the rukn is the release, not the devotion.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies a fundamental principle in the Hanafi school: the legal validity of manumission does not depend on the religious quality of the intention. Even if a person frees a slave for a sinful purpose – such as for an idol or to defy God – the slave is set free legally. This ruling underscores the distinction between the essential components (arkan) of an act and the conditions of reward (qurbah). In this lesson, we will examine the proof for this position and understand why intention is irrelevant to the contractual effectiveness of manumission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act in question is manumission (i'taq). The ruling is that it is valid (sahih) even if done for a sinful purpose, such as for Satan or an idol.
2
Locate the proof
The author argues that the rukn (essential pillar) of manumission is the removal of property (raf' al-raq). The quality of devotion (qurbah) is not part of the rukn and does not affect validity. Evidence: manumission and kitabah are legislated even when devoid of qurbah.
This is a logical proof: the act itself is defined by its essential effect, not by the intention.
3
Apply the principle
If the rukn is independent of intention, then any valid phrasing of freeing, even with a sinful intent, produces legal consequences. The slave is freed.
4
Note the limit
The ruling applies to a Muslim owner. The passage also touches on the Dar al-Harb discussion, which involves a separate condition regarding whether the slave is freed in enemy territory.
The Dar al-Harb issue is not central to the main ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وبتحرير لوجه الله وللشيطان وللصنم أي يصح العتق بتحرير هو عبادة أو معصية لأن الإعتاق هو الركن المؤثر في إزالة الرق وصفة القربة لا تأثير لها في ذلك، ألا ترى أن العتق والكتابة بالمال مشروعان وإن عريا عن صفة القربة فلا ينعدم بعدمها أصل العتق، ولا يخفى أن الإعتاق للصنم إنما هو صادر من كافر، وأما إذا صدر من مسلم فينبغي قوله : (ثم قال المسلم إذا دخل دار الحرب الخ) مقتضاه أنه في الاستحسان يعتق عند الكل وقد مر قريباً أنه لو أعتق المسلم عبده في دار الحرب لا يعتق خلافاً لأبي يوسف. وجمع بينهما في الفتح بأن يراد بالمسلم ثمة الذي نشأ في دار الحرب وهنا نص على أنه داخل هناك بعد أن كان هنا فلذا لم
His saying: (And by freeing for the sake of God, for the sake of Satan, and for the sake of an idol) i.e., manumission is valid by a freeing that is an act of worship or an act of disobedience, because manumission is the essential pillar that effects removal of slavery, and the quality of devotion has no effect on that. Do you not see that manumission and kitabah by wealth are legislated even if they are devoid of the quality of devotion, so the essence of manumission is not negated by its absence. And it is not hidden that freeing for an idol comes only from a disbeliever. But if it comes from a Muslim, then his saying: (Then he said: The Muslim when he enters Dar al-Harb...) its implication is that by istihsan he is freed according to all, and it has passed recently that if a Muslim frees his slave in Dar al-Harb, he is not freed contrary to Abu Yusuf. And a reconciliation between them was made in al-Fath by intending by 'Muslim' there the one who grew up in Dar al-Harb, and here the text indicates that he entered there after having been here, therefore it was not... (incomplete).
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof that the validity of manumission does not depend on the intention. The matn states that manumission is valid even if done for God (i.e., as an act of worship), for Satan (i.e., sin), or for an idol (disbelief). The sharh explains that the essential element (rukn) of manumission is the removal of the property right (raqabah) from the slave; the quality of being a devotional act (qurbah) is incidental and does not affect the validity. As evidence, the author points out that both manumission and kitabah (a contract of gradual emancipation) are legislated even when they lack the quality of devotion, such as when done for purely worldly reasons. He then notes that freeing for an idol is typically an act of a disbeliever (kafir). If a Muslim were to do it, it would be problematic. However, the text then shifts to a discussion about a Muslim entering Dar al-Harb and freeing a slave there, attempting to reconcile two apparent contradictions. The passage ends abruptly, leaving the reconciliation incomplete. The core teaching remains clear: the act of manumission is effective regardless of the religious quality of the intention.

Key Terms
التحرير (al-tahreer): The act of freeing a slave; manumission.
الإعتاق (al-i'taq): The legal act of removing the state of slavery; a synonym for tahreer in this context.
القربة (al-qurbah): An act of devotion or obedience that brings one closer to God.
المعصية (al-ma'siyah): An act of disobedience to God; a sin.
الركن (al-rukn): The essential component of an act without which it is not valid; in manumission, the rukn is the act of releasing the slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a man frees his slave for the sake of an idol (i.e., as an offering), what is the ruling?
A) The manumission is invalid because it is done for a false deity.
B) The manumission is valid but sinful.
C) The manumission is valid and rewarded.
D) The manumission is invalid because the slave becomes an idol worshipper.

Key Takeaway
Manumission is legally valid irrespective of the intention; the essential element is the act of freeing, not the devotional quality.
