Definition and Basic Rule: Emancipation of Close Relatives
Ruling with proof: Conditions for automatic emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'dhu al-rahim al-muhrim' and state the Hanafi requirement that automatic emancipation of a close relative occurs only in Dar al-Islam.

Core Idea
Emancipation of a close relative is wajib, but only if ownership occurs in Dar al-Islam.

This lesson begins the discussion on automatic emancipation (itq) when a close relative is owned. The Hanafi school establishes that owning a mahram relative in Dar al-Islam triggers immediate freedom for the slave. However, the ruling changes if ownership takes place in Dar al-Harb. We will first understand who qualifies as a 'dhu rahm muhrim' and then examine the territorial condition.

Guided Path
1
Define the category of relative
The passage defines 'dhu al-rahim al-muhrim' as two persons who trace back to a single origin, either directly or through one intermediary. Identify the examples given: brothers, and uncle and nephew.
The definition is from al-Muhit, a standard Hanafi reference.
2
State the basic ruling
Ownership of such a relative in Dar al-Islam causes automatic emancipation; it is wajib upon the owner.
The slave becomes free instantly without any action needed.
3
Explain the territorial condition
The ruling is explicitly restricted to Dar al-Islam because Islamic law has no jurisdiction in Dar al-Harb. The source says 'we have no ruling in Dar al-Harb'.
This condition is unique to the Hanafi school; other schools may not require it.
4
Note the owner's status
The owner can be Muslim or non-Muslim because they are equal in ownership and in the obligation to maintain family ties.
This is stated explicitly: 'al-malik fashamila al-muslim wa al-kafir'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب العتق
لوجه الله وللشيطان وللصنم وبكره وسكر وإن أضافه إلى ملك أو شرط صح ولو حرر وأطلق في الصلة القرابة وذو الرحم المحرم شخصان يدليان إلى أصل واحد ليس بينهما واسطة كالأخوين أو أحدهما بواسطة والآخر بغير واسطة كابن الأخ مع العم في النسبة إلى الجد؛ كذا في المحيط، المالك فشمل المسلم والكافر لأنهما يستويان في الملك وفيما يلزمهم من وحرمة القطيعة. ويشترط أن يكون في دار الإسلام لأنه لا حكم لنا في دار الحرب فلو ملك قريبه في دار الحرب أو أعتق المسلم عبده في دار الحرب لا يعتق خلافاً لأبي يوسف، وعلى هذا الخلاف إذا أعتق الحربي عبده في دار الحرب وذكر الخلاف في الإيضاح . وفي الكافي للحاكم: عتق الحربي في دار الحرب قريبه باطل ولم يذكر خلافاً، أما إذا أعتقه وخلاه ففي المختلف قال يعتق عند أبي يوسف وولاؤه له وقالا : لا ولاء له لأن عتقه بالتخلية لا بالإعتاق ثم قال : المسلم إذا دخل دار الحرب فاشترى عبداً حربياً فأعتقه ثمة فالقياس أنه لا يعتق بدون التخلية، وفي الاستحسان يعتق بدونها ولا ولاء له عندهما قياساً، وله الولاء عند أبي يوسف استحساناً. وفي المحيط: وإن كان عبده مسلماً أو ذمياً عتق بالإجماع لأنه ليس بمحل للاسترقاق بالاستيلاء اهـ والصبي جعل أهلاً لهذا العتق وكذا المجنون حتى عتق القريب عليهما عند الملك لأنه تعلق به حق العبد فشابه النفقة. وفي البدائع : ولو اشترى أمة وهي حبلى من أبيه والأمة لغير أبيه جاز الشراء وعتق ما في بطنها ولا تعتق الأمة ولا يجوز بيعها قبل أن تضع وله أن يبيعها إذا وضعت وإنما عتق الحمل لأنه أخوه وقد ملكه فيعتق عليه اهـ. فأفاد أن الحمل داخل تحت قولهم وبملك قريب بناء على أنه مملوك قبل الوضع مع أنهم قالوا الحمل لا يدخل تحت اسم المملوك حتى لو قال كل مملوك لي حر لا يعتق الحمل فيحتاج إلى الجواب وأطلق المصنف في الملك فشمل ما إذا باشر سببه بنفسه أو بنائبه فدخل ما إذا اشترى العبد المأذون ذا رحم محرم من مولاه ولا دين عليه فإنه يعتق بخلاف المديون لا يعتق ما اشتراه عنده خلافاً لهما. وخرج المكاتب إذا اشترى ابن مولاه فإنه لا يعتق في قولهم جميعاً كما في الظهيرية، وشمل الكل والبعض فإذا ملك بعض قريبه عتق عليه بقدره كما سيأتي .
Book of Emancipation. For the sake of Allah and for the sake of Satan and for the sake of an idol, and under duress and intoxication, and if he adds it to ownership or conditions, it is valid. If he freed and was absolute in connection, then kinship and a close relative are two persons who trace back to one origin with no intermediary between them, like two brothers, or one with an intermediary and the other without, like a son of a brother with a paternal uncle in relation to the grandfather; thus in al-Muhit. Owner includes Muslim and disbeliever because they are equal in ownership and in what obligates them of [maintaining ties] and the prohibition of severance. It is required that it be in Dar al-Islam because we have no jurisdiction in Dar al-Harb. So if one owns his relative in Dar al-Harb or if a Muslim emancipates his slave in Dar al-Harb, he does not become free, contrary to Abu Yusuf. And upon this difference, if a Harbi emancipates his slave in Dar al-Harb; the difference is mentioned in al-Idah. And in al-Kafi by al-Hakim: the emancipation of a Harbi of his relative in Dar al-Harb is null, and no difference is mentioned. As for if he emancipates him and lets him go, then in al-Mukhtalaf he said: it becomes free according to Abu Yusuf and his patronage belongs to him, and they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: no patronage for him because his emancipation is by letting go, not by setting free. Then he said: if a Muslim enters Dar al-Harb and buys a Harbi slave and emancipates him there, by analogy he does not become free without letting go, and by istihsan he becomes free without it, and there is no patronage for him according to them by analogy, while according to Abu Yusuf he has patronage by istihsan. And in al-Muhit: if his slave is Muslim or dhimmi, he becomes free by consensus because he is not a subject for enslavement by conquest. End quote. And a minor is considered qualified for this emancipation, likewise an insane person, so that the relative becomes free upon them upon ownership because the right of the slave is attached, resembling maintenance. And in al-Bada'i: if one buys a female slave who is pregnant from his father, and the slave is not his father's, the purchase is valid and what is in her womb becomes free, but the female slave does not become free, and it is not permissible to sell her before she gives birth, and he may sell her after she gives birth. The pregnancy becomes free only because it is his brother and he has owned it, so it becomes free upon him. End quote. So it conveys that the pregnancy is included in their statement 'by owning a relative' based on the view that it is owned before birth, although they said the pregnancy does not fall under the term 'owned property', so that if one says 'every owned property of mine is free', the pregnancy does not become free, so a response is needed. And the author generalized in ownership, so it includes whether he conducts the cause himself or through his deputy, thus including if the authorized slave buys a close relative from his master and he has no debt, then he becomes free, unlike if he is in debt: he does not become free when he buys it, according to him (Abu Hanifa) contrary to them (the two). And the mukatab is excluded: if he buys the son of his master, it does not become free in the opinion of all of them, as in al-Zahiriya. And it includes the whole and the part: so if one owns part of his relative, it becomes free upon him to the extent of that part, as will come.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, expanding on the legal conditions for automatic emancipation of a close relative when owned. The author begins by defining qareeb (close relative) and dhu rahm muhrim: persons who trace back to a common ancestor, either directly or through an intermediary. Then he clarifies that 'owner' includes both Muslim and non-Muslim, since the obligation to maintain ties of kinship and the prohibition of severing them apply to both. However, a key condition is that the ownership must occur in Dar al-Islam, because Islamic law has no jurisdiction in Dar al-Harb. Therefore, if one owns his relative in Dar al-Harb, or if a Muslim emancipates his slave there, the emancipation is not effective according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, but Abu Yusuf holds it is effective. The passage then discusses the case of a Harbi (enemy combatant) emancipating his relative in Dar al-Harb: according to al-Kafi, it is null. There is further ikhtilaf about whether letting go suffices for emancipation. The text then notes consensus that emancipation of a Muslim or dhimmi slave in Dar al-Harb is valid because they are not legitimate subjects of enslavement. Next, it covers that a minor or insane person can cause emancipation of their relative by owning them, since the slave's right to freedom is attached. The author addresses a specific case from al-Bada'i: buying a pregnant slave from one's father—the child becomes free because it is a brother, but the mother does not. This raises a problem: pregnancy is not usually considered 'owned property' for the purposes of general emancipation vows, yet here it is considered owned for the relative rule. The author notes a need for response. Finally, the passage explains that ownership can be direct or through a deputy, and includes partial ownership: if one owns a portion of a relative, only that portion becomes free. This passage demonstrates the detailed casuistry of the Hanafi school in applying the principle of automatic emancipation of close relatives, considering various scenarios and positions.

Key Terms
ذو الرحم المحرم (dhu al-rahim al-muhrim): A close relative who is prohibited for marriage, defined in this passage as two persons tracing back to one origin, either directly or through one intermediary.
دار الإسلام (dar al-islam): Territory where Islamic law is applied and Muslims are secure. In Hanafi fiqh, this condition is required for automatic emancipation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The owner must be Muslim.
B) The relative must be male.
C) The ownership must occur in Dar al-Islam.
D) The emancipation must be verbally declared.

Key Takeaway
Automatic emancipation of a close relative (dhu rahm muhrim) upon ownership is wajib, but only if the ownership takes place within Dar al-Islam.

Intra-Hanafi Disputes and Special Cases in Emancipation of Relatives
Ruling with proof: Intra-Hanafi dispute and exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the position of Abu Yusuf versus Abu Hanifa and Muhammad regarding emancipation in Dar al-Harb, and apply the rulings to special cases including pregnancy, partial ownership, and authorized slaves.

Core Idea
Abu Yusuf allows emancipation in Dar al-Harb via istihsan, but the mufta bihi follows the qiyas-based majority.

This lesson delves into the intra-Hanafi disagreements on whether automatic emancipation applies in Dar al-Harb. Abu Yusuf differs from Abu Hanifa and Muhammad on this point, and the passage also addresses special scenarios: the case of a Harbi slave, a pregnant slave, an authorized slave acting as buyer, and partial ownership. Understanding these nuances is key to mastering the Hanafi position on 'itq al-qareeb'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute on Dar al-Harb
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that owning a relative in Dar al-Harb does not cause emancipation due to lack of jurisdiction. Abu Yusuf holds it does, using istihsan.
This is the main ikhtilaf; the text also mentions a sub-issue about letting go.
2
Analyze the qiyas vs istihsan argument
The qiyas is that emancipation in Dar al-Harb is ineffective because legal authority is absent. Istihsan favors the slave's freedom due to the relationship.
Hanafi response: the sabab (cause) is incomplete in Dar al-Harb.
3
Examine the case of a Harbi slave owned by a Muslim in Dar al-Harb
If a Muslim buys a Harbi slave there and emancipates him, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad require letting go (takhliyah) for freedom; Abu Yusuf does not.
Consensus: if the slave is Muslim or dhimmi, emancipation is valid regardless.
4
Consider special scenarios
The passage covers pregnancy (womb child owned as brother), the authorized slave buying a relative, the mukatab, and partial ownership.
Each case tests the boundaries of the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب العتق
لوجه الله وللشيطان وللصنم وبكره وسكر وإن أضافه إلى ملك أو شرط صح ولو حرر وأطلق في الصلة القرابة وذو الرحم المحرم شخصان يدليان إلى أصل واحد ليس بينهما واسطة كالأخوين أو أحدهما بواسطة والآخر بغير واسطة كابن الأخ مع العم في النسبة إلى الجد؛ كذا في المحيط، المالك فشمل المسلم والكافر لأنهما يستويان في الملك وفيما يلزمهم من وحرمة القطيعة. ويشترط أن يكون في دار الإسلام لأنه لا حكم لنا في دار الحرب فلو ملك قريبه في دار الحرب أو أعتق المسلم عبده في دار الحرب لا يعتق خلافاً لأبي يوسف، وعلى هذا الخلاف إذا أعتق الحربي عبده في دار الحرب وذكر الخلاف في الإيضاح . وفي الكافي للحاكم: عتق الحربي في دار الحرب قريبه باطل ولم يذكر خلافاً، أما إذا أعتقه وخلاه ففي المختلف قال يعتق عند أبي يوسف وولاؤه له وقالا : لا ولاء له لأن عتقه بالتخلية لا بالإعتاق ثم قال : المسلم إذا دخل دار الحرب فاشترى عبداً حربياً فأعتقه ثمة فالقياس أنه لا يعتق بدون التخلية، وفي الاستحسان يعتق بدونها ولا ولاء له عندهما قياساً، وله الولاء عند أبي يوسف استحساناً. وفي المحيط: وإن كان عبده مسلماً أو ذمياً عتق بالإجماع لأنه ليس بمحل للاسترقاق بالاستيلاء اهـ والصبي جعل أهلاً لهذا العتق وكذا المجنون حتى عتق القريب عليهما عند الملك لأنه تعلق به حق العبد فشابه النفقة. وفي البدائع : ولو اشترى أمة وهي حبلى من أبيه والأمة لغير أبيه جاز الشراء وعتق ما في بطنها ولا تعتق الأمة ولا يجوز بيعها قبل أن تضع وله أن يبيعها إذا وضعت وإنما عتق الحمل لأنه أخوه وقد ملكه فيعتق عليه اهـ. فأفاد أن الحمل داخل تحت قولهم وبملك قريب بناء على أنه مملوك قبل الوضع مع أنهم قالوا الحمل لا يدخل تحت اسم المملوك حتى لو قال كل مملوك لي حر لا يعتق الحمل فيحتاج إلى الجواب وأطلق المصنف في الملك فشمل ما إذا باشر سببه بنفسه أو بنائبه فدخل ما إذا اشترى العبد المأذون ذا رحم محرم من مولاه ولا دين عليه فإنه يعتق بخلاف المديون لا يعتق ما اشتراه عنده خلافاً لهما. وخرج المكاتب إذا اشترى ابن مولاه فإنه لا يعتق في قولهم جميعاً كما في الظهيرية، وشمل الكل والبعض فإذا ملك بعض قريبه عتق عليه بقدره كما سيأتي .
Book of Emancipation. For the sake of Allah and for the sake of Satan and for the sake of an idol, and under duress and intoxication, and if he adds it to ownership or conditions, it is valid. If he freed and was absolute in connection, then kinship and a close relative are two persons who trace back to one origin with no intermediary between them, like two brothers, or one with an intermediary and the other without, like a son of a brother with a paternal uncle in relation to the grandfather; thus in al-Muhit. Owner includes Muslim and disbeliever because they are equal in ownership and in what obligates them of [maintaining ties] and the prohibition of severance. It is required that it be in Dar al-Islam because we have no jurisdiction in Dar al-Harb. So if one owns his relative in Dar al-Harb or if a Muslim emancipates his slave in Dar al-Harb, he does not become free, contrary to Abu Yusuf. And upon this difference, if a Harbi emancipates his slave in Dar al-Harb; the difference is mentioned in al-Idah. And in al-Kafi by al-Hakim: the emancipation of a Harbi of his relative in Dar al-Harb is null, and no difference is mentioned. As for if he emancipates him and lets him go, then in al-Mukhtalaf he said: it becomes free according to Abu Yusuf and his patronage belongs to him, and they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: no patronage for him because his emancipation is by letting go, not by setting free. Then he said: if a Muslim enters Dar al-Harb and buys a Harbi slave and emancipates him there, by analogy he does not become free without letting go, and by istihsan he becomes free without it, and there is no patronage for him according to them by analogy, while according to Abu Yusuf he has patronage by istihsan. And in al-Muhit: if his slave is Muslim or dhimmi, he becomes free by consensus because he is not a subject for enslavement by conquest. End quote. And a minor is considered qualified for this emancipation, likewise an insane person, so that the relative becomes free upon them upon ownership because the right of the slave is attached, resembling maintenance. And in al-Bada'i: if one buys a female slave who is pregnant from his father, and the slave is not his father's, the purchase is valid and what is in her womb becomes free, but the female slave does not become free, and it is not permissible to sell her before she gives birth, and he may sell her after she gives birth. The pregnancy becomes free only because it is his brother and he has owned it, so it becomes free upon him. End quote. So it conveys that the pregnancy is included in their statement 'by owning a relative' based on the view that it is owned before birth, although they said the pregnancy does not fall under the term 'owned property', so that if one says 'every owned property of mine is free', the pregnancy does not become free, so a response is needed. And the author generalized in ownership, so it includes whether he conducts the cause himself or through his deputy, thus including if the authorized slave buys a close relative from his master and he has no debt, then he becomes free, unlike if he is in debt: he does not become free when he buys it, according to him (Abu Hanifa) contrary to them (the two). And the mukatab is excluded: if he buys the son of his master, it does not become free in the opinion of all of them, as in al-Zahiriya. And it includes the whole and the part: so if one owns part of his relative, it becomes free upon him to the extent of that part, as will come.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, expanding on the legal conditions for automatic emancipation of a close relative when owned. The author begins by defining qareeb (close relative) and dhu rahm muhrim: persons who trace back to a common ancestor, either directly or through an intermediary. Then he clarifies that 'owner' includes both Muslim and non-Muslim, since the obligation to maintain ties of kinship and the prohibition of severing them apply to both. However, a key condition is that the ownership must occur in Dar al-Islam, because Islamic law has no jurisdiction in Dar al-Harb. Therefore, if one owns his relative in Dar al-Harb, or if a Muslim emancipates his slave there, the emancipation is not effective according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, but Abu Yusuf holds it is effective. The passage then discusses the case of a Harbi (enemy combatant) emancipating his relative in Dar al-Harb: according to al-Kafi, it is null. There is further ikhtilaf about whether letting go suffices for emancipation. The text then notes consensus that emancipation of a Muslim or dhimmi slave in Dar al-Harb is valid because they are not legitimate subjects of enslavement. Next, it covers that a minor or insane person can cause emancipation of their relative by owning them, since the slave's right to freedom is attached. The author addresses a specific case from al-Bada'i: buying a pregnant slave from one's father—the child becomes free because it is a brother, but the mother does not. This raises a problem: pregnancy is not usually considered 'owned property' for the purposes of general emancipation vows, yet here it is considered owned for the relative rule. The author notes a need for response. Finally, the passage explains that ownership can be direct or through a deputy, and includes partial ownership: if one owns a portion of a relative, only that portion becomes free. This passage demonstrates the detailed casuistry of the Hanafi school in applying the principle of automatic emancipation of close relatives, considering various scenarios and positions.

Key Terms
عتق بالتخلية (itq bi al-takhliyah): Emancipation by letting the slave go without formal words, considered valid by Abu Yusuf in certain contexts, but not by the majority.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if a Muslim buys a Harbi slave in Dar al-Harb and emancipates him without letting him go, according to Abu Hanifa?
A) The slave becomes free immediately.
B) The slave does not become free.
C) The slave becomes free only if he is Muslim.
D) The slave becomes free but without patronage.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has an internal dispute on emancipation in Dar al-Harb; the mufta bihi follows Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, requiring Dar al-Islam or takhliyah for Harbi slaves.

Emancipation with Intention for Allah, Satan, or Idol
Commentary on emancipation validity irrespective of intention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that emancipation is valid regardless of intention, and explain why intention does not affect legal validity.

Core Idea
The act of emancipation removes slavery by its nature; intention affects reward or sin, not legal validity.

In this passage, the commentator on Kanz al-Daqa'iq explores a key Hanafi principle: the legal effect of an act is independent of its moral classification. Emancipation, whether for Allah, Satan, or an idol, is valid because the effective cause is the act itself, not the intention. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping the Hanafi approach to legal validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is emancipation (ītq). The ruling in all three cases (for Allah, Satan, idol) is that the emancipation is valid; the slave becomes free.
The commentator states: 'emancipation is valid by freeing that is worship or disobedience'.
2
Locate the rational proof
The proof is that the effective pillar (rukn) is the act of freeing itself, which removes slavery. The quality of devotion (qurbah) has no impact on that. This is proven by analogy to kitabah for money, which is legislated even without devotion.
The text says: 'Do you not see that emancipation and kitabah for money are legislated even if they are devoid of the quality of devotion?'
3
Classify the types of emancipation
Emancipation is divided into four types: obligatory, recommended, permissible, and sinful. The classification depends on intention, but all are legally valid.
The commentator references al-Muhit: emancipation may be permissible (without intention) or sinful (for Satan).
4
Examine the cross-madhhab dispute
Malik held that freeing a more valuable non-Muslim slave is better than a Muslim slave. The Hanafi commentator rejects this, insisting the hadith 'the best is the most valuable' must be restricted to Muslims.
The critic says this view is 'far from correct' and must be restricted to Muslims because it enables a Muslim to fulfill his purposes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

His saying: 'And by freeing for the sake of Allah, and for the sake of Satan, and for the sake of an idol' means that emancipation is valid by freeing that is worship or disobedience, because the act of freeing is the effective pillar in removing slavery, and the quality of devotional act has no impact on that. Likewise, emancipation without intention is permissible as in al-Tabyin. As for what is said about emancipating a disbeliever mentioned above, it is a possibility countered by the apparent, for the apparent is the firmness of beliefs and their understanding, so he does not revert. Similarly, we observe free-born people among them only increase in attachment to remaining in their control (freedom?). 'Face' in language has meanings: it is said for the face of a human etc., and it is known; the beginning of the day; the path you intend by speech; the nobles of a people; and I turned something on its face means in its proper way. 'Shaytan' is one of the devils among mankind and jinn, meaning their rebellious ones. The nun is original if it is from shatuna (became distant from good) and extra if from shata (perished). 'Sanam' is an image of a human from wood, gold, or silver; if from stone it is wathan; thus in Ghayat al-Bayan.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq's statement that emancipation is valid whether it is for Allah, Satan, or an idol. The commentator explains that the act of emancipation itself is the effective cause (rukn) in removing slavery; the intention of devotion (qurbah) does not affect its validity. Therefore, even if the act is sinful (e.g., for Satan), the legal effect—the slave becomes free—still occurs. However, there is a difference between emancipation for a human and for Satan: the latter is sinful because it involves veneration of Satan. For a Muslim, emancipation for an idol would entail kufr if done with intent to venerate the idol. The author then notes that emancipation can be obligatory, recommended, permissible, or sinful, citing al-Muhit. He also discusses the case of emancipating a dhimmi slave: there is reward for it as it may lead him to study the Quran and remove doubts, but if there is fear of him turning apostate or causing harm, it becomes prohibited. The commentator criticizes Malik's view that freeing a more valuable disbelieving slave is better than a Muslim slave, stating it must be restricted to Muslims. Finally, he provides linguistic definitions of 'wajh Allah', 'shaytan', and 'sanam', grounding the legal discussion in lexical precision. The passage demonstrates the Hanafi principle that the validity of an act is independent of its moral classification; emancipation fulfills its legal purpose regardless of the intention, though the intention affects reward or sin.

Key Terms
وجه الله (wajh Allah): Metaphor for the pleasure of Allah; literally 'face of Allah' is not taken literally.
الشيطان (al-shaytan): Rebellious beings from jinn and mankind.
الصنم (al-sanam): An image of a human from wood, gold, or silver; if from stone it is called wathan.
عتق (itq): Emancipation of a slave; legal act of freeing.
كتابة (kitabah): Contract of manumission where the slave pays for freedom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The emancipation is valid and rewarded because the act is good.
D) The slave does not become free because the intention was evil.

Key Takeaway
Emancipation is legally valid regardless of intention; intention only affects reward or sin, not the legal effect.

Emancipation Under Duress or Intoxication
States that emancipation is valid even if performed under duress or intoxication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on emancipation under duress and intoxication and explain the underlying rationale of the rukn issuing from the qualified person in its locus.

Core Idea
Emancipation is valid even under duress or intoxication because the essential act issues from a qualified person in its proper place.

This lesson examines a ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq as explained in Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: emancipation (ʿitq) performed under duress or intoxication is legally valid in the Hanafi school. The passage clarifies that the reason is the pillar (rukn) of the act—the utterance or action of freeing—emanates from someone qualified (ahl) and is applied to the proper subject (mahall). Duress and intoxication do not nullify the legal effect, though they may affect moral responsibility. We will unpack the definition of duress and the scope of intoxication according to the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Identify the act as emancipation (ʿitq) and state that the ruling is that it is valid (sahih) even when performed under duress or intoxication.
The act's validity is not conditioned on full voluntary consent in this specific context.
2
Understand the rationale: rukn and capacity
Analyze the author's reasoning: the pillar (rukn) issues from a qualified person (ahl) in its locus (mahall). The person is qualified because he is the owner and of legal capacity; duress/intoxication do not affect these elements.
The rukn here is the utterance or definitive action indicating freeing.
3
Define duress (ikrah) in Hanafi law
Duress includes both life-threatening (mulji') and non-life-threatening (ghayr mulji') compulsion. The Hanafi school holds that it invalidates free choice but does not void the legal act.
The text explicitly includes both types.
4
Define intoxication (sukr) in context
Intoxication is restricted to that from a prohibited substance (e.g., wine). The passage cuts off but implies that intoxication from permissible sources may have different rulings.
The full text likely continues with the distinction.
5
Appreciate the objectivity of legal effects
Hanafi fiqh often focuses on outward performance; inner coercion does not invalidate the act, though it may affect sinfulness or other consequences.
This is a key principle: legal effects can be separate from moral responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وبكره وسكر) أي يصح العتق مع الإكراه والسكر لصدور الركن من الأهل في المحل والإكراه حمل الغير على ما لا يرضاه وأطلقه فشمل الملجيء وهو ما يفوت النفس أو العضو وغير الملجىء، وأما السكر فأطلقه أيضاً وهو مقيد بما كان من محرم أو
His saying: 'By duress and intoxication' means that emancipation is valid with duress and intoxication because the pillar issues from the qualified person in its locus. Duress is forcing another to what he does not consent to; he (the author) generalized it, so it includes compelling (mulji') which causes loss of life or limb, and non-compelling. As for intoxication, he also generalized it, and it is restricted to what is from a prohibited (substance) or...
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the ruling in Kanz al-Daqa'iq that emancipation is valid even under duress or intoxication. The commentator clarifies that the reason is that the essential element (rukn) of the act—the statement or action of freeing—emanates from someone qualified to perform it (ahl) and is applied to the proper subject (mahl). Duress is defined as forcing someone to do something against their will, and it includes both life-threatening (mulji') and non-life-threatening compulsion. Similarly, intoxication is left general, but it is restricted to intoxication from prohibited substances, as implied by the context (the text cuts off). The implication is that while duress and intoxication may affect the moral responsibility of the person, they do not invalidate the legal effect of the emancipation. This is consistent with the Hanafi position that the validity of an act depends on its outward performance and the capacity of the agent; inner coercion or lack of awareness does not nullify the act, though it may affect sinfulness or other consequences. The passage is brief but underscores the objectivity of legal effects in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
الإكراه (al-ikrah): Compelling someone to do something against his will, including life-threatening (mulji') and non-life-threatening.
السكر (al-sukr): Intoxication from a prohibited substance.
الملجيء (al-mulji'): Duress that threatens loss of life or limb.
غير الملجىء (ghayr al-mulji'): Duress that does not threaten life or limb, e.g., financial harm or imprisonment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man is forced at gunpoint to say 'you are free' to his slave. What is the ruling?
A) The slave does not become free because there is no consent.
B) The slave becomes free because the act of emancipation is valid despite duress.
C) The emancipation is valid only if the duress is life-threatening.
D) The emancipation is invalid because it is not genuine.

Key Takeaway
Emancipation under duress or intoxication is valid because the pillar (rukn) issues from a qualified person in its proper locus.

8 min
The Four Legal Categories of Emancipation
Categorization of emancipation based on intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four categories of emancipation in Hanafi fiqh and identify which category applies based on intention and consequences.

Core Idea
Emancipation's ruling depends on intention: fard, mandub, mubah, or ma'siya.

In this passage, the author explains that emancipating a slave can fall into four distinct legal categories: obligatory, recommended, permissible, or sinful. The key factor is the intention behind the act and the likely consequences. For example, freeing a slave to venerate Satan is sinful, while freeing without any intention is merely permissible. We will unpack each category and also discuss a special case of prohibited emancipation when harm is feared.

Guided Path
1
State the four categories
Identify the four types of hukm for emancipation mentioned in the text: fard, mandub, mubah, and ma'siya.
2
Analyze the examples
Understand what makes emancipation permissible (without intention or for a human) and what makes it sinful (for Satan).
The text distinguishes between intention for a human and intention for Satan.
3
Examine the prohibited case
According to Fath al-Qadir, emancipation is prohibited (ma'siya) if the master strongly believes the slave will go to dar al-harb, apostatize, or commit crimes. This is contrary to the Zahiris who consider it valid and not sinful.
The prohibition is due to likely harm.
4
Apply the categories
Given a scenario, determine which category applies by analyzing the intention and likely consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب العتق
أن يكفر به إذا قصد تعظيمه وقدمنا أن أنواعه أربعة فرض ومندوب ومباح ومعصية. وفي المحيط أن الإعتاق قد يقع مباحاً لا قربة بأن أعتق من غير نية أو أعتق لوجه فلان، وقد يقع معصية بأن أعتقه لوجه الشيطان .اهـ. ففرق بين الإعتاق لآدمي وبين الإعتاق للشيطان وعلل حرمة الإعتاق للشيطان بأنه قصد تعظيمه، وكذا العتق بلا نية مباح كما في التبيين. وذكر في فتح القدير أن من الإعتاق المحرم إذا غلب على ظنه أنه إن أعتقه يذهب إلى دار الحرب أو يرتد أو يخاف منه السرقة وقطع الطريق وينفذ عتقه . تحريمه خلافاً للظاهرية. هذا وفي عتق العبد الذمي ما لم يخف ما ذكرنا أجر لتمكينه من النظر في الآيات والاشتغال بما يزيل الشبهة عنه، وأما ما عن مالك أنه إذا كان أغلى ثمناً من العبد المسلم يكون عتقه أفضل من عتق المسلم لقوله عليه السلام أفضلها أغلاها (۱) ـ بالمهملة والمعجمة - فبعيد عن الصواب ويجب تقييده بالأعلى من المسلمين لأنه تمكين المسلم من مقاصده وتفريغه. وأما ما يقال في عتق الكافر مما ذكرنا فهو احتمال يقابله ظاهر فإن الظاهر رسوخ الاعتقادات والفها فلا يرجع وكذا نشاهد الأحرار بالأصالة منهم لا يزدادون إلا ارتباط بقاء يدهم عرضت حريته . نعم الوجه الظاهر في استحباب عتقه تحصيل الجزية منه للمسلمين، وأما تفريغه للتأمل فيسلم فهو احتمال والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم .اهـ. وأراد بوجه الله رضاه مجازاً والوجه في اللغة يجيء على معان؛ يقال وجه الإنسان وغيره وهو معروف، ووجه النهار أوله، ووجه الكلام السبيل التي تقصدها به ووجوه القوم ساداتهم، وصرفت الشيء على وجهه أي على سننه . والشيطان واحد شياطين الإنسان والجن بمعنى مردتهم، والنون أصليه إن كان من شطن أي بعد عن الخير وزائدة إن كان من شاط يشيط أي هلك. وأما الصنم فهو صورة الإنسان من خشب أو ذهب أو فضة فإن كان من حجر فهو وثن؛ كذا في غاية البيان.
The Book of Emancipation. That he disbelieves if he intends to venerate it, and we have presented that its types are four: obligatory, recommended, permissible, and sinful. In al-Muhit: Emancipation may occur as permissible, not a religious act, such as freeing without intention or freeing for the sake of so-and-so; and it may occur as sinful, such as freeing for the sake of Satan. So he distinguished between emancipation for a human and emancipation for Satan, and explained the prohibition of emancipation for Satan because it intends veneration. Likewise, emancipation without intention is permissible as in al-Tabyin. It is mentioned in Fath al-Qadir that among prohibited emancipation is when he strongly thinks that if he frees him, he will go to the abode of war, or apostatize, or fears theft and highway robbery from him, and his emancipation takes effect; its prohibition is contrary to the Zahiris. Regarding the emancipation of the dhimmi slave, as long as he does not fear what we mentioned, it is rewarded for enabling him to consider the verses and engage in removing doubts from him. As for what is reported from Malik that if his price is higher than that of a Muslim slave, his emancipation is better than freeing a Muslim, due to his statement (peace be upon him): 'The best of them is the highest in price' — this is far from correctness and must be restricted to the highest among Muslims because it is enabling a Muslim for his purposes and emptying him. As for what is said about freeing the disbeliever from what we mentioned, it is a possibility countered by the apparent, for the apparent is the firmness of beliefs and understanding, so he does not return. Likewise, we observe free-born people originally from them do not increase except in attachment to remaining in their hands when their freedom is offered. Indeed, the apparent aspect in recommending his emancipation is to obtain jizya from him for the Muslims. As for emptying him for reflection so he submits, it is merely a possibility. And Allah Most High knows best. He intended by 'the face of God' His pleasure, metaphorically. Face in language comes in meanings: it is said for the face of a person and other than it, which is known; the face of the day means its beginning; the face of speech means the way intended by it; the faces of a people are their leaders; 'I directed the thing on its face' means on its proper path. Shaytan is one of the shayateen of humans and jinn, meaning their rebels; the noon is original if from shatana (he distanced from good), and extra if from shata yashitu (he perished). As for the idol (sanam), it is the image of a human from wood, gold, or silver; if from stone it is wathan; so in Ghayat al-Bayan.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the types of emancipation (i'taq) as derived from the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq. The author, Imam Zayn al-Din al-Hanafi, begins by stating that the types are four: obligatory (fard), recommended (mandub), permissible (mubah), and sinful (ma'siya). He then elaborates with examples: permissible emancipation occurs when done without any intention or for a worldly reason; sinful emancipation occurs when done for the sake of Satan (i.e., to venerate him). The prohibition of freeing for Satan is because it amounts to veneration. Freeing without intention is permissible. Then from Fath al-Qadir, he mentions a case of prohibited emancipation: if the master believes the freed slave will go to dar al-harb, apostatize, or become a criminal, the emancipation is still valid but sinful, contrary to the Zahiri school. The discussion turns to freeing a dhimmi slave. Generally, it is rewarded because it may lead him to Islam. The author rejects Malik's view that freeing a high-priced dhimmi is better than freeing a Muslim, arguing that the hadith 'the best is the highest in price' must be interpreted among Muslims themselves. The benefit of freeing a dhimmi is to obtain jizya for Muslims, while hoping for his conversion is merely a possibility. The passage concludes with linguistic explanations of 'face of God,' 'shaytan,' and 'sanam/wathan.' The overall ruling is that emancipation has varying legal statuses based on intention and consequences.

Key Terms
الإعتاق (al-i'tāq): The act of freeing a slave; emancipation.
الشيطان (al-shaytān): A rebellious devil among jinn or humans.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on emancipating a slave for the sake of Satan?
A) Mubah (permissible)
B) Ma'siya (sinful)
C) Fard (obligatory)
D) Mandub (recommended)

Key Takeaway
Emancipation in Hanafi fiqh is classified according to intention: fard, mandub, mubah, or ma'siya.

10 min
Freeing a Dhimmi Slave: Reward, Disagreement with Malik, and Linguistic Notes
Ruling on freeing dhimmi slave and critique of Maliki view

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on freeing a dhimmi slave, critique the Maliki position using the hadith 'أفضلها أغلاها', and identify the correct interpretation.

Core Idea
Freeing a dhimmi is rewarded, but not better than freeing a Muslim, contrary to what some infer from the hadith.

The author now turns to the case of freeing a non-Muslim dhimmi slave. While such an act can be rewarded for its potential to lead the slave to Islam, the author strongly rejects the view attributed to Imam Malik that freeing a higher-priced dhimmi is superior to freeing a Muslim. He insists the hadith 'the best is the highest in price' must be interpreted among Muslims only. We will dissect this argument, examine the linguistic explanations, and understand the Hanafi perspective.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling on freeing a dhimmi
Note that emancipating a dhimmi slave is rewarded as long as no harm is feared. The reward is for enabling him to consider Islamic proof and remove doubts.
2
Understand Malik's argument and the hadith
Malik is reported to say that if a dhimmi slave is more expensive than a Muslim slave, freeing the dhimmi is better. He cites the hadith: 'The best of them is the highest in price.'
The hadith is known in Bukhari regarding emancipation.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The author rejects this, stating the hadith must be restricted to Muslims. The benefit of freeing a Muslim is enabling him to fulfill his religious and worldly purposes, which is greater.
4
Recognize the alternative benefit
The apparent benefit of freeing a dhimmi is to collect jizya from him. The hope of his conversion is merely a possibility.
5
Review the linguistic notes
The passage ends with linguistic explanations of 'face of God', 'shaytan', and the difference between sanam (image) and wathan (stone idol).
These clarify terms used in the discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms
الذمي (al-dhimmī): A non-Muslim subject living under the protection of an Islamic state, paying jizya.
الجزية (al-jizya): A tax paid by non-Muslims in exchange for protection and exemption from military service.
الصنم (al-ṣanam): An idol carved from wood, gold, or silver in the shape of a human.
الوثن (al-wathan): A stone idol or unshaped object of worship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is it not better to free a high-priced dhimmi than a Muslim slave?
A) Because dhimmi are not eligible for reward.
B) Because the hadith 'the best is the highest in price' only applies among Muslims.
C) Because freeing a dhimmi is always sinful.
D) Because dhimmi are considered enemies.

Key Takeaway
Freeing a dhimmi is rewarded, but never surpasses freeing a Muslim; the hadith 'the best is the highest in price' must be interpreted exclusively among Muslims.
